# Faithfulness audit of `proofnet_verified_gt/`

For each Lean file we asked Claude to compare the informal statement against the Lean theorem signature and decide whether the Lean encoding is a *strictly faithful* formalization (neither stronger nor weaker).

## Summary

| verdict | count |
|---|---|
| faithful | 326 |
| weaker | 23 |
| stronger | 5 |
| different | 12 |
| unclear | 1 |
| **total** | **367** |

## faithful (326)

### proofnet-1

**Informal statement**

> Let $H$ be the subgroup generated by two elements $a, b$ of a group $G$. Prove that if $a b=b a$, then $H$ is an abelian group.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_2_2_9 {G : Type*} [Group G] {a b : G}
  (h : a * b = b * a) :
  ∀ x y : closure {x | x = a ∨ x = b}, x * y = y * x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim says the subgroup H generated by a and b is abelian, given ab = ba. The Lean signature takes the same hypothesis a*b = b*a and concludes that any two elements x, y of `closure {x | x = a ∨ x = b}` (i.e., the subgroup generated by the set {a, b}) commute, which is exactly the definition of H being abelian. The quantifier structure (∀ x y in H) matches, the hypothesis matches, and the ambient group G is arbitrary. No extra hypotheses (like Fintype, commutativity of G, etc.) are added, and none are dropped. This is a logically equivalent formalization.

### proofnet-2

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if a group contains exactly one element of order 2 , then that element is in the center of the group.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_2_4_19 {G : Type*} [Group G] {x : G}
  (hx : orderOf x = 2) (hx1 : ∀ y, orderOf y = 2 → y = x) :
  x ∈ center G
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypotheses `orderOf x = 2` together with `∀ y, orderOf y = 2 → y = x` exactly express "x is the unique element of order 2 in G," which matches "the group contains exactly one element of order 2." The conclusion `x ∈ center G` is the informal claim that this element lies in the center. The group G is arbitrary, matching the informal universal quantification. No extra hypotheses (like finiteness or commutativity) are imposed, and nothing is dropped. Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-3

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a group of even order contains an element of order $2 .$

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_2_11_3 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G]
  (hG : Even (card G)) : ∃ x : G, orderOf x = 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says a group of even order has an element of order 2. "Even order" implicitly means the group is finite with even cardinality, which is exactly captured by `[Fintype G]` together with `Even (card G)`. The conclusion `∃ x : G, orderOf x = 2` matches "contains an element of order 2" precisely. No extra hypotheses (like commutativity) are added, and no part of the claim is weakened. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-4  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Artin_exercise_3_5_6_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $V$ be a vector space which is spanned by a countably infinite set. Prove that every linearly independent subset of $V$ is finite or countably infinite.

**Lean signature**

```lean


theorem Artin_exercise_3_5_6_corrected {K V : Type*} [Field K] [AddCommGroup V]
  [Module K V] {S : Set V} (hS : Set.Countable S) (hSinf : S.Infinite)
  (hS1 : span K S = ⊤) {ι : Type*} (R : ι → V)
  (hR : LinearIndependent K R) : Countable ι
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypotheses match: `hS : Set.Countable S` together with `hSinf : S.Infinite` exactly captures "countably infinite", and `span K S = ⊤` says S spans V. The conclusion `Countable ι` for a linearly independent family `R : ι → V` is equivalent to saying the linearly independent subset (the image of R, which equals ι in cardinality since linear independence of a family forces injectivity) is at most countable, i.e., finite or countably infinite. The ambient setting (vector space over a field K) matches. So the Lean statement asserts the same claim as the informal one.

### proofnet-5

**Informal statement**

> Let $Z$ be the center of a group $G$. Prove that if $G / Z$ is a cyclic group, then $G$ is abelian and hence $G=Z$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_6_1_14 (G : Type*) [Group G]
  (hG : IsCyclic $ G ⧸ (center G)) :
  center G = ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that if G/Z(G) is cyclic, then G is abelian (equivalently Z(G) = G). The Lean signature takes an arbitrary group G with the hypothesis that G ⧸ center G is cyclic and concludes center G = ⊤, which is precisely the statement that the center equals the whole group, i.e., G is abelian. The quantification over arbitrary G is correctly captured by the type variable, and `IsCyclic` on the quotient group matches "G/Z is cyclic". The conclusion `center G = ⊤` is logically equivalent to "G is abelian and G = Z", so the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-6

**Informal statement**

> Prove that no group of order $p^2 q$, where $p$ and $q$ are prime, is simple.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_6_4_3 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G] {p q : ℕ}
  (hp : Prime p) (hq : Prime q) (hG : card G = p^2 * q) :
  IsSimpleGroup G → False
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says no group of order p^2 q (with p, q prime) is simple. The Lean signature takes a finite group G of order p^2 * q with p and q prime, and concludes that G being simple leads to False — i.e., G is not simple. The hypotheses match (p, q prime; |G| = p^2 q), and the conclusion (not simple) matches. Note that the informal statement doesn't explicitly require p ≠ q, but neither does the Lean version, so they match on that point. The use of Nat.Prime via `Prime p` is standard. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-7

**Informal statement**

> Prove that two elements $a, b$ of a group generate the same subgroup as $b a b^2, b a b^3$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_6_8_1 {G : Type*} [Group G]
  (a b : G) : closure ({a, b} : Set G) = Subgroup.closure {b*a*b^2, b*a*b^3}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that {a, b} and {bab², bab³} generate the same subgroup of G. The Lean signature states exactly this: the subgroup closure of {a, b} equals the subgroup closure of {b*a*b^2, b*a*b^3} in an arbitrary group G. Both sides use `Subgroup.closure` (the unqualified `closure` on the left resolves to `Subgroup.closure` in this context), the universal quantification over arbitrary groups is preserved via `{G : Type*} [Group G]`, and a, b are arbitrary elements. No extra hypotheses (like finiteness or commutativity) are introduced, and no direction of the equality is dropped.

### proofnet-8

**Informal statement**

> Prove that in the ring $\mathbb{Z}[x],(2) \cap(x)=(2 x)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_10_2_4 :
  span ({2} : Set $ Polynomial ℤ) ⊓ (span {X}) =
  span ({2 * X} : Set $ Polynomial ℤ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that in ℤ[x], the intersection of the ideals (2) and (x) equals the ideal (2x). The Lean signature uses `span` over `Polynomial ℤ` to form the ideals generated by {2}, {X}, and {2*X}, and uses `⊓` for ideal intersection. In Mathlib, `Ideal.span` of a singleton corresponds exactly to the principal ideal generated by that element, and `⊓` on ideals is set-theoretic intersection. Thus the Lean statement asserts (2) ∩ (X) = (2X) in ℤ[X], matching the informal claim exactly.

### proofnet-9

**Informal statement**

> Let $I, J$ be ideals in a ring $R$. Prove that the residue of any element of $I \cap J$ in $R / I J$ is nilpotent.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_10_4_6 {R : Type*} [CommRing R]
  (I J : Ideal R) (x : ↑(I ⊓ J)) :
  IsNilpotent ((Ideal.Quotient.mk (I*J)) x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement quantifies over x in the subtype ↑(I ⊓ J), which corresponds exactly to "any element of I ∩ J". It then asserts that the image under Ideal.Quotient.mk (I*J) — the residue in R/(IJ) — is nilpotent, matching the informal conclusion. The use of CommRing is appropriate: this is exercise 10.4.6 from Artin's Algebra, where the ambient ring context is commutative rings, and the statement (I∩J)^2 ⊆ IJ relies on commutativity. Quantifier structure (∀ I J, ∀ x ∈ I∩J) and the conclusion (IsNilpotent of the residue class) match the informal claim. No extra or missing hypotheses appear.

### proofnet-10

**Informal statement**

> Let $R$ be a ring, with $M$ an ideal of $R$. Suppose that every element of $R$ which is not in $M$ is a unit of $R$. Prove that $M$ is a maximal ideal and that moreover it is the only maximal ideal of $R$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_10_7_10 {R : Type*} [Ring R]
  (M : Ideal R) (hM : ∀ (x : R), x ∉ M → IsUnit x)
  (hProper : ∃ x : R, x ∉ M) :
  IsMaximal M ∧ ∀ (N : Ideal R), IsMaximal N → N = M
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures both conclusions of the informal claim: `IsMaximal M` (M is maximal) and `∀ N, IsMaximal N → N = M` (M is the unique maximal ideal). The hypothesis `hM` directly encodes "every element of R not in M is a unit." The extra hypothesis `hProper : ∃ x, x ∉ M` makes explicit what the informal statement implicitly requires: M must be a proper ideal, since otherwise (M = R) `hM` is vacuously true but M cannot be maximal — so the informal statement is only sensible/true under this assumption. Using `[Ring R]` rather than commutative ring is consistent with the informal "Let R be a ring." The encoding of "only maximal ideal" via universally quantifying N and concluding N = M is logically equivalent to uniqueness. Overall the Lean statement matches the intended mathematical content.

### proofnet-11

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $x^3 + 6x + 12$ is irreducible in $\mathbb{Q}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_11_4_1b :
  Irreducible (12 + 6 * X + X ^ 3 : Polynomial ℚ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove x^3 + 6x + 12 is irreducible over ℚ. The Lean signature states `Irreducible (12 + 6*X + X^3 : Polynomial ℚ)`, which is the same polynomial (addition is commutative) in ℚ[X], and `Irreducible` in Mathlib for a polynomial over a field means exactly the standard notion of irreducibility (non-unit, non-zero, and not factoring as a product of two non-units). The coefficients, the ambient ring, and the conclusion all match.

### proofnet-12

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $x^2+1$ is irreducible in $\mathbb{F}_7$

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_11_4_6b {F : Type*} [Field F] [Fintype F] (hF : card F = 7) :
  Irreducible (X ^ 2 + 1 : Polynomial F)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show x^2+1 is irreducible in F_7[x]. The Lean signature quantifies over any finite field F of cardinality 7; since all fields of cardinality 7 are isomorphic to F_7 (the prime field of characteristic 7), the assertion is equivalent to irreducibility of X^2+1 over F_7. The conclusion `Irreducible (X^2+1 : Polynomial F)` is exactly the polynomial-ring irreducibility claim. No hypotheses are missing or extraneous, and the type encoding faithfully captures the intended ambient ring.

### proofnet-13

**Informal statement**

> Let $p$ be a prime integer. Prove that the polynomial $x^n-p$ is irreducible in $\mathbb{Q}[x]$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_11_4_8 (p : ℕ) (hp : Prime p) (n : ℕ) (hn : n > 0) :
  Irreducible (X ^ n - (p : Polynomial ℚ) : Polynomial ℚ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes "p prime, n ≥ 1 ⟹ X^n − p is irreducible in ℚ[X]", which matches the informal claim. Using `p : ℕ` with `Prime p` (Mathlib's `Prime` on ℕ coincides with the usual notion of prime, equivalent to "prime integer" after coercion to ℚ). The added hypothesis `n > 0` is the natural implicit assumption from the informal statement — for n = 0 the polynomial becomes the constant 1 − p which is not irreducible, so the informal statement implicitly requires n ≥ 1. The polynomial `X^n − (p : ℚ)` in `Polynomial ℚ` faithfully represents x^n − p in ℚ[x], and `Irreducible` is the correct Mathlib predicate. No hypotheses are silently added or dropped, and the conclusion matches.

### proofnet-15

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $-(-v) = v$ for every $v \in V$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_1_3 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] {v : V} : -(-v) = v
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement comes from Axler's Linear Algebra Done Right, where V is a vector space over a field F. The Lean signature encodes this exactly: F is a Field, V is an AddCommGroup with Module F V structure (the standard Mathlib formalization of a vector space). The conclusion -(-v) = v is stated for an arbitrary v : V (implicit universal quantification via the implicit binder), matching "for every v ∈ V". No extra hypotheses (like finite-dimensionality) are imposed, and none required by the informal statement are dropped. This is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-16

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the intersection of any collection of subspaces of $V$ is a subspace of $V$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_1_8 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] {ι : Type*} (u : ι → Submodule F V) :
  ∃ U : Submodule F V, (⋂ (i : ι), (u i).carrier) = ↑U
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim says: the intersection of any collection of subspaces of V is itself a subspace. The Lean signature fixes a field F, a vector space V (AddCommGroup + Module F V), and an arbitrary index type ι with a family u : ι → Submodule F V, then asserts the existence of a Submodule U whose carrier equals ⋂ i, (u i).carrier. Saying "there exists a submodule whose underlying set equals this intersection" is exactly the assertion that the intersection set is a subspace. The index type being an arbitrary Type* faithfully captures "any collection," and the empty-ι case (where the intersection is all of V) is correctly handled since V itself is a submodule. No extra hypotheses (e.g. finiteness, nonemptiness) are imposed, and no part of the conclusion is weakened.

### proofnet-17

**Informal statement**

> Show that every linear map from a one-dimensional vector space to itself is multiplication by some scalar. More precisely, prove that if $\operatorname{dim} V=1$ and $T \in \mathcal{L}(V, V)$, then there exists $a \in \mathbf{F}$ such that $T v=a v$ for all $v \in V$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_3_1 {F V : Type*}
  [AddCommGroup V] [Field F] [Module F V] [FiniteDimensional F V]
  (T : V →ₗ[F] V) (hT : finrank F V = 1) :
  ∃ c : F, ∀ v : V, T v = c • v
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes an arbitrary field F and an F-module V, assumes finrank F V = 1, and concludes ∃ c : F, ∀ v, T v = c • v, which exactly matches the informal claim "if dim V = 1 then T is scalar multiplication". The [FiniteDimensional F V] hypothesis is redundant given finrank = 1 (a positive finrank already implies finite-dimensionality in Mathlib's convention, and even if it didn't, finite-dimensionality is implicit in the informal statement "dim V = 1"), so it does not strengthen or weaken the claim. AddCommGroup + Module F + Field F is the standard Mathlib encoding of an F-vector space. The quantifier structure (∃ scalar, ∀ vector) matches exactly.

### proofnet-18  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_4_4_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $p \in \mathcal{P}(\mathbf{C})$ has degree $m$. Prove that $p$ has $m$ distinct roots if and only if $p$ and its derivative $p^{\prime}$ have no roots in common.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Axler_exercise_4_4_corrected (p : Polynomial ℂ) (m : ℕ) (hp : p ≠ 0) (hm : m = p.degree) :
    @card (p.rootSet ℂ) (rootSetFintype p ℂ) = m ↔
    Disjoint (p.rootSet ℂ) ((Polynomial.derivative p).rootSet ℂ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypothesis `hm : m = p.degree` (with `m : ℕ` coerced into `WithBot ℕ`) together with `hp : p ≠ 0` correctly captures "p has degree m". The left side `card (p.rootSet ℂ) = m` says the set of complex roots of p has exactly m elements, i.e. p has m distinct roots (since p has at most m roots, this is equivalent to "m distinct roots"). The right side `Disjoint (p.rootSet ℂ) ((derivative p).rootSet ℂ)` says p and p' share no complex root. The edge case m = 0 still works: p is a nonzero constant with empty rootSet, p' = 0 whose rootSet is also empty, so disjointness holds and card = 0. The encoding matches the informal statement.

### proofnet-19  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_5_4_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $S, T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ are such that $S T=T S$. Prove that $\operatorname{null} (T-\lambda I)$ is invariant under $S$ for every $\lambda \in \mathbf{F}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_5_4_corrected {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
    [Module F V] (S T : V →ₗ[F] V) (hST : S ∘ T = T ∘ S) (c : F) :
    Submodule.map S (ker (T - c • LinearMap.id)) ≤ ker (T - c • LinearMap.id)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement assumes S,T are linear operators on V with ST=TS and concludes that null(T-λI) is invariant under S for every λ in F. The Lean version takes S T : V →ₗ[F] V over an arbitrary field F and module V, hypothesizes S ∘ T = T ∘ S (function composition equality, which for linear maps is equivalent to LinearMap composition equality), and for every c : F concludes Submodule.map S (ker (T - c • id)) ≤ ker (T - c • id), i.e. the kernel of T-cI is mapped into itself by S — exactly the invariance claim. The quantifier over c, the operator T - c • id corresponding to T - λI, and the use of ker as the null space all match. No extra or missing hypotheses are introduced.

### proofnet-20

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is such that every vector in $V$ is an eigenvector of $T$. Prove that $T$ is a scalar multiple of the identity operator.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_5_12 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] {S : End F V}
  (hS : ∀ v : V, ∃ c : F, v ∈ eigenspace S c) :
  ∃ c : F, S = c • LinearMap.id
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypothesis `∀ v, ∃ c, v ∈ eigenspace S c` unfolds to `∀ v, ∃ c, S v = c • v`, which captures "every vector is an eigenvector" (the v = 0 case is trivially satisfied, matching the standard convention that the informal claim is non-vacuous only on nonzero vectors). The conclusion `∃ c, S = c • LinearMap.id` exactly states that S is a scalar multiple of the identity operator. The ambient setup (V a module over a field F, S a linear endomorphism) matches `T ∈ L(V)`. No extra hypotheses (like finite-dimensionality) are imposed, and none are needed for this result. The Lean encoding is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-21

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ has $\operatorname{dim} V$ distinct eigenvalues and that $S \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ has the same eigenvectors as $T$ (not necessarily with the same eigenvalues). Prove that $S T=T S$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_5_20 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] [FiniteDimensional F V] {S T : End F V}
  (h1 : card (T.Eigenvalues) = finrank F V)
  (h2 : ∀ v : V, (∃ c : F, v ∈ eigenspace S c) ↔ (∃ c : F, v ∈ eigenspace T c)) :
  S * T = T * S
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature matches the informal statement. `card (T.Eigenvalues) = finrank F V` correctly expresses "T has dim V distinct eigenvalues" (since `Eigenvalues` is the subtype of scalars that are actual eigenvalues). Hypothesis h2 says v ∈ eigenspace S c for some c iff v ∈ eigenspace T c for some c; the zero vector trivially lies in every eigenspace on both sides, so this condition is equivalent (for nonzero v) to "v is an eigenvector of S iff v is an eigenvector of T", which is precisely "S has the same eigenvectors as T (not necessarily with the same eigenvalues)". The conclusion `S * T = T * S` is the commutation in `End F V`. Background assumptions (finite-dimensional F-module) match L(V) for V finite-dimensional, which is required for "dim V distinct eigenvalues" to make sense.

### proofnet-22  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_6_2_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $u, v \in V$. Prove that $\langle u, v\rangle=0$ if and only if $\|u\| \leq\|u+a v\|$ for all $a \in \mathbf{F}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_6_2_corrected {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] {F : Type*}
    [RCLike F] [InnerProductSpace F V]
    (u v : V) :
    ⟪u, v⟫_F = 0 ↔ ∀ (a : F), ‖u‖ ≤ ‖u + a • v‖
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature places u and v in an inner product space V over a field F that is either ℝ or ℂ (via RCLike), matching Axler's setting where F ∈ {ℝ, ℂ}. The biconditional ⟪u, v⟫_F = 0 ↔ ∀ a : F, ‖u‖ ≤ ‖u + a • v‖ states exactly the informal claim: the inner product vanishes iff u has minimal norm among u + av for all scalars a. The quantifier structure (universal over a ∈ F), the conclusion (norm inequality), and the hypothesis-free framing (just u, v ∈ V) all align with the informal statement. No extraneous hypotheses (like nonzero v, finite-dimensionality) are added or dropped.

### proofnet-23  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_6_7_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $V$ is a complex inner-product space, then $\langle u, v\rangle=\frac{\|u+v\|^{2}-\|u-v\|^{2}+\|u+i v\|^{2} i-\|u-i v\|^{2} i}{4}$ for all $u, v \in V$.

**Lean signature**

```lean



theorem Axler_exercise_6_7_corrected {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [InnerProductSpace ℂ V]
    (u v : V) :
    ⟪u, v⟫_ℂ =
      (‖u + v‖ ^ 2 - ‖u - v‖ ^ 2 +
          (‖u - I • v‖ ^ 2 - ‖u + I • v‖ ^ 2) * I) / 4
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement formalizes Axler's complex polarization identity in a complex inner product space, with universal quantification over u, v and the correct typeclass setup (`NormedAddCommGroup V`, `InnerProductSpace ℂ V`). The apparent sign discrepancy on the imaginary part — Axler writes `+‖u+iv‖²·i − ‖u−iv‖²·i` while Lean has `(‖u−iv‖² − ‖u+iv‖²)·I` — exactly reflects Mathlib's convention that `⟪·,·⟫_ℂ` is conjugate-linear in the first argument, whereas Axler's `⟨·,·⟩` is linear in the first. Conjugating Axler's RHS flips precisely the signs of the i-coefficient terms, producing the Lean expression. A direct expansion using ‖u±iv‖² = ‖u‖² + ‖v‖² ∓ 2 Im⟪u,v⟫ confirms the Lean identity holds. So the Lean formula expresses the same theorem under Mathlib's convention; the "corrected" suffix reflects this convention adjustment.

### proofnet-25

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is normal, then $\operatorname{range} T=\operatorname{range} T^{*}.$

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_7_6 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [RCLike F] [InnerProductSpace F V]
  [FiniteDimensional F V] (T : End F V)
  (hT : T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T) :
  range T = range (adjoint T)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

Axler's exercise 7.6 is set in the standard Axler context where V is a finite-dimensional inner product space over ℝ or ℂ, and T ∈ L(V) is normal, meaning T T* = T* T. The Lean signature uses [RCLike F] [InnerProductSpace F V] [FiniteDimensional F V], matching Axler's ambient setting, and encodes normality as T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T (using End F V multiplication, which is composition). The conclusion range T = range (adjoint T) matches the informal claim. No extra hypotheses are introduced beyond what Axler implicitly assumes, and none are dropped. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-26

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $V$ is a complex inner-product space and $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is a normal operator such that $T^{9}=T^{8}$. Prove that $T$ is self-adjoint and $T^{2}=T$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Axler_exercise_7_10 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [InnerProductSpace ℂ V]
  [FiniteDimensional ℂ V] (T : End ℂ V)
  (hT : T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T) (hT1 : T^9 = T^8) :
  IsSelfAdjoint T ∧ T^2 = T
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

This is exercise 7.10 from Axler's "Linear Algebra Done Right," where the standing assumption throughout the inner-product-space chapters is that V is finite-dimensional, so the `FiniteDimensional ℂ V` hypothesis matches the textbook context. The hypothesis `hT : T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T` is exactly the definition of a normal operator. The conclusion `IsSelfAdjoint T ∧ T^2 = T` matches "T is self-adjoint and T² = T". The use of `End ℂ V` (i.e., `V →ₗ[ℂ] V`) corresponds to T ∈ L(V). Powers via the monoid structure on `End` agree with iterated composition, so `T^9 = T^8` matches the informal hypothesis. No quantifier or type drift; the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim under the standard Axler reading.

### proofnet-27

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is self-adjoint, $\lambda \in \mathbf{F}$, and $\epsilon>0$. Prove that if there exists $v \in V$ such that $\|v\|=1$ and $\|T v-\lambda v\|<\epsilon,$ then $T$ has an eigenvalue $\lambda^{\prime}$ such that $\left|\lambda-\lambda^{\prime}\right|<\epsilon$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_7_14 {𝕜 V : Type*} [RCLike 𝕜] [NormedAddCommGroup V]
  [InnerProductSpace 𝕜 V] [FiniteDimensional 𝕜 V]
  {T : Module.End 𝕜 V} (hT : IsSelfAdjoint T)
  {l : 𝕜} {ε : ℝ} (he : ε > 0) : (∃ v : V, ‖v‖= 1 ∧ ‖T v - l • v‖ < ε) →
  (∃ l' : T.Eigenvalues, ‖l - l'‖ < ε)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal claim accurately: T is a self-adjoint endomorphism of a finite-dimensional inner product space over RCLike 𝕜 (which corresponds to F = ℝ or ℂ in Axler), λ ∈ 𝕜, ε > 0. The hypothesis ∃ v, ‖v‖ = 1 ∧ ‖T v - l • v‖ < ε matches the informal hypothesis exactly. The conclusion ∃ l' : T.Eigenvalues, ‖l - l'‖ < ε uses Mathlib's subtype of eigenvalues of T (those scalars μ with HasEigenvalue), and ‖l - l'‖ in RCLike is the absolute value |λ - λ'|, so this matches "T has an eigenvalue λ' with |λ - λ'| < ε". Finite-dimensionality is implicit in Axler's setup (T ∈ L(V) presupposes finite-dim V in his book), so adding [FiniteDimensional 𝕜 V] is faithful rather than a strengthening or weakening.

### proofnet-28

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the addition of residue classes $\mathbb{Z}/n\mathbb{Z}$ is associative.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_3 (n : ℕ) : 
  ∀ (x y z : ZMod n), (x + y) + z = x + (y + z)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove associativity of addition on Z/nZ. The Lean signature states ∀ x y z : ZMod n, (x+y)+z = x+(y+z), which is exactly associativity of addition in ZMod n. ZMod n is Mathlib's encoding of Z/nZ (with ZMod 0 = ℤ, but the informal statement also implicitly covers any n, and integer addition is associative too, so this is fine). No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped. This matches the informal claim exactly.

### proofnet-29  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_5_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that for all $n>1$ that $\mathbb{Z}/n\mathbb{Z}$ is not a group under multiplication of residue classes.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_5_corrected (n : ℕ) (hn : 1 < n) :
  ¬ (∀ x : ZMod n, ∃ y : ZMod n, x * y = (1 : ZMod n) ∧ y * x = 1)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that (ℤ/nℤ, ·) fails to be a group when n>1. Since multiplication on ZMod n is always associative, closed, and has identity 1, the only group axiom that can fail is the existence of inverses. The Lean statement asserts ¬(∀ x, ∃ y, x·y = 1 ∧ y·x = 1), i.e. not every element has a two-sided multiplicative inverse, which is exactly the failure of the only non-automatic group axiom. Hence "not a group under multiplication" and the Lean negation-of-inverses statement are logically equivalent for ZMod n. The hypothesis 1 < n matches the informal n > 1. So the formalization is strictly faithful.

### proofnet-30

**Informal statement**

> Let $x$ be an element of $G$. Prove that $x^2=1$ if and only if $|x|$ is either $1$ or $2$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_16 {G : Type*} [Group G]
  (x : G) : x ^ 2 = 1 ↔ (orderOf x = 1 ∨ orderOf x = 2)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says x² = 1 iff the order |x| is 1 or 2, in an arbitrary group G with x ∈ G. The Lean signature takes G to be an arbitrary group, x : G, and states x^2 = 1 ↔ (orderOf x = 1 ∨ orderOf x = 2). Mathlib's `orderOf` matches the standard notion of order |x| (with orderOf = 1 iff x = 1, and otherwise the least positive n with x^n = 1, or 0 if no such n exists). The biconditional matches the informal claim exactly, with no extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-31

**Informal statement**

> Let $x$ and $y$ be elements of $G$. Prove that $xy=yx$ if and only if $y^{-1}xy=x$ if and only if $x^{-1}y^{-1}xy=1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_18 {G : Type*} [Group G]
  (x y : G) : (x * y = y * x ↔ y⁻¹ * x * y = x) ∧ (y⁻¹ * x * y = x ↔ x⁻¹ * y⁻¹ * x * y = 1)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is a three-way equivalence: xy=yx ↔ y⁻¹xy=x ↔ x⁻¹y⁻¹xy=1. The Lean statement asserts the conjunction of two iffs: (xy=yx ↔ y⁻¹xy=x) and (y⁻¹xy=x ↔ x⁻¹y⁻¹xy=1). By transitivity these two iffs together are logically equivalent to the three-way equivalence. The group is an arbitrary group G, x and y are arbitrary elements, matching the informal statement. No extra hypotheses are added or removed.

### proofnet-32

**Informal statement**

> If $x$ and $g$ are elements of the group $G$, prove that $|x|=\left|g^{-1} x g\right|$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_22a {G : Type*} [Group G] (x g : G) :
  orderOf x = orderOf (g⁻¹ * x * g)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove that for any elements x, g in a group G, the order of x equals the order of g⁻¹xg. The Lean signature universally quantifies over a Group G (via `{G : Type*} [Group G]`) and elements x, g, and asserts `orderOf x = orderOf (g⁻¹ * x * g)`. The `orderOf` function in Mathlib gives the order of an element (returning 0 for infinite order), and the equality holds in both finite and infinite cases, matching |x| = |g⁻¹xg|. The types and quantifier structure match exactly.

The Lean signature takes an arbitrary group G, hypothesizes ∀ x : G, x^2 = 1, and concludes ∀ a b : G, a*b = b*a, which is exactly the definition of being abelian. The hypothesis and conclusion match precisely with no extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-36

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be any group. Prove that the map from $G$ to itself defined by $g \mapsto g^{-1}$ is a homomorphism if and only if $G$ is abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_6_17 {G : Type*} [Group G] (f : G → G)
  (hf : f = λ g => g⁻¹) :
  (∀ x y : G, f x * f y = f (x*y)) ↔ ∀ x y : G, x*y = y*x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says g ↦ g⁻¹ is a homomorphism iff G is abelian. A homomorphism means f(xy) = f(x)f(y), equivalently (xy)⁻¹ = x⁻¹y⁻¹ for all x,y, which holds iff G is abelian. The Lean signature fixes f via the hypothesis hf : f = λ g => g⁻¹, then states (∀ x y, f x * f y = f(x*y)) ↔ (∀ x y, x*y = y*x). The equation f x * f y = f(x*y) is just the symmetric form of the homomorphism condition. The universal quantification over groups G is captured by the {G : Type*} [Group G] binder. No extra hypotheses (like Fintype, commutativity, etc.) are added or removed. This is a faithful formalization.

The informal statement says: for a group G with |G| = n > 2, there is no subgroup H of order n-1. The Lean signature takes an arbitrary finite group G with card G > 2 and an arbitrary subgroup H, and concludes card H ≠ card G - 1, which is exactly the contrapositive-style formalization of "G cannot have a subgroup of order n-1". The Fintype assumption is implicit in the informal statement (n = |G| presupposes finiteness). Quantifier structure (∀ G, ∀ H) matches. No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-38  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_4_corrected`

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_4_corrected {G : Type*} [Group G] (H : Subgroup G) :
    Subgroup.closure ((H : Set G) \ {1}) = H
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: for any subgroup H of G, H equals the subgroup generated by H \ {1}. The Lean signature takes an arbitrary group G and an arbitrary subgroup H, then asserts Subgroup.closure ((H : Set G) \ {1}) = H. "Subgroup.closure S" in Mathlib is precisely the subgroup generated by S, and (H : Set G) \ {1} is the underlying set of H minus the identity, matching "H − {1}". The quantification over G and H is captured by the universally quantified parameters, and no extra hypotheses (finiteness, abelian, etc.) are added. Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-39

**Informal statement**

> Show that the subgroup of all rotations in a dihedral group is a maximal subgroup.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_16b {n : ℕ} {hn : n ≠ 0}
  {R : Subgroup (DihedralGroup n)}
  (hR : R = Subgroup.closure {DihedralGroup.r 1}) :
  R ≠ ⊤ ∧
  ∀ K : Subgroup (DihedralGroup n), R ≤ K → K = R ∨ K = ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that the rotation subgroup of D_n is maximal. In Mathlib, `DihedralGroup.r 1` generates all rotations (since `(r 1)^k = r k`), so `Subgroup.closure {DihedralGroup.r 1}` is precisely the rotation subgroup. The conclusion `R ≠ ⊤ ∧ ∀ K, R ≤ K → K = R ∨ K = ⊤` is exactly the definition of `R` being a maximal subgroup (proper, with no subgroup strictly between it and the whole group). The added hypothesis `n ≠ 0` matches the standard reading of D_n as a finite dihedral group. The implicit-binder style for `hn` is a minor stylistic oddity but does not change the mathematical content. Hence the Lean signature is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-40

**Informal statement**

> Let $A$ be an abelian group and let $B$ be a subgroup of $A$. Prove that $A / B$ is abelian.

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_1_3a {A : Type*} [CommGroup A] (B : Subgroup A) :

```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes A as a CommGroup (abelian group) and B as an arbitrary subgroup, matching the informal hypotheses. In a commutative group every subgroup is normal, so the quotient A ⧸ B is well-defined as a group, and the conclusion ∀ a b : A ⧸ B, a*b = b*a is exactly the assertion that A/B is abelian. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped, and the conclusion is logically equivalent to "A/B is abelian."

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_1_22b {G : Type*} [Group G] (I : Type*) [Nonempty I]

```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The conclusion `Normal (⨅ (i : I), H i)` asserts that the infimum (which in Subgroup G is the intersection of underlying sets) is normal. ### proofnet-42

**Informal statement**

> Let $H \leq K \leq G$. Prove that $|G: H|=|G: K| \cdot|K: H|$ (do not assume $G$ is finite).

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_2_11 {G : Type*} [Group G] {H K : Subgroup G}
  (hHK : H ≤ K) :
  H.index = K.index * H.relindex K
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

In Mathlib, `Subgroup.index H` denotes |G : H|, `K.index` denotes |G : K|, and `H.relindex K` denotes |K : H ∩ K|, which equals |K : H| under the hypothesis `H ≤ K`. The Lean statement therefore asserts |G:H| = |G:K| · |K:H| for arbitrary (not necessarily finite) groups, with no extra finiteness assumption (the index is defined as 0 when infinite, and the identity holds in the natural-number arithmetic, matching the cardinal-arithmetic claim). The hypothesis `H ≤ K` matches the informal "H ≤ K ≤ G". This is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

So the Lean statement asserts: every proper additive subgroup of ℚ has infinite index, which is exactly the informal claim that ℚ has no proper subgroups of finite index. ### proofnet-44

**Lean signature**

```lean

    IsCyclic G ∧ ∃ G_fin : Fintype G, Nat.Prime (@card G G_fin)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

A cyclic group of prime cardinality is exactly Z_p, so the conjunction captures "G ≅ Z_p for some prime p" (the existential ∃ G_fin : Fintype G also encodes finiteness, matching the conclusion that G must be finite). No extra hypotheses (like assuming G finite up front) are imposed, and no part of the conclusion is weakened. Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-47  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_2_14_corrected`

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_2_14_corrected {G : Type*} [Fintype G] [Group G]
  (hG : ¬ (card G).Prime) (hG_ne_one : card G ≠ 1)
  (hG1 : ∀ k : ℕ, k ∣ card G →
    ∃ (H : Subgroup G) (fH : Fintype H), @card H fH = k) :
  ¬ IsSimpleGroup G
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The hypothesis that G has a subgroup of order k for each positive divisor k of n is faithfully expressed by `∀ k, k ∣ card G → ∃ H : Subgroup G, card H = k` (note k=0 is excluded since 0 ∤ card G for card G ≠ 0). ### proofnet-48  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_3_26_corrected`

```lean


theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_3_26_corrected {α : Type*} [Fintype α] (ha : Fintype.card α > 1)
  (G : Subgroup (Equiv.Perm α))
  (h_tran : ∀ a b : α, ∃ σ : G, (σ : Equiv.Perm α) a = b) :

```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The conclusion ∃ σ ∈ G, ∀ a, σ a ≠ a is exactly the existence of a fixed-point-free element. The quantifier structure, hypotheses, and ambient objects match the informal statement, so this is a strictly faithful formalization.

### proofnet-50

**Informal statement**

> If $H$ is the unique subgroup of a given order in a group $G$ prove $H$ is characteristic in $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_4_7 {G : Type*} [Group G] {H : Subgroup G} [Fintype H]
  (hH : ∀ (K : Subgroup G) (fK : Fintype K), card H = @card K fK → H = K) :
  H.Characteristic
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: if H is the unique subgroup of G of its (given) order, then H is characteristic. The Lean signature assumes [Fintype H] (so H has a finite order, matching the implicit reading of "order"), and the hypothesis hH states that any subgroup K with the same cardinality as H equals H — exactly the uniqueness condition. The conclusion H.Characteristic matches "H is characteristic in G". Restricting K to those with a Fintype instance is not a real restriction, because any K with the same cardinality as the finite H must itself be finite (and Fintype is a subsingleton). Hence the encoding is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-51

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $P \in \operatorname{Syl}_{p}(G)$ and $H$ is a subgroup of $G$ containing $P$ then $P \in \operatorname{Syl}_{p}(H)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_5_1a {p : ℕ} {G : Type*} [Group G]
  {P : Sylow p G} (H : Subgroup G) (hH : P ≤ H) :
  IsPGroup p (P.subgroupOf H) ∧
  ∀ (Q : Subgroup H), IsPGroup p Q → (P.subgroupOf H) ≤ Q → Q = (P.subgroupOf H)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is "P is a Sylow p-subgroup of H." In Mathlib, `Sylow p G` is defined as a maximal p-subgroup. The Lean conclusion unfolds exactly this definition for `P.subgroupOf H`: it asserts (1) `P.subgroupOf H` is a p-group, and (2) any p-subgroup Q of H containing it equals it (maximality). The hypothesis `P ≤ H` correctly captures "H contains P" via the coercion of a Sylow subgroup to a subgroup. The quantifiers and types (arbitrary group G, p : ℕ) match the informal statement. Hence the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-52

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a group of order 312 has a normal Sylow $p$-subgroup for some prime $p$ dividing its order.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_5_14 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G]
  (hG : card G = 312) :
  ∃ (p : ℕ) (P : Sylow p G), p.Prime ∧ (p ∣ card G) ∧ P.Normal
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature asserts exactly what the informal statement claims: there exists a prime p dividing |G| = 312 and a Sylow p-subgroup P of G that is normal. The hypotheses (G a finite group of order 312) match, and the conclusion (existence of a normal Sylow p-subgroup for some prime p dividing the order) matches the informal claim. The conditions p.Prime and p ∣ card G are appropriate (and ensure we are talking about a genuine Sylow subgroup for a relevant prime). This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-53  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_5_16`

**Informal statement**

> Let $|G|=p q r$, where $p, q$ and $r$ are primes with $p < q < r$. Prove that $G$ has a normal Sylow subgroup for either $p, q$ or $r$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_5_16 {p q r : ℕ} {G : Type*} [Group G]
  [Fintype G]  (hpqr : p < q ∧ q < r)
  (hpqr1 : p.Prime ∧ q.Prime ∧ r.Prime)(hG : card G = p*q*r) :
  (∃ (P : Sylow p G), P.Normal) ∨ (∃ (P : Sylow q G), P.Normal) ∨ (∃ (P : Sylow r G), P.Normal)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all the hypotheses: p, q, r are primes (hpqr1), p < q < r (hpqr), G is a finite group (Group G + Fintype G), and |G| = pqr (hG). The conclusion is a disjunction asserting the existence of a normal Sylow p-subgroup, normal Sylow q-subgroup, or normal Sylow r-subgroup, which exactly matches "G has a normal Sylow subgroup for either p, q, or r." The Fintype assumption is needed to make sense of `card G = p*q*r` and is implicit in the informal claim that |G| = pqr is a finite number. The formalization is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-58

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a subgroup $H$ of $G$ is normal if and only if $[G, H] \leq H$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_5_4_2 {G : Type*} [Group G] (H : Subgroup G) :
  H.Normal ↔ ⁅(⊤ : Subgroup G), H⁆ ≤ H
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says H ⊴ G iff [G, H] ≤ H. In Lean, `H.Normal` is the standard normality predicate, and `⁅(⊤ : Subgroup G), H⁆` is the commutator subgroup `[⊤, H]` where `⊤ : Subgroup G` is the whole group G viewed as a subgroup. Thus `⁅⊤, H⁆` corresponds exactly to `[G, H]`, and the inequality `≤ H` is containment of subgroups. The biconditional and quantifier structure match the informal claim, with no extra or missing hypotheses (G is an arbitrary group, H an arbitrary subgroup). Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

  {x : R} (hx : x^2 = 1) : x = 1 ∨ x = -1

The informal statement assumes R is an integral domain (commutative ring with no zero divisors, with 1 ≠ 0) and concludes x = 1 or x = -1 given x² = 1. The Lean signature uses [CommRing R] [IsDomain R], which is exactly Mathlib's encoding of an integral domain (IsDomain enforces nontriviality and no zero divisors, CommRing provides commutativity). The hypothesis hx : x^2 = 1 and the conclusion x = 1 ∨ x = -1 match the informal claim precisely. No extra or missing assumptions.

This matches the informal statement: a Boolean ring (ring where every element is idempotent) is commutative. No extra hypotheses (like commutativity, finiteness, or characteristic) are silently assumed, and nothing is dropped. The quantifier structure and conclusion correspond precisely.

### proofnet-61

**Informal statement**

> Let $G=\left\{g_{1}, \ldots, g_{n}\right\}$ be a finite group. Prove that the element $N=g_{1}+g_{2}+\ldots+g_{n}$ is in the center of the group ring $R G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_7_2_12 {R G : Type*} [Ring R] [Group G] [Fintype G] :
  ∑ g : G, MonoidAlgebra.of R G g ∈ center (MonoidAlgebra R G)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that for a finite group G, the sum N = g_1 + ... + g_n of all group elements lies in the center of the group ring RG. The Lean signature takes a ring R, a group G with [Fintype G] (capturing finiteness), forms the sum ∑ g : G, MonoidAlgebra.of R G g (which is exactly the embedding of each group element into the group algebra summed over all elements), and asserts membership in center (MonoidAlgebra R G). ### proofnet-62

**Informal statement**

> An ideal $N$ is called nilpotent if $N^{n}$ is the zero ideal for some $n \geq 1$. Prove that the ideal $p \mathbb{Z} / p^{m} \mathbb{Z}$ is a nilpotent ideal in the ring $\mathbb{Z} / p^{m} \mathbb{Z}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_7_3_37 {p m : ℕ} (_hp : p.Prime) :
  IsNilpotent (span ({↑p} : Set $ ZMod $ p^m) : Ideal $ ZMod $ p^m)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean theorem states `IsNilpotent` of the ideal `span {p}` in `ZMod (p^m)`, which is exactly the image of `pℤ` in `ℤ/p^mℤ`, i.e. the ideal `pℤ/p^mℤ`. `IsNilpotent` on an ideal unfolds to "some power is the zero ideal," matching the informal definition. The hypothesis `p.Prime` matches the informal statement's standing assumption that `p` is prime (implicit from the notation `p^m`). The quantification over `p` and `m` as natural numbers also matches. No extra or missing hypotheses, and the conclusion is the same claim.

### proofnet-63

**Informal statement**

> Let $N$ be a positive integer. Let $M$ be an integer relatively prime to $N$ and let $d$ be an integer relatively prime to $\varphi(N)$, where $\varphi$ denotes Euler's $\varphi$-function. Prove that if $M_{1} \equiv M^{d} \pmod N$ then $M \equiv M_{1}^{d^{\prime}} \pmod N$ where $d^{\prime}$ is the inverse of $d \bmod \varphi(N)$: $d d^{\prime} \equiv 1 \pmod {\varphi(N)}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_1_12 {N : ℕ} (hN : N > 0) {M M': ℤ} {d : ℕ}
  (hMN : M.gcd N = 1) (hMd : d.gcd N.totient = 1)
  (hM' : M' ≡ M^d [ZMOD N]) :
  ∃ d' : ℕ, d' * d ≡ 1 [ZMOD N.totient] ∧
  M ≡ M'^d' [ZMOD N]
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal claim: hN : N > 0, hMN : gcd(M, N) = 1, hMd : gcd(d, φ(N)) = 1, and the hypothesis M' ≡ M^d (mod N), with conclusion that there exists d' satisfying d'·d ≡ 1 (mod φ(N)) and M ≡ M'^{d'} (mod N). The informal statement says "d' is the inverse of d mod φ(N)"; since gcd(d, φ(N)) = 1 such an inverse exists and is unique mod φ(N), and because gcd(M, N) = 1 (hence gcd(M', N) = 1), Euler's theorem makes M'^{d'} mod N depend only on d' mod φ(N). So "∃ d' which is an inverse and satisfies the congruence" is equivalent to the universal/canonical statement. Using d : ℕ rather than ℤ is harmless because M^d mod N depends only on d mod φ(N) when M is a unit mod N, and any integer d coprime to φ(N) has a natural-number representative coprime to φ(N). No hypotheses are silently added or dropped.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_4 {n : ℤ} {r s : ℚ}
  (h : r^2 + s^2 = n) :
  ∃ a b : ℤ, a^2 + b^2 = n
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

This is exactly the same claim — taking the universal over r, s outside is equivalent to assuming the existence of such r, s. The types (ℤ, ℚ) and the conclusion match, with no extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-65  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_6a_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the quotient ring $\mathbb{Z}[i] /(1+i)$ is a field of order 2.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_6a_corrected :
    IsField (GaussianInt ⧸ span ({⟨1, 1⟩} : Set GaussianInt)) ∧
    ∃ finR : Fintype (GaussianInt ⧸ span ({⟨1, 1⟩} : Set GaussianInt)),
      @Fintype.card _ finR = 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement quotients `GaussianInt` (which is ℤ[i]) by `span {⟨1,1⟩}`, where ⟨1,1⟩ represents 1+i, so the ideal is exactly (1+i). The conjunction `IsField ... ∧ ∃ finR : Fintype ..., Fintype.card = 2` captures both parts of the informal claim: the quotient is a field, and it has order 2. The existence of a Fintype instance together with cardinality 2 is the standard way to express "finite of order 2" in Lean. No extra hypotheses are imposed and no part of the conclusion is weakened or strengthened.

### proofnet-69

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\frac{(x+2)^p-2^p}{x}$, where $p$ is an odd prime, is irreducible in $\mathbb{Z}[x]$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_9_4_2d {p : ℕ} (hp : p.Prime ∧ p > 2)
  {f : Polynomial ℤ} (hf : f = (X + 2)^p):
  Irreducible (∑ n ∈ (f.support \ {0}), (f.coeff n : Polynomial ℤ) * X ^ (n-1) :
  Polynomial ℤ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypothesis `p.Prime ∧ p > 2` correctly captures "p is an odd prime" (since the only even prime is 2). The polynomial expression encodes ((X+2)^p − 2^p)/X correctly: f = (X+2)^p has support {0,1,…,p} (all binomial coefficients C(p,n)·2^(p−n) are nonzero), so summing over support \ {0} gives Σ_{n=1}^{p} C(p,n)·2^(p−n) · X^(n−1), which equals ((X+2)^p − 2^p)/X — the n=0 term (constant 2^p) is exactly what's subtracted, and dividing by X corresponds to the X^(n−1) shift. The conclusion `Irreducible` over `Polynomial ℤ` matches "irreducible in ℤ[x]". No extra or missing hypotheses, and the type is correct.

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_2_5 {G : Type*} [Group G]

```

### proofnet-74  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_2_3_17_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> If $G$ is a group and $a, x \in G$, prove that $C\left(x^{-1} a x\right)=x^{-1} C(a) x$

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Herstein_exercise_2_3_17_corrected {G : Type*} [Group G] (a x : G) :
  centralizer {x⁻¹ * a * x} = (fun g : G => x⁻¹ * g * x) '' (centralizer {a})
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that the centralizer of x⁻¹ a x equals the conjugate x⁻¹ C(a) x. In Lean, `centralizer {x⁻¹ * a * x}` is the set of elements commuting with x⁻¹ a x (centralizer of a singleton equals centralizer of the element), and `(fun g => x⁻¹ * g * x) '' (centralizer {a})` is exactly the set {x⁻¹ c x : c ∈ C(a)}, i.e., x⁻¹ C(a) x. Both sides are sets in the arbitrary group G, and the statement is universally quantified over G, a, x via the type and arguments. No extra hypotheses (like finiteness or commutativity) are imposed, and the conclusion is a set equality matching the informal claim. Hence the encoding is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-75

**Informal statement**

> If $a > 1$ is an integer, show that $n \mid \varphi(a^n - 1)$, where $\phi$ is the Euler $\varphi$-function.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_4_36 {a n : ℕ} (h : a > 1) :
  n ∣ (a ^ n - 1).totient
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is: for integer a > 1 and positive integer n, n divides φ(a^n − 1). The Lean version uses a, n : ℕ with hypothesis a > 1, and concludes n ∣ (a^n − 1).totient. Using ℕ for a is fine since a > 1 forces a positive. The case n = 0 is not excluded in Lean, but it is vacuously true (0 ∣ φ(0) = 0), so including it does not weaken or strengthen the claim. Mathlib's Nat.totient on a^n − 1 matches Euler's φ. The natural-subtraction a^n − 1 equals the intended value since a ≥ 2 implies a^n ≥ 1. Hence the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal one.

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_30 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G]

### proofnet-79

**Informal statement**

> If $G$ is an abelian group and if $G$ has an element of order $m$ and one of order $n$, where $m$ and $n$ are relatively prime, prove that $G$ has an element of order $mn$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_6_15 {G : Type*} [CommGroup G] {m n : ℕ}
  (hm : ∃ (g : G), orderOf g = m)
  (hn : ∃ (g : G), orderOf g = n)
  (hmn : m.Coprime n) :
  ∃ (g : G), orderOf g = m * n
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature uses `CommGroup G` for "abelian group", encodes "has an element of order m" as `∃ g : G, orderOf g = m` (and similarly for n), encodes coprimality via `Nat.Coprime`, and concludes with `∃ g : G, orderOf g = m * n`. This matches the informal statement directly. The quantifier structure (universal over G via the type variable, existentials over the elements) and the conclusion are preserved. No extra hypotheses (like Fintype) are added, and no required hypotheses are dropped. Edge cases like m=0 or n=0 are consistent under standard Mathlib conventions for `orderOf` and `Nat.Coprime`.

### proofnet-81

**Informal statement**

> If $G_1$ and $G_2$ are cyclic groups of orders $m$ and $n$, respectively, prove that $G_1 \times G_2$ is cyclic if and only if $m$ and $n$ are relatively prime.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_9_2 {G H : Type*} [Fintype G] [Fintype H] [Group G]
  [Group H] (hG : IsCyclic G) (hH : IsCyclic H) :
  IsCyclic (G × H) ↔ (card G).Coprime (card H)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes two finite cyclic groups G and H (via [Fintype G], [Fintype H], [Group G], [Group H], hG : IsCyclic G, hH : IsCyclic H), which corresponds to "G_1 and G_2 cyclic of orders m and n" since finiteness is implicit in having an order m, n. The conclusion `IsCyclic (G × H) ↔ (card G).Coprime (card H)` matches "G_1 × G_2 is cyclic iff m and n are relatively prime", with card G = m and card H = n. The quantifier structure (universal over such groups) is preserved by the type-level binders. No extraneous hypotheses or missing hypotheses; this is the standard faithful encoding.

### proofnet-82

**Informal statement**

> If $P$ is a $p$-Sylow subgroup of $G$ and $P \triangleleft G$, prove that $P$ is the only $p$-Sylow subgroup of $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_11_6 {G : Type*} [Group G] {p : ℕ} (hp : Nat.Prime p)
  {P : Sylow p G} (hP : P.Normal) :
  ∀ (Q : Sylow p G), P = Q
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts that for every Sylow p-subgroup Q of G, P equals Q, given that P is normal. This is exactly the informal claim that a normal p-Sylow subgroup is the unique p-Sylow subgroup. The `Sylow p G` type encodes p-Sylow subgroups, `P.Normal` matches "P ◁ G", and the prime hypothesis `hp` is standard (and harmless even if redundant since `Sylow p G` is well-defined regardless). The universal quantifier over Q correctly captures uniqueness.

### proofnet-83

**Informal statement**

> Show that any subgroup of order $p^{n-1}$ in a group $G$ of order $p^n$ is normal in $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_11_22 {p : ℕ} {n : ℕ} {G : Type*} [Fintype G]
  [Group G] (hp : Nat.Prime p) (hG : card G = p ^ n) {K : Subgroup G}
  [Fintype K] (hK : card K = p ^ (n-1)) :
  K.Normal
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is the standard result that an index-p subgroup of a p-group is normal. The Lean signature captures this: G is a finite group of order p^n with p prime, K is a subgroup of order p^(n-1), and the conclusion is K.Normal. The edge cases introduced by Nat subtraction (n=0 gives n-1=0, so |G|=1 and |K|=1, trivially normal; n=1 gives |K|=1, also trivially normal) do not falsify the claim, so no spurious strengthening or weakening occurs. Fintype instances on G and K are natural finiteness witnesses consistent with "group of order p^n". The hypothesis structure and conclusion match the informal statement exactly.

### proofnet-86  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_4_2_9`

**Informal statement**

> Let $p$ be an odd prime and let $1 + \frac{1}{2} + ... + \frac{1}{p - 1} = \frac{a}{b}$, where $a, b$ are integers. Show that $p \mid a$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Herstein_exercise_4_2_9 {p : ℕ} (hp : Nat.Prime p) (hp1 : Odd p) :
  ∀ (a b : ℤ), (a / b : ℚ) = ∑ i ∈ Finset.range (p-1), (1 / (i + 1) : ℚ) → ↑p ∣ a
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement quantifies over all integer pairs (a, b) with a/b = ∑_{i=0}^{p-2} 1/(i+1) in ℚ, and concludes p ∣ a. The sum ∑ 1/(i+1) for i ∈ range(p-1) covers 1/1 + 1/2 + ... + 1/(p-1), matching the informal sum exactly. For any (a,b) with a/b equal to this rational sum, a must be an integer multiple of the reduced-form numerator (so the universal quantifier is equivalent to the claim about the reduced numerator), and the b=0 case is vacuous since the harmonic-like sum is nonzero. The hypotheses (p prime, p odd) match the informal "odd prime". Hence the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Herstein_exercise_4_3_25_corrected
    (I : TwoSidedIdeal (Matrix (Fin 2) (Fin 2) ℝ)) :
    I = ⊥ ∨ I = ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that any ideal of the ring of 2×2 real matrices is either the zero ideal or the whole ring. The Lean signature quantifies over `TwoSidedIdeal (Matrix (Fin 2) (Fin 2) ℝ)` and concludes `I = ⊥ ∨ I = ⊤`, where `⊥` is the zero ideal and `⊤` is the whole ring. In the noncommutative setting of matrix rings, "ideal" conventionally means two-sided ideal, matching `TwoSidedIdeal`. The matrix ring `Matrix (Fin 2) (Fin 2) ℝ` correctly encodes 2×2 matrices over ℝ. The quantifier structure (for all ideals I) and conclusion match exactly.

### proofnet-88

**Informal statement**

> Let $F = \mathbb{Z}_p$ be the field of integers $\mod p$, where $p$ is a prime, and let $q(x) \in F[x]$ be irreducible of degree $n$. Show that $F[x]/(q(x))$ is a field having at exactly $p^n$ elements.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_5_16 {p n: ℕ} (hp : Nat.Prime p)
  {q : Polynomial (ZMod p)} (hq : Irreducible q) (hn : q.degree = n) :
  (∃ is_fin : Fintype $ Polynomial (ZMod p) ⧸ span ({q}),
  @card (Polynomial (ZMod p) ⧸ span {q}) is_fin = p ^ n) ∧
  IsField (Polynomial (ZMod p) ⧸ span {q})
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature uses F = ZMod p with p prime, q an irreducible polynomial of degree n, and concludes both that the quotient Polynomial (ZMod p) / span {q} has a Fintype instance with cardinality p^n and that it IsField. The hypothesis q.degree = n correctly pins down the degree (with n coerced into WithBot ℕ). The ideal span {q} is the principal ideal (q), so the quotient is F[x]/(q(x)). Both claimed conclusions — being a field and having exactly p^n elements — match the informal statement, with no extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-91

**Informal statement**

> Let $V$ be a vector space over an infinite field $F$. Show that $V$ cannot be the set-theoretic union of a finite number of proper subspaces of $V$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_5_2_20 {F V ι: Type*} [Infinite F] [Field F]
  [AddCommGroup V] [Module F V] [Finite ι] {u : ι → Submodule F V}
  (hu : ∀ i : ι, u i ≠ ⊤) :
  (⋃ i : ι, (u i : Set V)) ≠ ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement captures the informal claim precisely: `Infinite F` + `Field F` matches "infinite field", `AddCommGroup V` + `Module F V` makes V a vector space over F, `Finite ι` with `u : ι → Submodule F V` indexes a finite collection of subspaces, and `u i ≠ ⊤` says each is a proper subspace. The conclusion `(⋃ i, (u i : Set V)) ≠ ⊤` asserts (in `Set V`, where `⊤ = univ`) that the set-theoretic union is not all of V, which is exactly the informal conclusion. The empty-index edge case is consistent (the empty union is ∅ ≠ univ since V contains 0). No hidden hypothesis is added or dropped.

### proofnet-96  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_2_21_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Define $\wedge(n)=\log p$ if $n$ is a power of $p$ and zero otherwise. Prove that $\sum_{A \mid n} \mu(n / d) \log d$ $=\wedge(n)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_2_21_corrected {l : ℕ → ℝ}
  (hl : ∀ p n : ℕ, p.Prime → 0 < n → l (p ^ n) = Real.log p)
  (hl1 : ∀ m : ℕ, ¬ IsPrimePow m → l m = 0) :
  l = fun n ↦ (∑ d ∈ Nat.divisors n, ((ArithmeticFunction.moebius (n / d) : ℤ) : ℝ) * log d)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypotheses on `l` exactly characterize the von Mangoldt function Λ: `hl` gives l(p^n) = log p for prime p and n ≥ 1 (matching "power of p" with positive exponent), and `hl1` gives l(m) = 0 whenever m is not a prime power (using Mathlib's `IsPrimePow`, which means m = p^k with k ≥ 1). Edge cases align: l(1) = 0 (since 1 is not a prime power), and l(0) = 0, with the divisor sum at n=0 being empty (=0) and at n=1 being μ(1)·log 1 = 0. The conclusion `l = fun n ↦ ∑ d ∈ divisors n, μ(n/d) · log d` is function equality, i.e. for all n the identity Λ(n) = Σ_{d|n} μ(n/d) log d holds — exactly the informal claim. The reindexing in the sum (μ(n/d) log d vs. μ(d) log(n/d)) matches the informal statement directly. This is a faithful formalization.

The notation `[ZMOD 6]` denotes equality in `ZMod 6`, so this says (p : ZMod 6) = -1, which is exactly p ≡ 5 (mod 6). `Infinite` on the subtype correctly captures "infinitely many such primes." No extra hypotheses or weakening are introduced.

### proofnet-99

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_3_14 {p q n : ℕ} (hp0 : p.Prime ∧ p > 2)
  (hq0 : q.Prime ∧ q > 2) (hpq0 : p ≠ q) (hpq1 : p - 1 ∣ q - 1)

### proofnet-100  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_5_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Consider a prime $p$ of the form $4 t+3$. Show that $a$ is a primitive root modulo $p$ iff $-a$ has order $(p-1) / 2$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_5_corrected {p t : ℕ} (hp0 : p.Prime) (hp1 : p = 4 * t + 3)
  (a : (ZMod p)ˣ) :
  IsPrimitiveRoot (a : ZMod p) (p - 1) ↔
    IsPrimitiveRoot (- (a : ZMod p)) ((p - 1) / 2)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement assumes p is prime and equals 4t+3 (matching the informal hypothesis that p is a prime of form 4t+3). It then states the iff: `IsPrimitiveRoot a (p-1)` (a has order p-1, i.e., a is a primitive root mod p) iff `IsPrimitiveRoot (-a) ((p-1)/2)` (-a has order (p-1)/2). The element a is given as a unit of ZMod p, which is appropriate since being a primitive root requires a to be a unit (and -a a unit follows). The biconditional and quantifier structure faithfully match "a is a primitive root iff -a has order (p-1)/2". No extra or missing hypotheses materially alter the claim.

### proofnet-101  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_8_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $p$ be an odd prime. Show that $a$ is a primitive root modulo $p$ iff $a^{(p-1) / q} \not \equiv 1(p)$ for all prime divisors $q$ of $p-1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_8_corrected {p : ℕ} (hp : Odd p) (hp' : p.Prime) (a : (ZMod p)ˣ) :
  IsPrimitiveRoot (a : ZMod p) (p - 1) ↔
    ∀ q : ℕ, q ∣ (p - 1) → q.Prime →
      ¬ ((a : ZMod p) ^ ((p - 1) / q) = 1)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes "a is a primitive root mod p" as `IsPrimitiveRoot (a : ZMod p) (p - 1)`, which matches because the multiplicative group of ZMod p has order p−1 when p is prime. Taking `a : (ZMod p)ˣ` reflects the implicit assumption gcd(a,p)=1 in the informal statement. The right-hand side quantifies over natural numbers q with both `q ∣ (p−1)` and `q.Prime`, which is precisely "for all prime divisors q of p−1", and asserts a^((p−1)/q) ≠ 1 in ZMod p. The hypotheses `Odd p` and `p.Prime` correctly capture "odd prime p". The biconditional matches the informal "iff", so the formalization is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-103

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $a$ is negative then $p \equiv q(4 a)$ together with $p\not | a$ imply $(a / p)=(a / q)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_5_37 {p q : ℕ} [Fact (p.Prime)] [Fact (q.Prime)] {a : ℤ}
  (ha : a < 0) (h0 : p ≡ q [ZMOD 4*a]) (h1 : ¬ ((p : ℤ) ∣ a)) :
  legendreSym p a = legendreSym q a
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the same claim: a < 0, p ≡ q mod 4a, p ∤ a, conclude legendreSym p a = legendreSym q a. The apparent absence of an "odd prime" hypothesis is harmless because p ≡ q mod 4a with |4a| ≥ 4 forces p = 2 ↔ q = 2, in which case the conclusion is trivial; legendreSym is well-defined in Mathlib for p = 2 as well. The seemingly missing q ∤ a hypothesis follows from the given ones: if q | a, then since a | 4a we get a | p, forcing a ∈ {±1,±p}, contradicting either a < 0 with q prime or p ∤ a. The modular condition `[ZMOD 4*a]` is the correct integer congruence mod 4a, and types/coercions match. So the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal one.

### proofnet-105

**Informal statement**
