```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_1 (X : Type*) [TopologicalSpace X] (A : Set X)
  (h1 : ∀ x ∈ A, ∃ U : Set X, x ∈ U ∧ IsOpen U ∧ U ⊆ A) :
  IsOpen A
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature introduces a topological space X, a subset A, and the hypothesis that for every x in A there exists an open set U containing x with U ⊆ A, then concludes IsOpen A. This matches the informal statement exactly: same hypothesis (pointwise local openness), same conclusion (A is open), and no extra assumptions like nonemptiness or finiteness. This is a standard, faithful encoding.

### proofnet-106

**Informal statement**

> If $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$ is a family of topologies on $X$, show that $\bigcap \mathcal{T}_\alpha$ is a topology on $X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_4a1 (X I : Type*) (T : I → Set (Set X)) (h : ∀ i, is_topology X (T i)) :
  is_topology X (⋂ i : I, T i)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature quantifies over an arbitrary index type I and an arbitrary family T : I → Set (Set X) of collections that are each topologies on X (via the hypothesis `∀ i, is_topology X (T i)`), and concludes that the indexed intersection `⋂ i : I, T i` is also a topology on X. This is exactly the informal claim that the intersection of a family of topologies on X is a topology on X. The index type I is allowed to be empty or arbitrary, matching the informal "family ${\mathcal T}_\alpha$". Assuming `is_topology` is the standard predicate, the encoding is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-107

**Informal statement**

> Let $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$ be a family of topologies on $X$. Show that there is a unique smallest topology on $X$ containing all the collections $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_4b1 (X I : Type*) (T : I → Set (Set X)) (h : ∀ i, is_topology X (T i)) :
  ∃! T', is_topology X T' ∧ (∀ i, T i ⊆ T') ∧
  ∀ T'', is_topology X T'' → (∀ i, T i ⊆ T'') → T' ⊆ T''
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature asserts the existence of a unique T' on X that (a) is a topology, (b) contains every T_i, and (c) is contained in every other topology T'' that contains all T_i. This is precisely the definition of "the unique smallest topology containing all the T_α". The use of ∃! captures uniqueness, the inclusion conditions encode "smallest", and the universal quantification over the family I matches the informal "family T_α". The hypothesis `is_topology X (T i)` for every i matches the assumption that each T_α is a topology. No extra hypotheses (e.g. finiteness, nonemptiness of I) are imposed, and the conclusion matches exactly.

### proofnet-108

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $\mathcal{A}$ is a basis for a topology on $X$, then the topology generated by $\mathcal{A}$ equals the intersection of all topologies on $X$ that contain $\mathcal{A}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_5a {X : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] (A : Set (Set X)) (hA : IsTopologicalBasis A) :
  generateFrom A = generateFrom (sInter {T | is_topology X T ∧ A ⊆ T})
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is: topology generated by A = intersection of all topologies containing A. In the Lean statement, the LHS `generateFrom A` is exactly the topology generated by A (smallest topology containing A). On the RHS, `sInter {T | is_topology X T ∧ A ⊆ T}` is the intersection of all topologies on X containing A (viewed as a set of sets), which is itself a topology; applying `generateFrom` to a collection that is already a topology yields that same topology back. So the Lean equality `generateFrom A = generateFrom (sInter …)` is logically equivalent to "topology generated by A = the intersection topology." The `[TopologicalSpace X]` instance is just needed to make `IsTopologicalBasis` typecheck and does not constrain the conclusion, which depends only on A. The hypothesis `IsTopologicalBasis A` matches "A is a basis for a topology on X." The encoding is a bit awkward (the extra `generateFrom` on the RHS is a no-op) but does not change the mathematical content.

### proofnet-109

**Informal statement**

> Show that the lower limit topology $\mathbb{R}_l$ and $K$-topology $\mathbb{R}_K$ are not comparable.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_6 :
  ¬ (∀ U, Rl.IsOpen U → K_topology.IsOpen U) ∧ ¬ (∀ U, K_topology.IsOpen U → Rl.IsOpen U)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

Two topologies being "not comparable" means neither is finer than the other, i.e., neither inclusion of open sets holds. The Lean statement asserts exactly this: ¬(every Rl-open is K-open) ∧ ¬(every K-open is Rl-open), which is the standard definition of incomparable topologies. The quantification over open sets U with the IsOpen predicates correctly captures the finer/coarser relation in both directions.

### proofnet-110

**Informal statement**

> Show that the collection $\{(a,b) \mid a < b, a \text{ and } b \text{ rational}\}$ is a basis that generates a topology different from the lower limit topology on $\mathbb{R}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_8b (T : Set (Set ℝ))
  (hT : T = {S : Set ℝ | ∃ a b : ℚ, a < b ∧ S = Ioo ↑a ↑b}) :
  IsTopologicalBasis T ∧
  generateFrom T ≠
  lower_limit_topology ℝ
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement (as given) refers to the collection of open intervals (a,b) with rational endpoints a<b. The Lean signature defines T as exactly {Ioo ↑a ↑b | a b : ℚ, a < b}, where Ioo is the open interval — matching the informal "(a,b) with a and b rational". The conclusion has two parts: (i) T is a topological basis (IsTopologicalBasis), and (ii) the topology generated by T is not equal to the lower-limit topology on ℝ — which is exactly what the informal statement asks to show. Quantifier structure, types (ℚ for endpoints, ℝ for the ambient space), and the inequality constraint are all preserved. No extra hypotheses are introduced and none are dropped.

### proofnet-111

**Informal statement**

> A map $f: X \rightarrow Y$ is said to be an open map if for every open set $U$ of $X$, the set $f(U)$ is open in $Y$. Show that $\pi_{1}: X \times Y \rightarrow X$ and $\pi_{2}: X \times Y \rightarrow Y$ are open maps.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_16_4 {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]
  (π₁ : X × Y → X)
  (π₂ : X × Y → Y)
  (h₁ : π₁ = Prod.fst)
  (h₂ : π₂ = Prod.snd) :
  IsOpenMap π₁ ∧ IsOpenMap π₂
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show that the two projections from a product space X × Y are open maps. The Lean signature takes arbitrary topological spaces X and Y, introduces π₁ and π₂ as functions equal to Prod.fst and Prod.snd respectively, and concludes IsOpenMap π₁ ∧ IsOpenMap π₂. Since the hypotheses pin down π₁ and π₂ as exactly the standard projections, this is logically equivalent to asserting that Prod.fst and Prod.snd are open maps. The Mathlib definition IsOpenMap matches the informal definition (image of every open set is open). No extra hypotheses (like finiteness, Hausdorff, etc.) are imposed, and nothing is dropped. Hence the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-112

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $U$ is open in $X$ and $A$ is closed in $X$, then $U-A$ is open in $X$, and $A-U$ is closed in $X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_17_4 {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  (U A : Set X) (hU : IsOpen U) (hA : IsClosed A) :
  IsOpen (U \ A) ∧ IsClosed (A \ U)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes an arbitrary topological space X, an open set U, and a closed set A, and concludes that U \ A is open and A \ U is closed. This matches the informal statement exactly: both conjuncts are stated, the hypotheses (U open, A closed) match, and the set difference U - A is correctly encoded as U \ A. No extra assumptions (like finiteness or T2) are imposed, and nothing is missing.

### proofnet-113

**Informal statement**

> Let $Y$ be an ordered set in the order topology. Let $f, g: X \rightarrow Y$ be continuous. Let $h: X \rightarrow Y$ be the function $h(x)=\min \{f(x), g(x)\}.$ Show that $h$ is continuous.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_18_8b {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]
  [LinearOrder Y] [OrderTopology Y] {f g : X → Y}
  (hf : Continuous f) (hg : Continuous g) :
  Continuous (λ x => min (f x) (g x))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal statement faithfully. Y is given a LinearOrder and OrderTopology, matching "ordered set in the order topology". X is an arbitrary topological space, f and g are continuous, and the conclusion is the continuity of x ↦ min(f x, g x), which is exactly h. LinearOrder is the standard Mathlib formalization of a (totally) ordered set, which is needed for `min` to be defined, and matches the informal usage. No extra hypotheses (e.g., Fintype, decidability beyond what LinearOrder provides) are silently introduced, and none are missing.

### proofnet-114

**Informal statement**

> Let $\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, \ldots$ be a sequence of the points of the product space $\prod X_\alpha$.  Show that this sequence converges to the point $\mathbf{x}$ if and only if the sequence $\pi_\alpha(\mathbf{x}_i)$ converges to $\pi_\alpha(\mathbf{x})$ for each $\alpha$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_19_6a
  {ι : Type*}
  {f : ι → Type*} {x : ℕ → Πa, f a}
  (y : Πi, f i)
  [Πa, TopologicalSpace (f a)] :
  Tendsto x atTop (𝓝 y) ↔ ∀ i, Tendsto (λ j => (x j) i) atTop (𝓝 (y i))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: in the product space ∏ X_α, a sequence x_n converges to x iff each projection π_α(x_n) converges to π_α(x). The Lean signature has a sequence x : ℕ → Π a, f a, a target y, and each f a carries a TopologicalSpace, which gives the product (Pi) topology automatically on Π a, f a. `Tendsto x atTop (𝓝 y)` is exactly sequence convergence, and the right-hand side says each coordinate-projection sequence j ↦ (x j) i tends to y i. The quantification over all coordinates i matches "for each α". No extra hypotheses (e.g., finiteness, Hausdorff, decidability) are added, and none are missing. This is the standard, faithful formalization.

### proofnet-115

**Informal statement**

> Define $f_{n}:[0,1] \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ by the equation $f_{n}(x)=x^{n}$. Show that the sequence $\left(f_{n}(x)\right)$ converges for each $x \in[0,1]$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_21_6a
  (f : ℕ → I → ℝ)
  (h : ∀ x n, f n x = ↑x ^ n) :
  ∀ x, ∃ y, Tendsto (λ n => f n x) atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature uses `I` (the unit interval [0,1]) as the domain, defines `f n x = x^n` via the hypothesis `h`, and concludes that for every `x ∈ [0,1]` there exists `y` with `f n x → y` as `n → ∞`. This is exactly the informal claim that the sequence (f_n(x)) converges for each x ∈ [0,1] (convergence of a real sequence means existence of a limit). The quantifier structure (∀ x, ∃ y) matches pointwise convergence, no extra hypotheses are added, and no specific limit value is required, so the statement is neither weaker nor stronger.

### proofnet-116

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be a topological space and let $Y$ be a metric space. Let $f_{n}: X \rightarrow Y$ be a sequence of continuous functions. Let $x_{n}$ be a sequence of points of $X$ converging to $x$. Show that if the sequence $\left(f_{n}\right)$ converges uniformly to $f$, then $\left(f_{n}\left(x_{n}\right)\right)$ converges to $f(x)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_21_8
  {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] {Y : Type*} [MetricSpace Y]
  {f : ℕ → X → Y} {x : ℕ → X}
  (hf : ∀ n, Continuous (f n))
  (x₀ : X)
  (hx : Tendsto x atTop (𝓝 x₀))
  (f₀ : X → Y)
  (hh : TendstoUniformly f f₀ atTop) :
  Tendsto (λ n => f n (x n)) atTop (𝓝 (f₀ x₀))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the same claim: X is a topological space, Y a metric space, f n are continuous functions X → Y, x n is a sequence in X converging to x₀, f converges uniformly to f₀, and the conclusion is that f n (x n) → f₀ x₀. The hypotheses (continuity of each f n, convergence of xₙ, uniform convergence) and the conclusion match exactly. TendstoUniformly with atTop is the standard Mathlib encoding of uniform convergence of a sequence. No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-117

**Informal statement**

> If $A \subset X$, a retraction of $X$ onto $A$ is a continuous map $r: X \rightarrow A$ such that $r(a)=a$ for each $a \in A$. Show that a retraction is a quotient map.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_22_2b {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  {A : Set X} (r : X → A) (hr : Continuous r) (h : ∀ x : A, r x = x) :
  IsQuotientMap r
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all components of the informal statement: X is a topological space, A is a subset of X (as `Set X`), r : X → A is continuous, and r restricted to A is the identity (∀ x : A, r x = x, where x : A is coerced to X on the input side and equality is in A). The conclusion `IsQuotientMap r` matches the claim that a retraction is a quotient map. The encoding of r as a map into the subtype A (rather than into X with image in A) is the standard Mathlib way to express a retraction onto A and is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-118

**Informal statement**

> Let $\left\{A_{n}\right\}$ be a sequence of connected subspaces of $X$, such that $A_{n} \cap A_{n+1} \neq \varnothing$ for all $n$. Show that $\bigcup A_{n}$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_23_2 {X : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] {A : ℕ → Set X} (hA : ∀ n, IsConnected (A n))
  (hAn : ∀ n, A n ∩ A (n + 1) ≠ ∅) :
  IsConnected (⋃ n, A n)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement quantifies a sequence A : ℕ → Set X of connected subspaces with consecutive nonempty intersections, and concludes the union is connected — exactly the informal claim. Mathlib's `IsConnected` requires nonemptiness, but each A_n is forced nonempty by `A n ∩ A (n+1) ≠ ∅`, so this matches the standard reading where "connected subspace" is nonempty. The conclusion `IsConnected (⋃ n, A n)` is likewise nonempty-and-connected, which agrees with the informal conclusion. No extra hypotheses (e.g., Hausdorff, finiteness) are imposed, and none are dropped.

### proofnet-120

**Informal statement**

> Let $A$ be a proper subset of $X$, and let $B$ be a proper subset of $Y$. If $X$ and $Y$ are connected, show that $(X \times Y)-(A \times B)$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_23_9 {X Y : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]
  (A₁ A₂ : Set X)
  (B₁ B₂ : Set Y)
  (hA : A₁ ⊂ A₂)
  (hB : B₁ ⊂ B₂)
  (hA : IsConnected A₂)
  (hB : IsConnected B₂) :
  IsConnected ({x | ∃ a b, x = (a, b) ∧ a ∈ A₂ ∧ b ∈ B₂} \
      {x | ∃ a b, x = (a, b) ∧ a ∈ A₁ ∧ b ∈ B₁})
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement reformulates the problem in a "subspace-style" way: instead of taking X and Y as the ambient connected spaces and A ⊂ X, B ⊂ Y as proper subsets, it takes connected subsets A₂ ⊆ X and B₂ ⊆ Y (via `IsConnected A₂/B₂`, which uses the subspace topology) and proper subsets A₁ ⊊ A₂, B₁ ⊊ B₂ (Lean's `⊂` on Set is strict subset). The conclusion that `(A₂ ×ˢ B₂) \ (A₁ ×ˢ B₁)` is connected is exactly the informal conclusion applied to the connected spaces A₂, B₂ with proper subsets A₁, B₁. Conversely, instantiating A₂ = univ, B₂ = univ recovers the informal statement verbatim. So the two statements are logically equivalent. The duplicated hypothesis names (`hA`, `hB` reused) only cause shadowing in tactic mode but do not drop any hypothesis from the statement. Hence the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-121  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_24_2_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $f: S^{1} \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ be a continuous map. Show there exists a point $x$ of $S^{1}$ such that $f(x)=f(-x)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Munkres_exercise_24_2_corrected
    {f : (Metric.sphere (0 : ℂ) 1 : Set ℂ) → ℝ}
    (hf : Continuous f) : ∃ x, f x = f (-x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes S^1 as the unit sphere of radius 1 centered at 0 in ℂ, which is the standard identification of S^1. The map f is a continuous function from this circle to ℝ, matching the informal hypothesis. The conclusion ∃ x, f x = f (-x) expresses precisely that some point and its antipode map to the same value, since negation on the unit circle in ℂ is the antipodal map (and the subtype on the sphere centered at 0 inherits a Neg instance because ‖-x‖ = ‖x‖). Quantifier structure, types, and the conclusion all align with the informal claim, so the formalization is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-122

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be locally path connected. Show that every connected open set in $X$ is path connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_25_4 {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  [LocPathConnectedSpace X] (U : Set X) (hU : IsOpen U)
  (hcU : IsConnected U) : IsPathConnected U
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that in a locally path connected space, every connected open set is path connected. The Lean signature assumes `LocPathConnectedSpace X`, takes an open set `U` with `IsOpen U` and `IsConnected U`, and concludes `IsPathConnected U`. In Mathlib, `IsConnected` bundles nonemptiness with preconnectedness, and `IsPathConnected` likewise requires nonemptiness; this matches the standard convention in Munkres where "connected" sets are implicitly nonempty (the empty set case is vacuous anyway). The hypotheses, conclusion, and quantifier structure all align with the informal claim.

### proofnet-125

**Informal statement**

> Show that X is countably compact if and only if every nested sequence $C_1 \supset C_2 \supset \cdots$ of closed nonempty sets of X has a nonempty intersection.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_28_5
  (X : Type*) [TopologicalSpace X] :
  countably_compact X ↔ ∀ (C : ℕ → Set X), (∀ n, IsClosed (C n)) ∧
  (∀ n, C n ≠ ∅) ∧ (∀ n, C (n + 1) ⊆ C n) → ∃ x, ∀ n, x ∈ C n
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts countably_compact X ↔ for every sequence C : ℕ → Set X satisfying (each C n is closed) ∧ (each C n is nonempty, encoded as C n ≠ ∅) ∧ (nested: C (n+1) ⊆ C n), there exists x ∈ ⋂ C n (encoded as ∃ x, ∀ n, x ∈ C n). The `→` has lower precedence than `∧`, so the conjunction is correctly the hypothesis. This matches the informal claim: nested decreasing sequence of closed nonempty sets has nonempty intersection. The encoding of "nonempty intersection" via ∃ x, ∀ n, x ∈ C n is logically equivalent to (⋂ n, C n).Nonempty. Assuming countably_compact is the standard definition, the formalization is faithful.

theorem Munkres_exercise_29_1 : ¬ LocallyCompactSpace ℚ

### proofnet-127

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $X$ is a Hausdorff space that is locally compact at the point $x$, then for each neighborhood $U$ of $x$, there is a neighborhood $V$ of $x$ such that $\bar{V}$ is compact and $\bar{V} \subset U$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_29_10 {X : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] [T2Space X] (x : X)
  (hx : ∃ U : Set X, x ∈ U ∧ IsOpen U ∧ (∃ K : Set X, U ⊆ K ∧ IsCompact K))
  (U : Set X) (hU : IsOpen U) (hxU : x ∈ U) :
  ∃ (V : Set X), IsOpen V ∧ x ∈ V ∧ IsCompact (closure V) ∧ closure V ⊆ U
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypothesis `hx` encodes "x has a compact neighborhood": there is an open set U containing x with U ⊆ K for some compact K, which is the standard definition of locally compact at x (x lies in the interior of a compact set). The conclusion provides an open V containing x with closure V compact and closure V ⊆ U, matching the informal claim. Munkres's convention takes "neighborhood" to mean an open set containing the point, so requiring U and V to be open matches the informal reading. T2Space gives the Hausdorff hypothesis. The quantifier structure (for every open neighborhood U of x, there exists V) matches. No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-128

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $X$ has a countable dense subset, every collection of disjoint open sets in $X$ is countable.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_30_13 {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  (h : ∃ (s : Set X), Countable s ∧ Dense s) (U : Set (Set X))
  (hU : ∀ (x y : Set X), x ∈ U → y ∈ U → x ≠ y → x ∩ y = ∅)
  (hUopen : ∀ (u : Set X), u ∈ U → IsOpen u) :
  Countable U
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is the standard separability result: if X has a countable dense subset, every pairwise-disjoint collection of open sets is countable. The Lean signature encodes the hypotheses correctly: `h` provides a countable dense subset (Countable s ∧ Dense s), `hU` expresses pairwise disjointness via x ≠ y → x ∩ y = ∅, and `hUopen` says each member of U is open. The conclusion `Countable U` matches. The empty set can technically be a member of U, but since U is a `Set (Set X)` it can appear at most once and does not affect countability, so this does not break equivalence with the informal statement. Types and quantifier structure align with the original claim.

### proofnet-129

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $X$ is normal, every pair of disjoint closed sets have neighborhoods whose closures are disjoint.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_31_2 {X : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] [NormalSpace X] {A B : Set X}
  (hA : IsClosed A) (hB : IsClosed B) (hAB : Disjoint A B) :
  ∃ (U V : Set X), IsOpen U ∧ IsOpen V ∧ A ⊆ U ∧ B ⊆ V ∧ closure U ∩ closure V = ∅
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: in a normal space, disjoint closed sets A, B have neighborhoods (open sets containing them) whose closures are disjoint. The Lean signature assumes NormalSpace X, takes disjoint closed A, B, and asserts existence of open U, V with A ⊆ U, B ⊆ V, and closure U ∩ closure V = ∅. "Neighborhoods" of a set typically means open sets containing the set (or sets whose interior contains it); using open sets here matches standard usage. Disjoint closures is expressed as their intersection being empty, which is equivalent. The hypotheses (normality, closedness, disjointness) match the informal version exactly.

### proofnet-134

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be completely regular. Show that $X$ is connected if and only if the Stone-Čech compactification of $X$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_38_6 {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [CompletelyRegularSpace X] :
  IsConnected (univ : Set X) ↔ IsConnected (univ : Set (StoneCech X))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes X to be a topological space with CompletelyRegularSpace, matching "X completely regular." The biconditional `IsConnected univ ↔ IsConnected univ` on X and StoneCech X correctly expresses "X is connected iff its Stone-Čech compactification is connected." Mathlib's `IsConnected` requires nonemptiness as well as preconnectedness, but this is the standard convention used in Munkres for connected spaces, and it applies symmetrically on both sides (X is empty iff StoneCech X is empty). The hypothesis CompletelyRegularSpace matches the informal hypothesis. No extra or missing assumptions, no quantifier or type drift.

### proofnet-135

**Informal statement**

> Let $(p_n)$ be a sequence and $f:\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$. The sequence $(q_k)_{k\in\mathbb{N}}$ with $q_k=p_{f(k)}$ is called a rearrangement of $(p_n)$. Show that if $f$ is an injection, the limit of a sequence is unaffected by rearrangement.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_12a {α : Type*} [TopologicalSpace α]
  (f : ℕ → ℕ) (p : ℕ → α) (a : α)
  (hf : Injective f) (hp : Tendsto p atTop (𝓝 a)) :
  Tendsto (λ n => p (f n)) atTop (𝓝 a)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement captures exactly the informal claim: given an injection f : ℕ → ℕ and a sequence p that tends to a, the rearranged sequence k ↦ p(f k) also tends to a. The hypothesis of injectivity matches, the conclusion (Tendsto … atTop (𝓝 a)) is precisely "the limit is unaffected by rearrangement." Generalizing the codomain from ℝ (Pugh's context) to an arbitrary topological space is incidental — injectivity of f : ℕ → ℕ implies f(k) → ∞ (preimages of finite sets are finite), so the same proof works, and the statement form is identical. No hypothesis is silently added or dropped, and the quantifier structure matches.

### proofnet-136

**Informal statement**

> Let $\mathcal{T}$ be the collection of open subsets of a metric space $\mathrm{M}$, and $\mathcal{K}$ the collection of closed subsets. Show that there is a bijection from $\mathcal{T}$ onto $\mathcal{K}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_29 (M : Type*) [MetricSpace M]
  (O C : Set (Set M))
  (hO : O = {s | IsOpen s})
  (hC : C = {s | IsClosed s}) :
  ∃ f : O → C, Bijective f
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks for a bijection between the collection of open subsets and the collection of closed subsets of a metric space M. The Lean signature defines O and C as the sets of open and closed subsets respectively, and asks for a bijection f : O → C (where O and C are coerced to subtypes). The natural bijection is complementation, and `Bijective f` correctly expresses bijectivity. The types and quantifier structure match the informal claim exactly.

### proofnet-138  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Pugh_exercise_2_57_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $S$ is connected, it is not true in general that its interior is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Pugh_exercise_2_57_corrected :
    ∃ S : Set (ℝ × ℝ), IsConnected S ∧ ¬ IsConnected (interior S)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim "it is not true in general that the interior of a connected set is connected" is exactly the negation of "for all connected S, interior S is connected", which is equivalent to "there exists a connected S whose interior is disconnected". The Lean signature asserts the existence of such an S in ℝ×ℝ. Pugh's exercise 2.57 is set in Euclidean space (ℝ²), so restricting to ℝ×ℝ matches the intended ambient space. The use of IsConnected (which in Mathlib also requires nonemptiness) is consistent on both sides — the counterexample S must be nonempty, and its interior being "not connected" includes the empty case as well as disconnected cases, matching the informal reading. Hence the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-140

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\sqrt{n+1}-\sqrt{n} \rightarrow 0$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_3_4 (n : ℕ) :
  Tendsto (λ n => (sqrt (n + 1) - sqrt n)) atTop (𝓝 0)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts Tendsto (fun n => sqrt(n+1) - sqrt n) atTop (𝓝 0), which is exactly the claim that √(n+1) − √n → 0 as n → ∞. The outer parameter `n : ℕ` is unused (shadowed by the lambda), so universally quantifying over it does not change the logical content: ∀ n, P is equivalent to P when n does not occur in P. Assuming `sqrt` refers to `Real.sqrt` (with the natural-number argument coerced to ℝ), the limit statement matches the informal claim exactly. No hypotheses are added or dropped, and the conclusion is the same limit.

### proofnet-142  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_1998_a3`

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ be a real function on the real line with continuous third derivative. Prove that there exists a point $a$ such that $f(a) \cdot f^{\prime}(a) \cdot f^{\prime \prime}(a) \cdot f^{\prime \prime \prime}(a) \geq 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_1998_a3 (f : ℝ → ℝ) (hf : ContDiff ℝ 3 f) :
  ∃ a : ℝ, (f a) * (deriv f a) * (iteratedDeriv 2 f a) * (iteratedDeriv 3 f a) ≥ 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal hypothesis "f has continuous third derivative on ℝ" corresponds exactly to `ContDiff ℝ 3 f`, which asserts that f is three times continuously differentiable. The conclusion asks for the existence of a point a where the product f(a)·f'(a)·f''(a)·f'''(a) is nonnegative; the Lean version uses `deriv f` for the first derivative and `iteratedDeriv 2 f`, `iteratedDeriv 3 f` for the second and third derivatives, which are the standard Mathlib encodings and agree with f', f'', f''' for C^3 functions on ℝ. The quantifier structure (∃ a : ℝ) and the inequality (≥ 0) match. No extra or missing hypotheses are present.

### proofnet-143  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2000_a2`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that there exist infinitely many integers $n$ such that $n, n+1, n+2$ are each the sum of the squares of two integers.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_2000_a2 :
  ∀ N : ℕ, ∃ n : ℕ, n > N ∧ ∃ i : Fin 6 → ℕ, n = (i 0)^2 + (i 1)^2 ∧
  n + 1 = (i 2)^2 + (i 3)^2 ∧ n + 2 = (i 4)^2 + (i 5)^2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean encoding "∀ N, ∃ n > N, P(n)" is the standard way to express "there exist infinitely many n satisfying P". Although the informal statement says "integers" while Lean uses ℕ, any integer that is a sum of two integer squares is necessarily non-negative, so the set of relevant n coincides with a subset of ℕ. Likewise, an integer m is a sum of two integer squares iff it is a sum of two natural-number squares (take absolute values), so writing the squares with i : Fin 6 → ℕ does not change the assertion. The three conditions n = a²+b², n+1 = c²+d², n+2 = e²+f² match exactly the informal requirement that n, n+1, n+2 are each sums of two squares. Therefore the Lean signature is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Putnam_exercise_2010_a4 (n : ℕ) (hn : n > 0) :
    ¬ Nat.Prime (10^10^10^n + 10^10^n + 10^n - 1)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature quantifies over n : ℕ with the hypothesis n > 0, matching "for each positive integer n." The expression 10^10^10^n + 10^10^n + 10^n - 1 mirrors the informal expression exactly (right-associative exponentiation in Lean matches the tower notation). Although natural-number subtraction is truncating, for n ≥ 1 the value 10^10^10^n + 10^10^n + 10^n is at least 10, so subtracting 1 behaves as ordinary subtraction and the value coincides with the integer-arithmetic interpretation. The conclusion ¬ Nat.Prime asserts non-primality, matching "is not prime." No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-145  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2017_b3`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $f(x)=\sum_{i=0}^{\infty} c_{i} x^{i}$ is a power series for which each coefficient $c_{i}$ is 0 or 1 . Show that if $f(2 / 3)=3 / 2$, then $f(1 / 2)$ must be irrational.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_2017_b3 (f : ℝ → ℝ) (c : ℕ → ℝ)
  (hf : f = λ x => (∑' (i : ℕ), (c i) * x^i))
  (hc : ∀ n, c n = 0 ∨ c n = 1)
  (hf1 : f (2/3) = 3/2) :
  Irrational (f (1/2))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes f as the tsum of c_i * x^i over ℕ, requires each c_i ∈ {0,1}, hypothesizes f(2/3) = 3/2, and concludes Irrational (f(1/2)) — exactly matching the informal statement. The use of `tsum` is appropriate: with 0/1 coefficients and convergence at x = 2/3, the series also converges absolutely at x = 1/2, so `tsum` agrees with the usual sum and `f(1/2)` denotes the genuine value of the power series at 1/2. Quantifier structure (universal over all such f and c) and types (real coefficients/values) are correct. No extra hypotheses or weakened conclusions are introduced.

### proofnet-146  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2018_b2`

**Informal statement**

> Let $n$ be a positive integer, and let $f_{n}(z)=n+(n-1) z+$ $(n-2) z^{2}+\cdots+z^{n-1}$. Prove that $f_{n}$ has no roots in the closed unit disk $\{z \in \mathbb{C}:|z| \leq 1\}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Putnam_exercise_2018_b2 (n : ℕ) (hn : n > 0) (f : ℕ → ℂ → ℂ)
  (hf : ∀ n : ℕ, f n = λ (z : ℂ) => (∑ i : Fin n, (n-i)* z^(i : ℕ))) :
  ¬ (∃ z : ℂ, ‖z‖ ≤ 1 ∧ f n z = 0)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean polynomial ∑_{i:Fin n} (n-i)·z^i expands to n + (n-1)z + (n-2)z² + ⋯ + 1·z^(n-1), which exactly matches f_n. The natural-number subtraction n-i is safe since i < n, and the coefficients coerce to ℂ correctly. The hypothesis hn : n > 0 matches "positive integer n". The conclusion ¬∃ z, ‖z‖ ≤ 1 ∧ f n z = 0 says exactly that f_n has no root in the closed unit disk (‖·‖ on ℂ is the modulus). The universally-quantified hypothesis hf defining f for all n is harmless — it pins down f n exactly. So the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal one.

### proofnet-147  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2020_b5`

**Informal statement**

> For $j \in\{1,2,3,4\}$, let $z_{j}$ be a complex number with $\left|z_{j}\right|=1$ and $z_{j} \neq 1$. Prove that $3-z_{1}-z_{2}-z_{3}-z_{4}+z_{1} z_{2} z_{3} z_{4} \neq 0 .$

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_2020_b5 (z : Fin 4 → ℂ) (hz0 : ∀ n, ‖z n‖ = 1)
  (hz1 : ∀ n, z n ≠ 1) :
  3 - z 0 - z 1 - z 2 - z 3 + (z 0) * (z 1) * (z 2) * (z 3) ≠ 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes the four complex numbers as a function `z : Fin 4 → ℂ`, with hypotheses `‖z n‖ = 1` (unit modulus) and `z n ≠ 1` for every index, matching the informal conditions on $z_1, z_2, z_3, z_4$. The conclusion `3 - z 0 - z 1 - z 2 - z 3 + (z 0)*(z 1)*(z 2)*(z 3) ≠ 0` is exactly the claim that $3 - z_1 - z_2 - z_3 - z_4 + z_1 z_2 z_3 z_4 \neq 0$ (with 0-based indexing). No extra hypotheses or conclusions are added, and none are dropped. So the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_5 (A minus_A : Set ℝ) (hA : A.Nonempty)

### proofnet-153

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $k \geq 3, x, y \in \mathbb{R}^k, |x - y| = d > 0$, and $r > 0$. Prove that if $2r > d$, there are infinitely many $z \in \mathbb{R}^k$ such that $|z-x|=|z-y|=r$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_16a
  (n : ℕ)
  (d r : ℝ)
  (x y _z : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n)) -- R^n
  (h₁ : n ≥ 3)
  (h₂ : ‖x - y‖ = d)
  (h₃ : d > 0)
  (h₄ : r > 0)
  (h₅ : 2 * r > d)
  : Set.Infinite {z : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n) | ‖z - x‖ = r ∧ ‖z - y‖ = r}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature uses `EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n)` with `n ≥ 3` matching $\mathbb{R}^k$ with $k \geq 3$. The hypotheses $\|x-y\| = d$, $d > 0$, $r > 0$, and $2r > d$ all match the informal statement. The conclusion `Set.Infinite {z | ‖z-x‖ = r ∧ ‖z-y‖ = r}` correctly encodes "there are infinitely many z with |z-x|=|z-y|=r". The unused parameter `_z` is just an extra variable that doesn't affect the logical content (it's an arbitrary point in the space, freely chosen, not constrained). The formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-154

**Informal statement**

> If $k \geq 2$ and $\mathbf{x} \in R^{k}$, prove that there exists $\mathbf{y} \in R^{k}$ such that $\mathbf{y} \neq 0$ but $\mathbf{x} \cdot \mathbf{y}=0$

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_18a
  (n : ℕ)
  (h : n > 1)
  (x : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n)) -- R^n
  : ∃ (y : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n)), y ≠ 0 ∧ ⟪x, y⟫_ℝ = 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: for k ≥ 2 and any x ∈ R^k, there exists y ≠ 0 with x·y = 0. The Lean version uses n > 1 (i.e., n ≥ 2), takes x in EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n) which is R^n with the standard inner product, and concludes ∃ y ≠ 0 with ⟪x, y⟫_ℝ = 0. The inner product on EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n) is the standard dot product. The hypothesis, conclusion, types, and quantifier structure all match. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-156

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $E\subset\mathbb{R}^k$ is uncountable, and let $P$ be the set of condensation points of $E$. Prove that $P$ is perfect.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_2_27a (k : ℕ) (E P : Set (EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin k)))
  (hE : ¬ Set.Countable E)
  (hP : P = {x | ∀ U ∈ 𝓝 x, ¬ Set.Countable (U ∩ E)}) :
  Perfect P
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The ambient space EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin k) is ℝ^k. E is an arbitrary subset, with the hypothesis ¬Set.Countable E corresponding to "uncountable". P is defined as {x | ∀ U ∈ 𝓝 x, ¬Countable (U ∩ E)}, which is the standard definition of condensation points (every neighborhood of x meets E in an uncountable set). The conclusion `Perfect P` in Mathlib means P is closed and every point is an accumulation point, matching "P is perfect". The quantifier structure and types align with the informal statement.

### proofnet-157

**Informal statement**

> Prove that every closed set in a separable metric space is the union of a (possibly empty) perfect set and a set which is at most countable.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_2_28 (X : Type*) [MetricSpace X] [SeparableSpace X]
  (A : Set X) (hA : IsClosed A) :
  ∃ P₁ P₂ : Set X, A = P₁ ∪ P₂ ∧ Perfect P₁ ∧ Set.Countable P₂
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts exactly the Cantor-Bendixson decomposition: for any closed set A in a separable metric space, there exist sets P₁ and P₂ with A = P₁ ∪ P₂, P₁ perfect, and P₂ at most countable. Mathlib's `Perfect` predicate (closed + every point is an accumulation point) is vacuously satisfied by the empty set, matching the informal "possibly empty perfect set". `Set.Countable` includes finite and empty sets, matching "at most countable". The hypotheses (separable metric space, A closed) match exactly, and no extra structure (like completeness) is silently assumed or omitted.

### proofnet-159  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_3_3`

**Informal statement**

> If $s_{1}=\sqrt{2}$, and $s_{n+1}=\sqrt{2+\sqrt{s_{n}}} \quad(n=1,2,3, \ldots),$ prove that $\left\{s_{n}\right\}$ converges, and that $s_{n}<2$ for $n=1,2,3, \ldots$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_3
  : ∃ (x : ℝ), Tendsto f atTop (𝓝 x) ∧ ∀ n, f n < 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

Assuming `f` is defined elsewhere as the recursive sequence s_n with s_1 = √2 and s_{n+1} = √(2 + √s_n), the Lean statement asserts exactly the two claims of the informal problem: (1) the sequence converges, encoded as ∃ x : ℝ, Tendsto f atTop (𝓝 x), and (2) every term is strictly less than 2, encoded as ∀ n, f n < 2. Convergence to a real limit is the standard Lean encoding of "{s_n} converges". The universal quantifier `∀ n` covers all natural-number indices, matching "for n = 1, 2, 3, …" (any 0-indexing shift just reindexes the sequence and the bound still holds). No extra hypotheses are introduced and no claim is weakened, so the encoding is logically equivalent.

**Lean signature**

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_6a
: Tendsto (λ (n : ℕ) => (∑ i ∈ range n, g i)) atTop atTop
```

Assuming `g` is defined elsewhere as `g i = √(i+1) − √i` (standard ProofNet convention), the Lean statement asserts that the partial sums `∑ i < n, g i` tend to `+∞` as `n → ∞`, which is exactly the informal claim that `lim_{n→∞} ∑_{i<n} a_i = ∞`. theorem Rudin_exercise_3_22 (X : Type*) [MetricSpace X] [CompleteSpace X] [Nonempty X]

  ∃ x, ∀ n, x ∈ G n
```

### proofnet-164

**Informal statement**

> If $f$ is a continuous mapping of a metric space $X$ into a metric space $Y$, prove that $f(\overline{E}) \subset \overline{f(E)}$ for every set $E \subset X$. ($\overline{E}$ denotes the closure of $E$).

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_2a
  {α : Type} [MetricSpace α]
  {β : Type} [MetricSpace β]
  (f : α → β)
  (h₁ : Continuous f)
  : ∀ (x : Set α), f '' (closure x) ⊆ closure (f '' x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature states: for metric spaces α and β, a continuous function f, and every set x ⊆ α, f(closure x) ⊆ closure(f(x)). This matches the informal statement exactly: continuous map between metric spaces, universal quantification over subsets E ⊆ X, and the conclusion f(Ē) ⊆ closure(f(E)). The image notation `f '' x` correctly represents f(E), and `closure` matches the topological closure. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-165

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ and $g$ be continuous mappings of a metric space $X$ into a metric space $Y$, and let $E$ be a dense subset of $X$. Prove that $f(E)$ is dense in $f(X)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_4a
  {α : Type} [MetricSpace α]
  {β : Type} [MetricSpace β]
  (f : α → β)
  (s : Set α)
  (h₁ : Continuous f)
  (h₂ : Dense s)
  : f '' Set.univ ⊆ closure (f '' s)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

"f(E) is dense in f(X)" unfolds to f(X) ⊆ closure(f(E)), which is exactly what the Lean conclusion `f '' Set.univ ⊆ closure (f '' s)` states. The hypotheses match: continuous f, dense s, metric spaces α and β. The informal statement also mentions g, but only as setup for part (a)'s claim about f(E); since the conclusion only concerns f, omitting g is appropriate. No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-166  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_4_5a`

**Informal statement**

> If $f$ is a real continuous function defined on a closed set $E \subset \mathbb{R}$, prove that there exist continuous real functions $g$ on $\mathbb{R}$ such that $g(x)=f(x)$ for all $x \in E$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_5a
  (f : ℝ → ℝ)
  (E : Set ℝ)
  (h₁ : IsClosed E)
  (h₂ : ContinuousOn f E)
  : ∃ (g : ℝ → ℝ), Continuous g ∧ ∀ x ∈ E, f x = g x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks: given a continuous real function f on a closed set E ⊂ ℝ, show there exists a continuous extension g on ℝ agreeing with f on E. The Lean signature takes f : ℝ → ℝ with ContinuousOn f E, E closed, and asserts existence of a continuous g : ℝ → ℝ with f x = g x for all x ∈ E. ContinuousOn f E captures "f is continuous on E" (only the values on E matter for the conclusion, since g need only agree on E). The conclusion ∀ x ∈ E, f x = g x matches exactly. This is a faithful encoding of Rudin's Exercise 4.5(a).

### proofnet-167

**Informal statement**

> If $f$ is defined on $E$, the graph of $f$ is the set of points $(x, f(x))$, for $x \in E$. In particular, if $E$ is a set of real numbers, and $f$ is real-valued, the graph of $f$ is a subset of the plane. Suppose $E$ is compact, and prove that $f$ is continuous on $E$ if and only if its graph is compact.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_6
  (f : ℝ → ℝ)
  (E : Set ℝ)
  (G : Set (ℝ × ℝ))
  (h₁ : IsCompact E)
  (h₂ : G = {(x, f x) | x ∈ E})
  : ContinuousOn f E ↔ IsCompact G
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement defines E as a compact subset of ℝ, f as a real-valued function, and G as the graph {(x, f(x)) | x ∈ E}, then states ContinuousOn f E ↔ IsCompact G. This matches the informal statement: E is compact, f is continuous on E iff its graph is compact. The types (ℝ → ℝ, Set ℝ, Set (ℝ × ℝ)) correspond exactly to the informal setup of real numbers and the plane. The biconditional captures "if and only if" faithfully.

### proofnet-169

**Informal statement**

> A uniformly continuous function of a uniformly continuous function is uniformly continuous.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_12
  {α β γ : Type*} [UniformSpace α] [UniformSpace β] [UniformSpace γ]
  {f : α → β} {g : β → γ}
  (hf : UniformContinuous f) (hg : UniformContinuous g) :
  UniformContinuous (g ∘ f)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement says: for any uniform spaces α, β, γ, if f : α → β and g : β → γ are uniformly continuous, then g ∘ f is uniformly continuous. This is exactly the informal claim that the composition of two uniformly continuous functions is uniformly continuous. Uniform continuity in Mathlib's `UniformContinuous` for `UniformSpace` matches the standard notion (and reduces to the metric-space ε-δ definition that Rudin uses, since metric spaces are uniform spaces). No extra hypotheses (e.g. completeness, boundedness) are added, and no hypotheses are dropped. The quantification over the three spaces and the two functions correctly captures the universal nature of the informal statement.

### proofnet-170  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_4_19_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is a real function with domain $R^{1}$ which has the intermediate value property: if $f(a) < c < f(b)$, then $f(x)=c$ for some $x$ between $a$ and $b$. Suppose also, for every rational $r$, that the set of all $x$ with $f(x)=r$ is closed. Prove that $f$ is continuous.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_19_corrected
  {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hf : ∀ a b c, a < b →
      ((f a < c ∧ c < f b) ∨ (f b < c ∧ c < f a)) →
      ∃ x, a < x ∧ x < b ∧ f x = c)
  (hg : ∀ r : ℚ, IsClosed {x | f x = r}) : Continuous f
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the IVP hypothesis: for a < b with c strictly between f(a) and f(b) (in either order), there exists x strictly between a and b with f(x)=c. The informal statement only writes "f(a) < c < f(b)", but since a and b are just two points (with "between a and b" symmetric in a,b), the standard reading is symmetric in the two endpoints — the Lean encoding fixes a<b and allows either ordering of f(a), f(b), which is logically equivalent. The strict inequality a < x < b is fine because c ≠ f(a), f(b) forces x to be interior. The second hypothesis `∀ r : ℚ, IsClosed {x | f x = r}` matches "for every rational r, the level set is closed" (with the standard ℚ ↪ ℝ coercion). The conclusion `Continuous f` matches "f is continuous" on ℝ. No spurious hypotheses are added or omitted.

### proofnet-174

**Informal statement**

> Suppose (a) $f$ is continuous for $x \geq 0$, (b) $f^{\prime}(x)$ exists for $x>0$, (c) $f(0)=0$, (d) $f^{\prime}$ is monotonically increasing. Put $g(x)=\frac{f(x)}{x} \quad(x>0)$ and prove that $g$ is monotonically increasing.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_6
  {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hf1 : ContinuousOn f (Set.Ici 0))
  (hf2 : DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Set.Ioi 0))
  (hf3 : f 0 = 0)
  (hf4 : MonotoneOn (deriv f) (Set.Ioi 0)) :
  MonotoneOn (λ x => f x / x) (Set.Ioi 0)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature matches the informal statement closely: ContinuousOn f (Set.Ici 0) corresponds to (a) "f is continuous for x ≥ 0"; DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Set.Ioi 0) corresponds to (b) "f'(x) exists for x > 0"; hf3 f 0 = 0 matches (c); MonotoneOn (deriv f) (Set.Ioi 0) matches (d) "f' is monotonically increasing" (since on the open set Ioi 0 where f is differentiable, deriv f equals f'). The conclusion MonotoneOn (λ x => f x / x) (Set.Ioi 0) is exactly that g(x) = f(x)/x is monotone on x > 0. Quantifier structure, types (ℝ → ℝ), and domains all align with Rudin's intent.

### proofnet-175  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_5_15_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $a \in R^{1}, f$ is a twice-differentiable real function on $(a, \infty)$, and $M_{0}, M_{1}, M_{2}$ are the least upper bounds of $|f(x)|,\left|f^{\prime}(x)\right|,\left|f^{\prime \prime}(x)\right|$, respectively, on $(a, \infty)$. Prove that $M_{1}^{2} \leq 4 M_{0} M_{2} .$

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_15_corrected
    {f : ℝ → ℝ} {a M0 M1 M2 : ℝ}
    (hf' : DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Set.Ioi a))
    (hf'' : DifferentiableOn ℝ (deriv f) (Set.Ioi a))
    (hbdd0 : BddAbove {(|f x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)})
    (hbdd1 : BddAbove {(|deriv f x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)})
    (hbdd2 : BddAbove {(|deriv (deriv f) x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)})
    (hM0 : M0 = sSup {(|f x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)})
    (hM1 : M1 = sSup {(|deriv f x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)})
    (hM2 : M2 = sSup {(|deriv (deriv f) x|) | x ∈ (Set.Ioi a)}) :
    (M1 ^ 2) ≤ 4 * M0 * M2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all the relevant content of the informal statement: f is differentiable on (a, ∞) and its derivative is also differentiable there (encoding "twice-differentiable on (a, ∞)"); the sets {|f x| : x ∈ (a,∞)}, {|f' x| : x ∈ (a,∞)}, {|f'' x| : x ∈ (a,∞)} are assumed bounded above (so the sup is the actual least upper bound), and M0, M1, M2 are defined as those suprema. The conclusion M1^2 ≤ 4·M0·M2 matches exactly. The use of `deriv f` and `deriv (deriv f)` corresponds to f' and f'' under the given differentiability hypotheses. No extra hypotheses (continuity beyond what twice-differentiability already provides, finiteness, etc.) are silently added, and none are dropped.

### proofnet-176  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_1_13a_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $f$ is holomorphic in an open set $\Omega$. Prove that if $\text{Re}(f)$ is constant, then $f$ is constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_13a_corrected {f : ℂ → ℂ} (Ω : Set ℂ) (a b : Ω) (hΩ : IsOpen Ω)
    (hconn : IsPreconnected Ω) (hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f Ω)
    (hc : ∃ c : ℝ, ∀ z ∈ Ω, (f z).re = c) : f a = f b
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes "f is constant" via universally-quantified arbitrary points a, b : Ω with conclusion f a = f b, which is equivalent to f being constant on Ω. The hypotheses are: Ω open, Ω preconnected, f differentiable on Ω (the Mathlib analogue of holomorphic on Ω for ℂ → ℂ), and Re(f) constant on Ω. The informal statement (Shakarchi 1.13a) talks about an open set Ω, but the conclusion "f is constant" is only true on a connected component — adding IsPreconnected is the standard "regions in Shakarchi" reading, which the theorem name "_corrected" reflects. Under that conventional reading, the Lean version matches the informal claim exactly. The DifferentiableOn ℂ formulation correctly captures complex holomorphy.

### proofnet-177  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_1_13c_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $f$ is holomorphic in an open set $\Omega$. Prove that if $|f|$ is constant, then $f$ is constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_13c_corrected {f : ℂ → ℂ} {Ω : Set ℂ} (a b : Ω)
    (hΩ : IsOpen Ω) (hconn : IsPreconnected Ω)
    (hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f Ω) (hc : ∃ c : ℝ, ∀ z ∈ Ω, ‖f z‖ = c) :
    f a = f b
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is Stein-Shakarchi Exercise 1.13(c), which in the book is set in a "region" (a connected open set) — connectedness is genuinely required, since on a disconnected open set |f| constant only forces f constant on each component. The Lean version makes this implicit hypothesis explicit via IsPreconnected (the "_corrected" suffix signals this fix), and otherwise matches: f : ℂ → ℂ, Ω open, DifferentiableOn ℂ f Ω is the standard Mathlib formulation of holomorphic on Ω, and ∃ c : ℝ, ∀ z ∈ Ω, ‖f z‖ = c precisely encodes "|f| is constant on Ω." The conclusion f a = f b for arbitrary a, b ∈ Ω (universally quantified through the section variables) is logically equivalent to "f is constant on Ω." So the Lean statement asserts the same mathematical content as the informal claim under its intended reading.

### proofnet-178

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the power series $\sum z^n/n^2$ converges at every point of the unit circle.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_19b (z : ℂ) (hz : ‖z‖ = 1) (s : ℕ → ℂ)
    (h : s = (λ n => ∑ i ∈ (range n), z ^ (i + 1) / (i + 1) ^ 2)) :
    ∃ y, Tendsto s atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement fixes an arbitrary z ∈ ℂ with ‖z‖ = 1 (i.e., on the unit circle) and asserts that the sequence of partial sums s_n = ∑_{i=0}^{n-1} z^(i+1)/(i+1)^2 = ∑_{k=1}^{n} z^k/k^2 tends to some limit y in ℂ. This is exactly the assertion that the power series ∑ z^n/n^2 converges at z. Universally quantifying over z on the unit circle (via the hypothesis hz) covers every point of the unit circle. The reindexing (i+1) correctly starts the sum at n=1, matching the informal series. No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-179  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_1_26_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is continuous in a region $\Omega$. Prove that any two primitives of $f$ (if they exist) differ by a constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_26_corrected :
    ∀ (f F₁ F₂ : ℂ → ℂ) (Ω : Set ℂ),
      Nonempty Ω → IsOpen Ω → IsConnected Ω →
      ContinuousOn f Ω → DifferentiableOn ℂ F₁ Ω → DifferentiableOn ℂ F₂ Ω →
      (∀ x ∈ Ω, deriv F₁ x = f x) → (∀ x ∈ Ω, deriv F₂ x = f x) →
      ∃ c : ℂ, ∀ x ∈ Ω, F₁ x = F₂ x + c
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

A "region" in complex analysis standardly means a nonempty open connected subset of ℂ, which is exactly captured by IsOpen Ω ∧ IsConnected Ω (and the redundant Nonempty Ω, since IsConnected in Mathlib already implies nonemptiness). The hypothesis that f is continuous on Ω is encoded as ContinuousOn f Ω. "Primitives of f" are functions F₁, F₂ that are differentiable on Ω with derivative equal to f, which the Lean statement spells out with DifferentiableOn ℂ F_i Ω together with deriv F_i x = f x for x ∈ Ω. The conclusion ∃ c, ∀ x ∈ Ω, F₁ x = F₂ x + c is exactly "F₁ and F₂ differ by a constant on Ω". No extra hypotheses (like analyticity) are imposed and none of the needed ones are dropped, so the Lean theorem matches the informal claim.

### proofnet-181  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_3_3`

**Informal statement**

> Show that $ \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{\cos x}{x^2 + a^2} dx = \pi \frac{e^{-a}}{a}$ for $a > 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_3_3 (a : ℝ) (ha : 0 < a) :
    Tendsto (λ y => ∫ x in -y..y, Real.cos x / (x ^ 2 + a ^ 2))
    atTop (𝓝 (Real.pi * (Real.exp (-a) / a)))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks for the value of the improper integral ∫_{-∞}^{∞} cos(x)/(x²+a²) dx for a > 0. The Lean signature expresses this as the limit, as y → ∞, of the symmetric interval integrals ∫_{-y}^{y} cos(x)/(x²+a²) dx, which is exactly the standard meaning of ∫_{-∞}^{∞} written in the informal statement. The hypothesis a > 0 matches, and the right-hand side π·e^{-a}/a matches. Since the integrand is absolutely integrable (bounded by 1/(x²+a²)), the symmetric limit also coincides with the Lebesgue integral, so there is no semantic drift between principal-value and full improper integral. The Lean encoding is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-182

**Informal statement**

> Show that $\int_0^1 \log(\sin \pi x) dx = - \log 2$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_3_9 : ∫ x in (0 : ℝ)..(1 : ℝ), Real.log (Real.sin (Real.pi * x)) = - Real.log 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement uses `intervalIntegral` from 0 to 1 of `Real.log (Real.sin (Real.pi * x))`, which directly matches the informal integral $\int_0^1 \log(\sin \pi x)\,dx$. The right-hand side `- Real.log 2` matches $-\log 2$. The types are all real, the bounds are correct, and the integrand is the same composition. This is a faithful encoding.

### proofnet-183

**Informal statement**

> Show that there is no holomorphic function $f$ in the unit disc $D$ that extends continuously to $\partial D$ such that $f(z) = 1/z$ for $z \in \partial D$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_3_22 (D : Set ℂ) (hD : D = ball 0 1) (f : ℂ → ℂ)
    (hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f D) (hfc : ContinuousOn f (closure D)) :
    ¬ ∀ z ∈ (sphere (0 : ℂ) 1), f z = 1 / z
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says no holomorphic function on the open unit disc, continuous on the closed disc, satisfies f(z)=1/z on the boundary. The Lean signature takes an arbitrary f that is differentiable on D=ball 0 1 and continuous on closure D, and concludes ¬ ∀ z ∈ sphere 0 1, f z = 1/z, i.e., f cannot equal 1/z on the entire unit circle. This is logically equivalent to the non-existence claim: for all such f, the boundary identity fails. The hypotheses (DifferentiableOn = holomorphic on the open disc, ContinuousOn on closure = continuous extension to ∂D) and conclusion (negation of the boundary equation on the unit circle) match the informal statement exactly.

### proofnet-184

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the products $a b$ and $b a$ are conjugate elements in a group.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_2_3_2 {G : Type*} [Group G] (a b : G) :
  ∃ g : G, b* a = g * a * b * g⁻¹
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says ab and ba are conjugate in a group, i.e., there exists g with ba = g(ab)g⁻¹. The Lean signature asserts ∃ g : G, b*a = g * a * b * g⁻¹, which is exactly the conjugacy of ab and ba (with g*(a*b)*g⁻¹ written without explicit parentheses). The quantifier structure (universal over a, b in an arbitrary group, existential over g) and the conclusion match precisely. No extra hypotheses (e.g., finiteness, abelianness) are imposed, and no hypotheses are dropped.

### proofnet-185

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the center of the product of two groups is the product of their centers.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_2_8_6 {G H : Type*} [Group G] [Group H] :
  Nonempty (center (G × H) ≃* (center G) × (center H))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim "Z(G × H) = Z(G) × Z(H)" cannot be expressed as literal equality since Z(G × H) lives inside G × H while Z(G) × Z(H) is a product of subgroups; the standard faithful encoding is a group isomorphism, which is exactly `center (G × H) ≃* center G × center H`. Wrapping in `Nonempty` just asserts existence of such an iso (i.e. that the two groups are isomorphic), which matches the mathematical content of the statement. The hypotheses ([Group G], [Group H]) are exactly what the informal statement requires—no extra finiteness/commutativity/etc. assumptions, and G, H are universally quantified. Hence the Lean theorem captures the same claim, neither weaker nor stronger.

### proofnet-186

**Informal statement**

> Prove that every homomorphism of fields is injective.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_3_2_7 {F : Type*} [Field F] {G : Type*} [Field G]
  (φ : F →+* G) : Injective φ
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says every field homomorphism is injective. The Lean signature takes two fields F and G and a ring homomorphism φ : F →+* G, and concludes Injective φ. A ring homomorphism between fields is exactly what is meant by a "homomorphism of fields" (it preserves both addition and multiplication, and the unit). The universal quantification over F, G, and φ matches the informal "every". This is the standard faithful encoding.

### proofnet-187

**Informal statement**

> Let $V$ be a vector space over an infinite field $F$. Prove that $V$ is not the union of finitely many proper subspaces.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_3_7_2 {K V : Type*} [Field K] [Infinite K] [AddCommGroup V]
  [Module K V] {ι : Type*} [Fintype ι] (γ : ι → Submodule K V)
  (h : ∀ i : ι, γ i ≠ ⊤) :
  (⋃ (i : ι), (γ i : Set V)) ≠ ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement assumes K is an infinite field, V a K-module (= K-vector space since K is a field), and a finite indexed family γ : ι → Submodule K V with each γ i a proper submodule (γ i ≠ ⊤). It concludes that the set-theoretic union ⋃ i, (γ i : Set V) is not ⊤, i.e. not equal to Set.univ, which is exactly the statement that the union does not cover V. Finiteness is captured via [Fintype ι], properness via γ i ≠ ⊤, and infiniteness of the field via [Infinite K]. The quantifier structure (universal over the family and the field) and the conclusion match the informal claim precisely.

### proofnet-188

**Informal statement**

> Prove that no group of order $p q$, where $p$ and $q$ are prime, is simple.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_6_4_2 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G] {p q : ℕ}
  (hp : Nat.Prime p) (hq : Nat.Prime q) (hG : card G = p*q) :
  IsSimpleGroup G → false
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that any group of order pq (p, q prime) fails to be simple. The Lean signature fixes a finite group G with `Fintype.card G = p*q` for primes p, q, and concludes `IsSimpleGroup G → False` (interpreting `false` charitably as the proposition `False`), i.e. G is not simple. This matches the informal statement: universally quantifying over G via `{G : Type*}` corresponds to "no group", the hypotheses on p and q being prime are present, and the order condition is exactly `card G = p*q`. The `Fintype` instance is harmless because a group of order pq is finite by definition. Mathlib's `IsSimpleGroup` requires nontriviality, but pq ≥ 4 makes this automatic, so no genuine extra hypothesis is added. Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-191

**Informal statement**

> Let $I, J$ be ideals of a ring $R$ such that $I+J=R$. Prove that $I J=I \cap J$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_10_4_7a {R : Type*} [CommRing R]
  (I J : Ideal R) (hIJ : I + J = ⊤) : I * J = I ⊓ J
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes a commutative ring R (Artin's "ring" in this exercise context is commutative), two ideals I and J, the hypothesis I + J = ⊤ (which is the Mathlib encoding of I + J = R), and concludes I * J = I ⊓ J, which is exactly the product equals intersection statement IJ = I ∩ J. The quantifier structure (∀ I J, hypothesis → conclusion) matches the informal "let I, J ... such that ... prove". No extra hypotheses are added beyond commutativity, which is standard in this context, and no hypotheses are dropped. This is a faithful formalization.

theorem Artin_exercise_11_4_6a {F : Type*} [Field F] [Fintype F] (hF : card F = 2) :
