### proofnet-195  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Artin_exercise_11_4_6c`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $x^3 - 9$ is irreducible in $\mathbb{F}_{31}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_11_4_6c : Irreducible (X^3 - 9 : Polynomial (ZMod 31))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show that $x^3 - 9$ is irreducible in $\mathbb{F}_{31}[x]$ (the polynomial ring over the field with 31 elements). The Lean signature states `Irreducible (X^3 - 9 : Polynomial (ZMod 31))`, where `ZMod 31` is the field $\mathbb{F}_{31}$ (since 31 is prime), `Polynomial (ZMod 31)` is the polynomial ring, and `Irreducible` is the standard Mathlib notion of irreducibility in a commutative monoid/ring. The polynomial `X^3 - 9` matches $x^3 - 9$, with `9` interpreted in `ZMod 31`. This is a direct and equivalent formalization.

### proofnet-196

**Informal statement**

> Prove that there are infinitely many primes congruent to $-1$ (modulo $4$).

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_11_13_3 (N : ℕ):
  ∃ p ≥ N, Nat.Prime p ∧ p + 1 ≡ 0 [MOD 4]
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim asks for infinitely many primes p with p ≡ -1 (mod 4). The Lean statement says: for every natural number N, there exists p ≥ N such that p is prime and p+1 ≡ 0 (mod 4). The condition p+1 ≡ 0 (mod 4) is exactly p ≡ -1 (mod 4) (equivalently p ≡ 3 (mod 4)). The "for all N, exists p ≥ N" formulation is the standard Lean/Mathlib way of stating that there are infinitely many such primes, and it is logically equivalent to the informal claim. No extra hypotheses are imposed and nothing is dropped.

### proofnet-197

**Informal statement**

> Let $K$ be a finite field. Prove that the product of the nonzero elements of $K$ is $-1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_13_6_10 {K : Type*} [Field K] [Fintype Kˣ] :
  (∏ x : Kˣ,  x) = -1
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

In a field, the nonzero elements are exactly the units, so Kˣ corresponds bijectively to K \ {0}. Assuming [Fintype Kˣ] is equivalent to assuming K is finite (since K = Kˣ ∪ {0}). The product ∏ x : Kˣ, x lives in Kˣ and equals -1 (which is a unit in any field, including characteristic 2 where -1 = 1); via the injective monoid embedding Kˣ ↪ K this is equivalent to saying the product of the nonzero elements of K is -1. So the Lean statement matches the informal claim exactly.

### proofnet-198

**Informal statement**

> Show that $\frac{-1 + \sqrt{3}i}{2}$ is a cube root of 1 (meaning that its cube equals 1).

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_1_2 :
  (⟨-1/2, Real.sqrt 3 / 2⟩ : ℂ) ^ 3 = 1
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean expression ⟨-1/2, Real.sqrt 3 / 2⟩ : ℂ constructs the complex number -1/2 + (√3/2)i, which equals (-1 + √3·i)/2. The conclusion `^ 3 = 1` exactly states that its cube equals 1, matching the informal claim that this number is a cube root of 1. No extra hypotheses are introduced and no part of the claim is weakened or strengthened.

### proofnet-199

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $a \in \mathbf{F}$, $v \in V$, and $av = 0$, then $a = 0$ or $v = 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_1_4 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] (v : V) (a : F): a • v = 0 → a = 0 ∨ v = 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: for a scalar a in field F and vector v in V, if a·v = 0 then a = 0 or v = 0. The Lean signature quantifies over a field F, an additive commutative group V with an F-module structure, takes v : V and a : F, and concludes a • v = 0 → a = 0 ∨ v = 0. This matches the standard reading of the Axler exercise (V is a vector space over F, encoded as Module F V over a Field F and AddCommGroup V). The quantifier structure (∀ a, ∀ v) and the conclusion (disjunction) match exactly. No extra hypotheses like Nontrivial or finite-dimensionality are imposed, and none are needed. The encoding is faithful.

### proofnet-200

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the union of two subspaces of $V$ is a subspace of $V$ if and only if one of the subspaces is contained in the other.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_1_9 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] (U W : Submodule F V):
  (∃ U' : Submodule F V, U'.carrier = ↑U ∪ ↑W) ↔ (U ≤ W ∨ W ≤ U)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim states: U ∪ W is a subspace ⟺ U ⊆ W ∨ W ⊆ U. The Lean statement encodes "U ∪ W is a subspace" as "there exists a submodule U' whose carrier equals U ∪ W". This is logically equivalent to U ∪ W being a subspace: if such U' exists, then U ∪ W is the carrier of a submodule (hence a subspace), and conversely if U ∪ W is a subspace one can take U' to be that submodule. The right-hand side U ≤ W ∨ W ≤ U is exactly "one of the subspaces is contained in the other". Hypotheses (V an F-vector space) match standard reading. So the encoding is faithful.

### proofnet-201

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $V$ is finite dimensional and that $T \in \mathcal{L}(V, W)$. Prove that there exists a subspace $U$ of $V$ such that $U \cap \operatorname{null} T=\{0\}$ and range $T=\{T u: u \in U\}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_3_8 {F V W : Type*} [AddCommGroup V]
  [AddCommGroup W] [Field F] [Module F V] [FiniteDimensional F V] [Module F W]
  (L : V →ₗ[F] W) :
  ∃ U : Submodule F V, U ⊓ (ker L) = ⊥ ∧
  (range L = range (domRestrict L U))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement assumes V is a finite-dimensional module over a field F and L is a linear map V →ₗ[F] W, matching the informal hypothesis that V is finite-dimensional and T ∈ L(V,W). The conclusion asserts existence of a submodule U with U ⊓ ker L = ⊥, which is exactly U ∩ null T = {0}, and range L = range (domRestrict L U), where range (domRestrict L U) = {L u : u ∈ U}, exactly matching range T = {Tu : u ∈ U}. The quantifier structure and types align with the standard Axler reading. No extra or missing hypotheses; this is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-203  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_5_11_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $S, T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$. Prove that $S T$ and $T S$ have the same eigenvalues.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Axler_exercise_5_11_corrected {F V : Type*} [Field F] [AddCommGroup V]
    [Module F V] [FiniteDimensional F V] (S T : End F V) :
    (S * T).Eigenvalues = (T * S).Eigenvalues
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement (Axler 5.11) asks to prove that for S, T ∈ L(V) on a finite-dimensional vector space V, the operators ST and TS have the same set of eigenvalues. The Lean signature uses `End F V` (which is L(V)), assumes `FiniteDimensional F V` (matching Axler's standing convention), and asserts `(S * T).Eigenvalues = (T * S).Eigenvalues`. In Mathlib, `Module.End.Eigenvalues f` collects exactly the eigenvalues of f, so equality of these objects is logically equivalent to "ST and TS have the same eigenvalues." Generalizing from Axler's ℝ/ℂ to an arbitrary field is the standard formalization choice and does not change the mathematical content of the exercise. Hypotheses, conclusion, and quantifier structure all line up.

### proofnet-204  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_5_13_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is such that every subspace of $V$ with dimension $\operatorname{dim} V-1$ is invariant under $T$. Prove that $T$ is a scalar multiple of the identity operator.

**Lean signature**

```lean


theorem Axler_exercise_5_13_corrected {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
    [Module F V] [FiniteDimensional F V] {T : End F V}
    (hS : ∀ U : Submodule F V, finrank F U = finrank F V - 1 →
      Submodule.map T U ≤ U) : ∃ c : F, T = c • LinearMap.id
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal statement accurately: V is a finite-dimensional vector space over a field F, T is an endomorphism of V, and the hypothesis quantifies over all submodules U with finrank U = finrank V - 1 and asserts T-invariance via `Submodule.map T U ≤ U`, which is the standard "T(U) ⊆ U" definition of invariant subspace. The conclusion `∃ c : F, T = c • LinearMap.id` is exactly "T is a scalar multiple of the identity." Edge cases from natural-number subtraction (dim V = 0 or 1) do not break faithfulness: in both cases the conclusion holds trivially and matches the informal claim. No extra hypotheses (like algebraic closure or characteristic) are added, and none are missing.

### proofnet-205

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $V$ is a real vector space and $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ has no eigenvalues. Prove that every subspace of $V$ invariant under $T$ has even dimension.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_5_24 {V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V]
  [Module ℝ V] [FiniteDimensional ℝ V] {T : End ℝ V}
  (hT : ∀ c : ℝ, eigenspace T c = ⊥) {U : Submodule ℝ V}
  (hU : Submodule.map T U = U) : Even (finrank ℝ U)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is from Axler, where V is implicitly finite-dimensional (the book's standing assumption when speaking of L(V) and eigenvalues), so the `FiniteDimensional ℝ V` hypothesis matches. "T has no eigenvalues" over ℝ is correctly encoded as `∀ c : ℝ, eigenspace T c = ⊥`. The conclusion `Even (finrank ℝ U)` is the right rendering of "even dimension." The only subtle point is that T-invariance is encoded as `Submodule.map T U = U` (equality) rather than ⊆; however, since T has no eigenvalue 0 it is injective, and on a finite-dimensional invariant subspace an injective endomorphism is surjective, so T(U) ⊆ U is equivalent to T(U) = U under these hypotheses. Hence the Lean statement is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-206

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\left(\sum_{j=1}^{n} a_{j} b_{j}\right)^{2} \leq\left(\sum_{j=1}^{n} j a_{j}{ }^{2}\right)\left(\sum_{j=1}^{n} \frac{b_{j}{ }^{2}}{j}\right)$ for all real numbers $a_{1}, \ldots, a_{n}$ and $b_{1}, \ldots, b_{n}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_6_3 {n : ℕ} (a b : Fin n → ℝ) :
  (∑ i, a i * b i) ^ 2 ≤ (∑ i : Fin n, (i + 1) * a i ^ 2) * (∑ i, b i ^ 2 / (i + 1))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement indexes over `Fin n` (i.e. i = 0, …, n−1) and uses `i + 1` in positions where the expected type is ℝ (since it is multiplied by `a i ^ 2 : ℝ` or used as a divisor of a real). With ℝ as the expected type, `i` is coerced to ℝ first and the addition is real addition (no Fin wraparound), so `i + 1` ranges over 1, 2, …, n, exactly matching the informal index j. The three sums and the inequality therefore correspond exactly to the informal Cauchy–Schwarz-type inequality. The n = 0 case yields 0 ≤ 0, which is vacuously consistent with the informal claim. No extra or missing hypotheses are introduced.

### proofnet-207

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $\left(e_{1}, \ldots, e_{m}\right)$ is an orthonormal list of vectors in $V$. Let $v \in V$. Prove that $\|v\|^{2}=\left|\left\langle v, e_{1}\right\rangle\right|^{2}+\cdots+\left|\left\langle v, e_{m}\right\rangle\right|^{2}$ if and only if $v \in \operatorname{span}\left(e_{1}, \ldots, e_{m}\right)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_6_13 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [RCLike F] [InnerProductSpace F V] {n : ℕ}
  {e : Fin n → V} (he : Orthonormal F e) (v : V) :
  ‖v‖^2 = ∑ i : Fin n, ‖⟪v, e i⟫_F‖^2 ↔ v ∈ Submodule.span F (e '' Set.univ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement matches the informal claim. The hypothesis `Orthonormal F e` for `e : Fin n → V` corresponds to "(e_1, ..., e_m) is an orthonormal list", v is an arbitrary vector, and the conclusion is the desired iff. On the LHS of the iff, `∑ i : Fin n, ‖⟪v, e i⟫_F‖^2` uses the norm on the scalar field F (with `RCLike F`), which equals |⟨v, e_i⟩|, so this is exactly Σ |⟨v, e_i⟩|². On the RHS, `Submodule.span F (e '' Set.univ)` is the span of the set {e i | i : Fin n}, i.e. span(e_1, ..., e_m). The setup over `RCLike F` correctly covers both real and complex inner product spaces as in Axler.

### proofnet-208

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $\operatorname{dim} V \geq 2$, then the set of normal operators on $V$ is not a subspace of $\mathcal{L}(V)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_7_5 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [RCLike F] [InnerProductSpace F V]
  [FiniteDimensional F V] (hV : finrank F V ≥ 2) :
  ∀ U : Submodule F (End F V), U.carrier ≠
  {T | T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that when dim V ≥ 2, the set of normal operators on V is not a subspace of L(V). The Lean version states that for every submodule U of End F V, the carrier of U is not equal to {T | T*adjoint T = adjoint T*T}, which is precisely the set of normal operators. Saying "no submodule has this set as its underlying set" is logically equivalent to "this set is not a subspace." The ambient setting (finite-dimensional inner product space over RCLike F, i.e., ℝ or ℂ) matches Axler's standard setup, and the hypothesis finrank F V ≥ 2 matches dim V ≥ 2. No hypotheses are silently added or dropped, and the conclusion is a direct rendering.

### proofnet-209

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a normal operator on a complex inner-product space is self-adjoint if and only if all its eigenvalues are real.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_7_9 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [InnerProductSpace ℂ V]
  [FiniteDimensional ℂ V] (T : End ℂ V)
  (hT : T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T) :
  IsSelfAdjoint T ↔ ∀ e : T.Eigenvalues, (e : ℂ).im = 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes Axler's Exercise 7.9: T is a normal endomorphism (hT : T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T) on a complex inner product space, and the conclusion is the biconditional that T is self-adjoint iff every eigenvalue has zero imaginary part (i.e., is real). The FiniteDimensional ℂ V hypothesis matches Axler's standing assumption for this exercise (without it, "eigenvalue" vs "spectrum" would diverge and the statement would be more subtle). T.Eigenvalues ranges over all eigenvalues, so ∀ e, (e:ℂ).im = 0 correctly expresses "all eigenvalues are real." Both directions of the iff are present, the normality hypothesis is included, and the underlying field is ℂ. This matches the informal claim faithfully.

### proofnet-210

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $V$ is a complex inner-product space. Prove that every normal operator on $V$ has a square root. (An operator $S \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ is called a square root of $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$ if $S^{2}=T$.)

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_7_11 {V : Type*} [NormedAddCommGroup V] [InnerProductSpace ℂ V]
  [FiniteDimensional ℂ V] {T : End ℂ V} (hT : T*adjoint T = adjoint T*T) :
  ∃ (S : End ℂ V), S ^ 2 = T
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is Axler Exercise 7.11, which in Axler's book takes place in a finite-dimensional complex inner-product space (this is the standing assumption throughout the relevant chapter). The Lean signature accordingly assumes `FiniteDimensional ℂ V`, which matches Axler's convention. The hypothesis `T * adjoint T = adjoint T * T` is exactly the definition of a normal operator (TT* = T*T). The conclusion `∃ S, S^2 = T` matches the definition of a square root given in the problem. Quantifier structure (∃ S) and types match. So the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-211

**Informal statement**

> Prove the the operation $\star$ on $\mathbb{Z}$ defined by $a\star b=a-b$ is not commutative.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_2a : ∃ a b : ℤ, a - b ≠ b - a
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

Non-commutativity of the operation a⋆b = a−b on ℤ means ¬∀ a b ∈ ℤ, a−b = b−a, which is classically equivalent to ∃ a b ∈ ℤ, a−b ≠ b−a. The Lean statement asserts exactly this existential, with the correct type ℤ and the correct operation. No extra hypotheses are added and none are missing, so the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-212

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the multiplication of residue class $\mathbb{Z}/n\mathbb{Z}$ is associative.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_4 (n : ℕ) (a b c : ZMod n) :
  (a * b) * c = a * (b * c)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that multiplication in ℤ/nℤ is associative. The Lean signature universally quantifies over n : ℕ and over a, b, c : ZMod n, then asserts (a * b) * c = a * (b * c), which is precisely the associativity law for multiplication on ZMod n. ZMod n is Mathlib's standard formalization of ℤ/nℤ (with ZMod 0 = ℤ, which is harmless since ℤ multiplication is also associative and is often considered the n=0 case). No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped. The statement matches the informal claim exactly.

### proofnet-213

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $(a_1a_2\dots a_n)^{-1} = a_n^{-1}a_{n-1}^{-1}\dots a_1^{-1}$ for all $a_1, a_2, \dots, a_n\in G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_15 {G : Type*} [Group G] (as : List G) :
  as.prod⁻¹ = (as.reverse.map (λ x => x⁻¹)).prod
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts that for any list `as` of elements in a group `G`, the inverse of the product of the list equals the product of the reversed list with each element inverted. This is exactly the informal claim that (a_1 a_2 ... a_n)^{-1} = a_n^{-1} a_{n-1}^{-1} ... a_1^{-1}. The use of `List G` correctly captures the finite sequence indexed by 1..n; quantification over `as : List G` covers all n and all choices of a_i. The empty list case (n = 0) gives 1^{-1} = 1, which is consistent with the convention. No extra hypotheses (like commutativity or finiteness) are added or dropped.

### proofnet-214

**Informal statement**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_17 {G : Type*} [Group G] {x : G} {n : ℕ+}

  x⁻¹ = x ^ (n - 1 : ℕ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The informal statement assumes x has finite order n (a positive integer) and concludes x⁻¹ = x^{n−1}. Since n ≥ 1, the natural-number subtraction n - 1 equals the intended n−1, and the exponent is interpreted in the group via `^`. The hypotheses, conclusion, and quantifier structure (universal over G a group and x ∈ G) match the informal claim, so this is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-217

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $A \times B$ is an abelian group if and only if both $A$ and $B$ are abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_1_29 {A B : Type*} [Group A] [Group B] :
  (∀ x y : A × B, x * y = y * x) ↔ (∀ x y : A, x * y = y * x) ∧
  (∀ x y : B, x * y = y * x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement assumes A and B are groups (matching the informal premise that A, B, and A×B are groups) and states the iff between commutativity of the product group A × B and commutativity of both A and B. Saying a group is abelian is exactly the condition `∀ x y, x*y = y*x`, so spelling it out rather than using `IsCommutative`/`CommGroup` is logically equivalent. The product group structure on A × B is the standard componentwise one provided by the `Group` instances. Both directions of the iff are captured. Hence the Lean version matches the informal claim exactly.

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_3_8 : Infinite (Equiv.Perm ℕ)

### proofnet-219

**Informal statement**

> Let $A$ and $B$ be groups. Prove that $A \times B \cong B \times A$.

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_6_11 {A B : Type*} [Group A] [Group B] :

```

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove that for arbitrary groups A and B, the direct product A × B is isomorphic to B × A. ### proofnet-220  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_6_23_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a finite group which possesses an automorphism $\sigma$ such that $\sigma(g)=g$ if and only if $g=1$. If $\sigma^{2}$ is the identity map from $G$ to $G$, prove that $G$ is abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_1_6_23_corrected {G : Type*} [Group G] [Finite G]
    (σ : MulAut G) (hs : ∀ g : G, σ g = g ↔ g = 1)
    (hs2 : ∀ g : G, σ (σ g) = g) :
    ∀ x y : G, x * y = y * x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all of the informal hypotheses: G is a group with [Finite G], σ : MulAut G is a group automorphism, hs encodes "σ(g)=g iff g=1", and hs2 says σ∘σ = id pointwise (equivalent to σ² being the identity map). The conclusion "∀ x y : G, x * y = y * x" is exactly the statement that G is abelian. No extra hypotheses (like commutativity, decidability, or specific cardinality) are smuggled in, and none of the informal hypotheses are dropped. Hence the Lean theorem is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-221  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_1_13_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $H$ be a subgroup of the additive group of rational numbers with the property that $1 / x \in H$ for every nonzero element $x$ of $H$. Prove that $H=0$ or $\mathbb{Q}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_1_13_corrected (H : AddSubgroup ℚ)
    (hH : ∀ ⦃x : ℚ⦄, x ∈ H → x ≠ 0 → (1 / x) ∈ H) :
    H = ⊥ ∨ H = ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes "H is an additive subgroup of ℚ" via `AddSubgroup ℚ`, matching the informal hypothesis exactly. The closure condition `∀ x ∈ H, x ≠ 0 → 1/x ∈ H` precisely mirrors "1/x ∈ H for every nonzero x ∈ H". The conclusion `H = ⊥ ∨ H = ⊤` corresponds to "H is the trivial subgroup {0} or all of ℚ", which matches "H = 0 or H = ℚ". No extra hypotheses (finiteness, decidability, etc.) are imposed and none are dropped, so the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-222

**Informal statement**

> A subgroup $M$ of a group $G$ is called a maximal subgroup if $M \neq G$ and the only subgroups of $G$ which contain $M$ are $M$ and $G$. Prove that if $H$ is a proper subgroup of the finite group $G$ then there is a maximal subgroup of $G$ containing $H$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_16a {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G] {H : Subgroup G}
  (hH : H ≠ ⊤) :
  ∃ M : Subgroup G, M ≠ ⊤ ∧
  (∀ K : Subgroup G, M ≤ K → K = M ∨ K = ⊤) ∧
  H ≤ M
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal statement faithfully. `[Fintype G]` corresponds to "finite group G", `H ≠ ⊤` corresponds to "H is a proper subgroup", and the conclusion asserts the existence of M which is proper (M ≠ ⊤), maximal (any K containing M equals M or ⊤), and contains H (H ≤ M). The maximality condition matches the informal definition exactly: the only subgroups containing M are M and G itself. Quantifier structure and types align.

### proofnet-223  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_16c`

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $G=\langle x\rangle$ is a cyclic group of order $n \geq 1$ then a subgroup $H$ is maximal if and only $H=\left\langle x^{p}\right\rangle$ for some prime $p$ dividing $n$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_2_4_16c {n : ℕ+} {G : Type*} [Group G] {x : G}
  (hx : orderOf x = n) (hG : closure ({x} : Set G) = ⊤) (H : Subgroup G) :
  (∃ p : ℕ, Prime p ∧ p ∣ n ∧ H = Subgroup.closure {x ^ p}) ↔
  (H ≠ ⊤ ∧ ∀ K : Subgroup G, H ≤ K → K = H ∨ K = ⊤)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The hypotheses (n : ℕ+ for n ≥ 1, orderOf x = n, and closure {x} = ⊤) correctly express that G is cyclic of order n generated by x. The left side of the iff matches "H = ⟨x^p⟩ for some prime p dividing n". The right side encodes maximality: H is a proper subgroup (H ≠ ⊤) and every subgroup K containing H equals H or ⊤, which is the standard definition of a maximal subgroup. The quantifier structure and types match. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-224

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $H$ and $K$ are normal subgroups of a group $G$ then their intersection $H \cap K$ is also a normal subgroup of $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_1_22a (G : Type*) [Group G] (H K : Subgroup G)
  [Normal H] [Normal K] :
  Normal (H ⊓ K)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement quantifies over an arbitrary group G, takes two subgroups H and K both assumed Normal, and concludes that their lattice meet H ⊓ K (which is the intersection of subgroups in Mathlib) is Normal. This matches the informal claim exactly: same hypotheses (H, K normal in G), same conclusion (H ∩ K normal in G), with no extra assumptions like finiteness or commutativity. The encoding of "intersection" as `H ⊓ K` is definitionally the set-theoretic intersection of subgroups.

### proofnet-225

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $H$ and $K$ are finite subgroups of $G$ whose orders are relatively prime then $H \cap K=1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_2_8 {G : Type*} [Group G] (H K : Subgroup G)
  [Fintype H] [Fintype K]
  (hHK : Nat.Coprime (card H) (card K)) :
  H ⊓ K = ⊥
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes two subgroups H, K of an arbitrary group G, equips each with a Fintype instance (matching "finite subgroups"), assumes Nat.Coprime (card H) (card K) (matching "orders are relatively prime"), and concludes H ⊓ K = ⊥. In Mathlib, the infimum of subgroups is their set-theoretic intersection, and ⊥ is the trivial subgroup {1}, so H ⊓ K = ⊥ is exactly the assertion H ∩ K = 1. The quantifier structure (universal over G, H, K) and hypotheses match the informal statement precisely.

### proofnet-226

**Informal statement**

> Use Lagrange's Theorem in the multiplicative group $(\mathbb{Z} / p \mathbb{Z})^{\times}$to prove Fermat's Little Theorem: if $p$ is a prime then $a^{p} \equiv a(\bmod p)$ for all $a \in \mathbb{Z}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_2_16 (p : ℕ) (hp : Nat.Prime p) (a : ℤ) :
  a ^ p ≡ a [ZMOD p]
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is Fermat's Little Theorem: for prime p and any integer a, a^p ≡ a (mod p). The Lean signature takes p : ℕ with hp : Nat.Prime p, an integer a : ℤ, and concludes a ^ p ≡ a [ZMOD p], which is integer congruence modulo p. This matches the informal claim exactly: same hypothesis (p prime), same universal quantification over a ∈ ℤ, and same conclusion (a^p ≡ a mod p). No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-227

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $H$ is a normal subgroup of $G$ of prime index $p$ then for all $K \leq G$ either $K \leq H$, or $G=H K$ and $|K: K \cap H|=p$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_3_3 {p : Nat.Primes} {G : Type*} [Group G]
  {H : Subgroup G} [hH : H.Normal] (hH1 : H.index = p) :
  ∀ K : Subgroup G, K ≤ H ∨ (H ⊔ K = ⊤ ∧ (K ⊓ H).relIndex K = p)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean encoding matches the informal statement. `p : Nat.Primes` with `H.index = p` captures "prime index p". `H.Normal` and `H : Subgroup G` capture the hypothesis. The universal quantifier `∀ K : Subgroup G` matches "for all K ≤ G" (every subgroup is ≤ G). The disjunction `K ≤ H ∨ ...` matches. Since H is normal, the product set HK is a subgroup and equals the join `H ⊔ K`; thus `H ⊔ K = ⊤` is equivalent to `G = HK`. Finally `(K ⊓ H).relIndex K = p` is exactly `[K : K ∩ H] = p`. No hypotheses are added or dropped, and the conclusion is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-228

**Informal statement**

> Use Cauchy's Theorem and induction to show that a finite abelian group has a subgroup of order $n$ for each positive divisor $n$ of its order.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_3_4_4 {G : Type*} [CommGroup G] [Fintype G] {n : ℕ}
    (hn : n ∣ (card G)) :
    ∃ (H : Subgroup G) (H_fin : Fintype H), @card H H_fin = n
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that any finite abelian group G has, for every positive divisor n of |G|, a subgroup of order n. The Lean signature takes [CommGroup G] [Fintype G] (finite abelian) and a hypothesis n ∣ card G, then concludes the existence of a subgroup H of cardinality n. The "positive" qualifier on n is automatic: since G is a group with Fintype instance, card G ≥ 1, so n = 0 would falsify n ∣ card G, making the hypothesis vacuous in that case. The quantifier structure (∀ n with n ∣ |G|, ∃ H, |H| = n) and the mathematical content match exactly. The Fintype H existential is just the standard Lean idiom for stating "H is finite of cardinality n," which is equivalent to the informal "subgroup of order n."

The quantifier structure (∃ K ≤ H, K.Normal ∧ K.index ≤ n!) matches "there is a normal subgroup K of G with K ≤ H and |G:K| ≤ n!". ### proofnet-234

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a group with subgroups $H$ and $K$ with $H \leq K$. Prove that if $H$ is characteristic in $K$ and $K$ is normal in $G$ then $H$ is normal in $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_4_8a {G : Type*} [Group G] (H K : Subgroup G)
  (hHK : H ≤ K) [hHK1 : (H.subgroupOf K).Characteristic] [hK : K.Normal] :
  H.Normal
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes exactly the informal claim: G is an arbitrary group, H and K are subgroups with H ≤ K, H viewed as a subgroup of K (`H.subgroupOf K`) is characteristic, K is normal in G, and the conclusion is that H is normal in G. The use of `H.subgroupOf K` is the standard Mathlib idiom for "H as a subgroup of K," and `.Characteristic` on it correctly captures "H is characteristic in K." The hypotheses and conclusion align with the informal statement, with no extra assumptions (no finiteness, no decidability) and no weakening.

### proofnet-241

**Informal statement**

> Let $P$ be a normal Sylow $p$-subgroup of $G$ and let $H$ be any subgroup of $G$. Prove that $P \cap H$ is the unique Sylow $p$-subgroup of $H$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_4_5_33 {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G] {p : ℕ}
  (P : Sylow p G) [hP : P.Normal] (H : Subgroup G) [Fintype H] :
  (∀ R : Sylow p H, R.toSubgroup = (H ⊓ P.toSubgroup).subgroupOf H) ∧
  Nonempty (Sylow p H)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim "P∩H is the unique Sylow p-subgroup of H" decomposes into existence (there is a Sylow p-subgroup of H, and it equals P∩H) and uniqueness (every Sylow p-subgroup of H equals P∩H). The Lean statement encodes uniqueness via `∀ R : Sylow p H, R.toSubgroup = (H ⊓ P).subgroupOf H` and existence via `Nonempty (Sylow p H)`. Together with the existence clause, the universal clause forces P∩H itself to be a Sylow p-subgroup of H, and the universal clause then guarantees uniqueness. The hypotheses (G finite, P a normal Sylow p-subgroup, H an arbitrary subgroup) match the informal statement exactly, and `(H ⊓ P).subgroupOf H` is the standard Mathlib encoding of P∩H viewed as a subgroup of H. No extra or missing hypotheses are introduced.

### proofnet-244  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_7_2_2_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $p(x)=a_{n} x^{n}+a_{n-1} x^{n-1}+\cdots+a_{1} x+a_{0}$ be an element of the polynomial ring $R[x]$. Prove that $p(x)$ is a zero divisor in $R[x]$ if and only if there is a nonzero $b \in R$ such that $b p(x)=0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_7_2_2_corrected {R : Type*} [CommRing R] (p : Polynomial R) :
    (∃ q : Polynomial R, q ≠ 0 ∧ p * q = 0) ↔
      (∃ b : R, b ≠ 0 ∧ b • p = 0)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is McCoy's theorem: in R[x] (R commutative), p(x) is a zero divisor iff some nonzero constant b kills it. The Lean signature encodes "p is a zero divisor" as ∃ q ≠ 0, p*q = 0, which is the standard definition (a left/two-sided zero divisor in a commutative ring), and encodes "there is a nonzero b ∈ R with b·p(x) = 0" as ∃ b ≠ 0, b • p = 0, where scalar multiplication of a polynomial by an element of R is the natural action and is equivalent to multiplication by the constant polynomial C b. The CommRing R hypothesis matches the standard ambient setting (Dummit & Foote work in commutative rings here, and McCoy's theorem requires commutativity). The biconditional, the quantifier structure, and the types all match the informal claim.

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_7_3_16 {R S : Type*} [Ring R] [Ring S]
   {φ : R →+* S} (hf : Function.Surjective φ) :
   φ '' (center R) ⊆ center S

The types (Ring R, Ring S), the surjectivity hypothesis, and the conclusion all match the informal claim directly. ### proofnet-247  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_2_4`

**Informal statement**

> Let $R$ be an integral domain. Prove that if the following two conditions hold then $R$ is a Principal Ideal Domain: (i) any two nonzero elements $a$ and $b$ in $R$ have a greatest common divisor which can be written in the form $r a+s b$ for some $r, s \in R$, and (ii) if $a_{1}, a_{2}, a_{3}, \ldots$ are nonzero elements of $R$ such that $a_{i+1} \mid a_{i}$ for all $i$, then there is a positive integer $N$ such that $a_{n}$ is a unit times $a_{N}$ for all $n \geq N$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_2_4 {R : Type*} [CommRing R] [IsDomain R] [GCDMonoid R]
  (h1 : ∀ a b : R, a ≠ 0 → b ≠ 0 → ∃ r s : R, gcd a b = r*a + s*b)
  (h2 : ∀ a : ℕ → R, (∀ i : ℕ, a i ≠ 0 ∧ a (i + 1) ∣ a i) →
  ∃ N : ℕ, ∀ n ≥ N, ∃ u : R, IsUnit u ∧ a n = u * a N) :
  IsPrincipalIdealRing R
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all parts of the informal statement. The `[GCDMonoid R]` typeclass plus `h1` together encode condition (i): gcds exist and a gcd of any two nonzero elements is expressible as ra+sb (since gcds are unique up to units, fixing a choice via GCDMonoid is equivalent to the informal "a gcd can be written as ra+sb"). `h2` faithfully encodes condition (ii), including the nonzero hypothesis and the "unit times a_N" conclusion. The target `IsPrincipalIdealRing R` together with `IsDomain R` gives "R is a PID." Quantifier structure, types, and the divisibility direction all match the informal claim.

### proofnet-248

**Informal statement**

> Let $R=\mathbb{Z}[\sqrt{-n}]$ where $n$ is a squarefree integer greater than 3. Prove that $2, \sqrt{-n}$ and $1+\sqrt{-n}$ are irreducibles in $R$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_5a {n : ℤ} (hn0 : n > 3) (hn1 : Squarefree n) :
  Irreducible (2 : Zsqrtd $ -n) ∧
  Irreducible (⟨0, 1⟩ : Zsqrtd $ -n) ∧
  Irreducible (1 + ⟨0, 1⟩ : Zsqrtd $ -n)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement requires n to be a squarefree integer > 3, and asks to show 2, √(-n), and 1+√(-n) are irreducible in Z[√(-n)]. The Lean signature uses Zsqrtd (-n), which is Mathlib's Z[√(-n)]. The element ⟨0,1⟩ in Zsqrtd represents 0 + 1·√(-n) = √(-n), and 1 + ⟨0,1⟩ represents 1 + √(-n). The hypotheses `n > 3` and `Squarefree n` match the informal "squarefree integer greater than 3". The conjunction asserts irreducibility of all three elements as required. This is a faithful encoding.

### proofnet-249  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_6b_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $q \in \mathbb{Z}$ be a prime with $q \equiv 3 \bmod 4$. Prove that the quotient ring $\mathbb{Z}[i] /(q)$ is a field with $q^{2}$ elements.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_8_3_6b_corrected {q : ℕ} (hq0 : q.Prime) (hq1 : q ≡ 3 [ZMOD 4]) :
    IsField (GaussianInt ⧸ span ({↑q} : Set GaussianInt)) ∧
    ∃ finR : Fintype (GaussianInt ⧸ span ({↑q} : Set GaussianInt)),
      @Fintype.card _ finR = q ^ 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes q as a Nat prime with q ≡ 3 [ZMOD 4], which captures the positive integer primes congruent to 3 mod 4 (the only mathematically distinct case since the ideal (q) equals (-q) in ℤ[i]). The quotient `GaussianInt ⧸ span ({↑q} : Set GaussianInt)` is exactly ℤ[i]/(q). The conjunction `IsField ... ∧ ∃ finR : Fintype ..., Fintype.card = q^2` matches the informal claim that the quotient is a field with q² elements. The use of an existential Fintype instance is the standard way to assert finiteness with given cardinality. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-250  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Dummit_Foote_exercise_9_1_10`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the ring $\mathbb{Z}\left[x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right] /\left(x_{1} x_{2}, x_{3} x_{4}, x_{5} x_{6}, \ldots\right)$ contains infinitely many minimal prime ideals.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_9_1_10 {f : ℕ → MvPolynomial ℕ ℤ}
    (hf : f = fun i => MvPolynomial.X (2*i) * MvPolynomial.X (2*i+1)) :
    Infinite (minimalPrimes (MvPolynomial ℕ ℤ ⧸ span (range f)))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature defines f i = X(2i) * X(2i+1), so f 0 = X₀X₁, f 1 = X₂X₃, etc., matching the informal generators (x₁x₂, x₃x₄, ...) up to 0/1-indexing. The ambient ring MvPolynomial ℕ ℤ matches ℤ[x₁, x₂, …]. The quotient `MvPolynomial ℕ ℤ ⧸ span (range f)` requires `span` to resolve to `Ideal.span` (since ⧸ on a ring requires an ideal), which equals the ideal generated by these monomials. The conclusion `Infinite (minimalPrimes ...)` says the set/type of minimal primes is infinite, matching "infinitely many minimal prime ideals". The `hf` hypothesis just names f via a definitional equation, which doesn't change the mathematical content. No extra hypotheses are imposed and no quantifier structure is altered.

### proofnet-258  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_2_2_6c`

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a group in which $(a b)^{n}=a^{n} b^{n}$ for some fixed integer $n>1$ for all $a, b \in G$. For all $a, b \in G$, prove that $\left(a b a^{-1} b^{-1}\right)^{n(n-1)}=e$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_2_6c {G : Type*} [Group G] {n : ℕ} (hn : n > 1)
  (h : ∀ (a b : G), (a * b) ^ n = a ^ n * b ^ n) :
  ∀ (a b : G), (a * b * a⁻¹ * b⁻¹) ^ (n * (n - 1)) = 1
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature quantifies over an arbitrary group G, fixes a natural number n > 1, assumes (a*b)^n = a^n * b^n for all a,b ∈ G, and concludes (aba⁻¹b⁻¹)^(n(n-1)) = 1 for all a,b ∈ G. This matches the informal statement exactly. The informal statement says "fixed integer n > 1", but since n > 1 the exponent is positive and using ℕ is equivalent to using a positive integer for exponentiation in a group. The exponent n*(n-1) is well-defined in ℕ since n ≥ 2. No hypotheses are added or dropped.

### proofnet-260

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a group such that all subgroups of $G$ are normal in $G$. If $a, b \in G$, prove that $ba = a^jb$ for some $j$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_23 {G : Type*} [Group G]
  (hG : ∀ (H : Subgroup G), H.Normal) (a b : G) :
  ∃ (j : ℤ) , b*a = a^j * b
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal statement faithfully. It quantifies over an arbitrary group G with the hypothesis that every subgroup is normal (Hamiltonian/Dedekind condition), takes arbitrary elements a, b ∈ G, and asserts the existence of an integer j with ba = a^j b. The informal "for some j" is naturally read as an integer exponent (since the cyclic subgroup ⟨a⟩ is normal, conjugates of a lie in ⟨a⟩, giving an integer power), which matches the Lean ℤ. No hypotheses are added or dropped, and the conclusion matches.

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_31 {G : Type*} [CommGroup G] [Fintype G]

### proofnet-262

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a group of order 9 must be abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_43 (G : Type*) [Group G] [Fintype G]
  (hG : card G = 9) :
  Nonempty (CommGroup G)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is: any group G of order 9 is abelian. The Lean signature takes an arbitrary type G with a `Group` instance and `Fintype G`, assumes `card G = 9`, and concludes `Nonempty (CommGroup G)`, i.e. G admits a commutative group structure. Since a `CommGroup` instance on G is equivalent to the group operation already on G being commutative (the multiplication is fixed by the `Group G` instance), this is the standard Mathlib idiom for "G is abelian". The hypotheses (finite group of order 9) match exactly, and the conclusion correctly captures commutativity. No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-263  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_2_5_52_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a finite group and $\varphi$ an automorphism of $G$ such that $\varphi(x) = x^{-1}$ for more than three-fourths of the elements of $G$. Prove that $\varphi(y) = y^{-1}$ for all $y \in G$, and so $G$ is abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_52_corrected {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G]
    (φ : G ≃* G) {I : Finset G} (hI : ∀ x ∈ I, φ x = x⁻¹)
    (hI1 : (0.75 : ℚ) * card G < card I) :
    (∀ x : G, φ x = x⁻¹) ∧ ∀ x y : G, x * y = y * x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures a finite group `G` with an automorphism `φ : G ≃* G` (a `MulEquiv` from `G` to itself, which is exactly an automorphism). The hypothesis encodes "more than three-fourths of elements satisfy φ(x) = x⁻¹" by exhibiting a Finset `I` of such elements with `|I| > 0.75 · |G|`; since every member of `I` satisfies the inversion property, this is equivalent to saying that the set of elements with φ(x) = x⁻¹ has cardinality > 3|G|/4. The conclusion `(∀ x, φ x = x⁻¹) ∧ ∀ x y, x*y = y*x` matches both parts of the informal claim (φ is inversion on all of G, and G is abelian). No extra or missing hypotheses (like decidability or commutativity) alter the math, so the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-264  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_2_7_7_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> If $\varphi$ is a homomorphism of $G$ onto $G'$ and $N \triangleleft G$, show that $\varphi(N) \triangleleft G'$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_7_7_corrected {G : Type*} [Group G] {G' : Type*} [Group G']
    (φ : G →* G') (hφ : Function.Surjective φ) (N : Subgroup G) [N.Normal] :
    (Subgroup.map φ N).Normal
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says φ is a homomorphism of G onto G' (i.e., surjective) and N is normal in G, then φ(N) is normal in G'. The Lean signature has φ : G →* G' (group homomorphism), hφ : Function.Surjective φ (onto), N : Subgroup G with [N.Normal] instance, and concludes (Subgroup.map φ N).Normal, which is exactly the image φ(N) being normal in G'. The hypotheses, conclusion, and types all align precisely.

### proofnet-265

**Informal statement**

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_8_15 {G H: Type*} [Fintype G] [Group G] [Fintype H] [Group H]
  (hG_nonab : ∃ x y : G, x * y ≠ y * x) (hH_nonab : ∃ x y : H, x * y ≠ y * x)
  {p q : ℕ} (hp : Nat.Prime p) (hq : Nat.Prime q)

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The conclusion Nonempty (G ≃* H) gives a multiplicative isomorphism, matching "isomorphic." No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-266

**Informal statement**

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_10_1 {G : Type*} [Group G] (A : Subgroup G)
  [A.Normal] {b : G} (hb : b ∉ A) (hp : Nat.Prime (orderOf b)) :
  A ⊓ (Subgroup.closure {b}) = ⊥
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all components of the informal statement: A is a subgroup of an arbitrary group G with the [A.Normal] instance (matching "normal subgroup"); b ∉ A (hb) matches "b ∉ A"; Nat.Prime (orderOf b) matches "b has prime order p" (the prime p is just existentially encoded as the orderOf b, which is the standard formalization). ### proofnet-270

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_2_6 {R : Type*} [Ring R] (a x : R)

### proofnet-271

**Informal statement**

> If $R$ is a commutative ring and $a \in R$, let $L(a) = \{x \in R \mid xa = 0\}$. Prove that $L(a)$ is an ideal of $R$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_3_1 {R : Type*} [CommRing R] (a : R) :
  ∃ I : Ideal R, {x : R | x*a=0} = I
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says L(a) = {x ∈ R | xa = 0} is an ideal of R. The Lean version asserts the existence of an ideal I whose underlying set equals {x : R | x*a = 0}. This is logically equivalent to "the set {x | xa = 0} is an ideal," since an ideal is determined by its underlying set, and a set is an ideal iff there exists an Ideal whose carrier equals it. The commutative ring hypothesis matches, and `x*a` corresponds to `xa`. No hypotheses are added or dropped, and the conclusion is the same statement.

### proofnet-272

**Informal statement**

> Show that $(p - 1)/2$ of the numbers $1, 2, \ldots, p - 1$ are quadratic residues and $(p - 1)/2$ are quadratic nonresidues $\mod p$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_4_9 (p : ℕ) (hp : Nat.Prime p) (hodd : p % 2 = 1) :
  (∃ S : Finset (ZMod p), 0 ∉ S ∧ S.card = (p-1)/2 ∧ ∀ q ∈ S, ∃ x : ZMod p, x^2 = q) ∧
  (∃ S : Finset (ZMod p), 0 ∉ S ∧ S.card = (p-1)/2 ∧ ∀ q ∈ S, ¬ ∃ x : ZMod p, x^2 = q)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement asserts existence of a Finset S ⊆ ZMod p of size (p-1)/2, not containing 0, all of whose elements are squares, AND existence of another such Finset of size (p-1)/2 not containing 0 of nonsquares. Individually, each conjunct only says "at least (p-1)/2 nonzero QRs" and "at least (p-1)/2 nonzero non-QRs". However, the nonzero squares and nonzero non-squares are disjoint subsets of (ZMod p) \ {0}, which has cardinality p-1. So having at least (p-1)/2 of each forces exactly (p-1)/2 of each, matching the informal claim. The identification of {1,...,p-1} with the nonzero elements of ZMod p is the standard one. Hence the two formulations are logically equivalent.

### proofnet-273  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_4_5_23`

**Informal statement**

> Let $F = \mathbb{Z}_7$ and let $p(x) = x^3 - 2$ and $q(x) = x^3 + 2$ be in $F[x]$. Show that $p(x)$ and $q(x)$ are irreducible in $F[x]$ and that the fields $F[x]/(p(x))$ and $F[x]/(q(x))$ are isomorphic.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_5_23 {p q: Polynomial (ZMod 7)}
  (hp : p = X^3 - 2) (hq : q = X^3 + 2) :
  Irreducible p ∧ Irreducible q ∧
  (Nonempty $ Polynomial (ZMod 7) ⧸ span ({p} : Set $ Polynomial $ ZMod 7) ≃+*
  Polynomial (ZMod 7) ⧸ span ({q} : Set $ Polynomial $ ZMod 7))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes p = X^3 - 2 and q = X^3 + 2 in (ZMod 7)[X], asserts both are irreducible, and asserts the existence of a ring isomorphism between the quotient rings Polynomial (ZMod 7) ⧸ span {p} and Polynomial (ZMod 7) ⧸ span {q}. Since ZMod 7 = ℤ/7ℤ = F, and span {p} is the ideal generated by p (so the quotient is F[x]/(p)), this matches the informal statement. A ring isomorphism between two fields is the same as a field isomorphism, so ≃+* faithfully encodes "the fields are isomorphic". Nonempty (A ≃+* B) is the standard way to express "there exists an isomorphism".

### proofnet-274

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $f(x) = x^3 + 3x + 2$ is irreducible in $Q[x]$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_6_2 : Irreducible (X^3 + 3*X + 2 : Polynomial ℚ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that the polynomial x^3 + 3x + 2 is irreducible in ℚ[x]. The Lean signature states `Irreducible (X^3 + 3*X + 2 : Polynomial ℚ)`, which is exactly the assertion that this polynomial, viewed in ℚ[x], is irreducible. The polynomial expression and the ambient ring match. Mathlib's `Irreducible` predicate for polynomials over a field corresponds to the standard mathematical notion of irreducibility. There are no extra hypotheses or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-275

**Informal statement**

> If $F$ is a field of characteristic $p \neq 0$, show that $(a + b)^m = a^m + b^m$, where $m = p^n$, for all $a, b \in F$ and any positive integer $n$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_5_1_8 {p m n: ℕ} {F : Type*} [Field F]
  (hp : p ≠ 0) (hn : n > 0)
  (hF : CharP F p) (a b : F) (hm : m = p ^ n) :
  (a + b) ^ m = a^m + b^m
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all the essential elements: F is a field, p is its characteristic with p ≠ 0 (and since CharP F p for a field forces p to be 0 or prime, hp : p ≠ 0 implies p is prime, matching the informal characteristic p ≠ 0), n is a positive natural number (n > 0 corresponds to "positive integer n"), m = p^n, and a, b are arbitrary elements of F. The conclusion (a+b)^m = a^m + b^m is exactly the Freshman's Dream identity stated informally. The quantifier structure (universal in a, b, F, n) is preserved. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-276

**Informal statement**

> If $a \in K$ is such that $a^2$ is algebraic over the subfield $F$ of $K$, show that a is algebraic over $F$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_5_3_7 {K : Type*} [Field K] {F : Subfield K}
  {a : K} (ha : IsAlgebraic F (a ^ 2)) : IsAlgebraic F a
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: if a² is algebraic over F, then a is algebraic over F. The Lean signature takes K as a field, F as a subfield of K, a in K, hypothesis that a^2 is algebraic over F, and concludes that a is algebraic over F. The types and quantifier structure match: K is a general field, F is an arbitrary subfield, and a is an arbitrary element. The hypothesis and conclusion correspond exactly. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-277  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_5_4_3_modified`

**Informal statement**

> If a ∈ ℂ satisfies p(a) = 0 where p(x) = x^5 + √2·x³ + √5·x² + √7·x + √11,
> then a is algebraic over ℚ of degree at most 80.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Herstein_exercise_5_4_3_modified {a : ℂ}
    (ha : a ^ 5 + (Real.sqrt 2 : ℂ) * a ^ 3 + (Real.sqrt 5 : ℂ) * a ^ 2 +
      (Real.sqrt 7 : ℂ) * a + (Real.sqrt 11 : ℂ) = 0) :
    ∃ q : Polynomial ℚ, q ≠ 0 ∧ q.natDegree ≤ 80 ∧
      Polynomial.aeval a q = 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim "a is algebraic over ℚ of degree at most 80" is equivalent to the existence of a nonzero polynomial q ∈ ℚ[x] with deg(q) ≤ 80 and q(a) = 0 (the minimal polynomial divides any such q, so its degree is ≤ 80). The Lean signature asserts exactly this: ∃ q : Polynomial ℚ with q ≠ 0, q.natDegree ≤ 80, and aeval a q = 0. The hypothesis encodes p(a)=0 with the same polynomial p(x)=x^5+√2 x^3+√5 x^2+√7 x+√11, with √· interpreted via Real.sqrt cast to ℂ (and since 2,5,7,11 are nonnegative, Real.sqrt gives the standard real square roots). Quantifier structure (universally over a satisfying p(a)=0, existentially over q) matches. No extra or missing hypotheses; the conclusion matches the informal claim.

### proofnet-278

**Informal statement**

> If $F$ is of characteristic $p \neq 0$, show that all the roots of $x^m - x$, where $m = p^n$, are distinct.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_5_6_14 {p m n: ℕ} (hp : p ≠ 0) (hn : n > 0)
  {F : Type*} [Field F] [CharP F p] (hm : m = p ^ n) :
  (X ^ m - X : Polynomial F).Separable
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that in a field F of characteristic p ≠ 0, the polynomial x^m - x with m = p^n has distinct roots. The Lean signature captures this via `(X^m - X).Separable`, which over a field is equivalent to having no repeated roots (i.e. all roots in any extension are distinct). The hypotheses `Field F`, `CharP F p`, and `p ≠ 0` correctly encode "F is of characteristic p ≠ 0" (and force p to be prime since F is a field). The added `n > 0` is implicit in Herstein's exercise (so m = p^n is the relevant Frobenius power); without it m=1 makes X^m - X = 0, which would be trivially non-separable, so requiring n > 0 is a reasonable disambiguation rather than a real strengthening. The conclusion (Separable) matches "all roots distinct" for a nonzero polynomial over a field.

### proofnet-280

**Informal statement**

> If $a$ is a nonzero integer, then for $n>m$ show that $\left(a^{2^{n}}+1, a^{2^{m}}+1\right)=1$ or 2 depending on whether $a$ is odd or even.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_2_4 {a : ℤ} (_ha : a ≠ 0)
  (hf : f_a = λ n m : ℕ => Int.gcd (a^(2^n) + 1) (a^(2^m)+1)) {n m : ℕ}
  (hnm : n > m) :
  (Odd a → f_a n m = 2) ∧ (Even a → f_a n m = 1)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says gcd(a^(2^n)+1, a^(2^m)+1) = 1 if a is even, and 2 if a is odd (since odd a makes both terms even, even a makes both terms odd). The Lean signature asserts exactly this conjunction: Odd a → gcd = 2, Even a → gcd = 1, under the hypothesis a ≠ 0 and n > m. The unusual encoding via a free variable f_a constrained by hf is logically equivalent to using the gcd expression directly. Types (a : ℤ, n m : ℕ) and Int.gcd match the informal claim. The verdict is faithful.

### proofnet-281

**Informal statement**

> Show that $\sum^{\prime} 1 / n$, the sum being over square free integers, diverges.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_2_27a :
  ¬ Summable (λ i : {p : ℤ // Squarefree p} => (1 : ℚ) / i)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim states that the sum of 1/n over (positive) squarefree integers diverges. The Lean encoding asserts that 1/i is not Summable over the subtype of squarefree integers in ℤ. Although the Lean version ranges over all of ℤ (including negative squarefree integers and ±1), this does not change the mathematical content: Summable in this context is equivalent to absolute (unconditional) summability, and the squarefree integers in ℤ are just ±(squarefree positive ℕ), so Σ |1/i| over squarefree ℤ equals 2·Σ_{n squarefree, n≥1} 1/n. Thus ¬Summable over squarefree ℤ holds iff the informal series diverges. The use of ℚ as the codomain doesn't matter for the (non-)summability claim. So the encoding faithfully captures the divergence statement.

### proofnet-283

**Informal statement**

> If $n$ is not a prime, show that $(n-1) ! \equiv 0(n)$, except when $n=4$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_3_10 {n : ℕ+} (hn0 : ¬ n.Prime) :
  Nat.factorial (n-1) ≡ 0 [MOD n] ↔ n ≠ 4
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim says: for non-prime n, (n-1)! ≡ 0 (mod n), with n=4 being the sole exception. The Lean signature, under the hypothesis that n is not prime, asserts the biconditional (n-1)! ≡ 0 [MOD n] ↔ n ≠ 4. The forward direction encodes "if the congruence holds, then n ≠ 4" (true, since 3! = 6 ≢ 0 mod 4), and the backward direction encodes "if n ≠ 4 then (n-1)! ≡ 0 mod n", which is exactly the informal claim. The "except when n=4" phrasing naturally means n=4 is a genuine exception where the conclusion fails, matching the iff. Using ℕ+ rather than ℕ is a benign encoding — factorials are defined on ℕ and n must be positive for the statement to make sense; PNat.Prime excludes 1 and the n=1 case is vacuously satisfied. No hypotheses are silently added or removed.

### proofnet-286

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $1^{k}+2^{k}+\cdots+(p-1)^{k} \equiv 0(p)$ if $p-1 \nmid k$ and $-1(p)$ if $p-1 \mid k$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_11 {p : ℕ} (hp : p.Prime) (k s: ℕ)
  (hs : s = ∑ n : Fin (p-1), (n + 1 : ℕ) ^ k) :
  ((¬ p - 1 ∣ k) → s ≡ 0 [MOD p]) ∧ (p - 1 ∣ k → s ≡ -1 [ZMOD p])
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature defines s as ∑_{n : Fin (p-1)} (n+1)^k = 1^k + 2^k + ... + (p-1)^k, which matches the informal sum. The conclusion is split into two implications: if p-1 ∤ k then s ≡ 0 mod p (using Nat.ModEq), and if p-1 ∣ k then s ≡ -1 mod p (using Int.ModEq via ZMOD, which correctly handles the negative residue). The hypothesis hp : p.Prime matches the implicit primality assumption. The mixing of MOD and ZMOD is appropriate: 0 ≡ s mod p as naturals is equivalent to as integers, and -1 needs ZMOD. The statement matches the informal claim faithfully.

### proofnet-287  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_5_28`

**Informal statement**

> Show that $x^{4} \equiv 2(p)$ has a solution for $p \equiv 1(4)$ iff $p$ is of the form $A^{2}+64 B^{2}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean


theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_5_28 {p : ℕ} (hp : p.Prime) (hp1 : p ≡ 1 [MOD 4]) :
    (∃ x, x ^ 4 ≡ 2 [MOD p]) ↔ ∃ A B, p = A ^ 2 + 64 * B ^ 2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that for a prime p ≡ 1 (mod 4), x^4 ≡ 2 (mod p) is solvable iff p = A² + 64B². The Lean signature encodes exactly this biconditional, with hypotheses p prime and p ≡ 1 (mod 4). The existential ∃ x with x : ℕ is equivalent to solvability over ℤ/p (any residue class has a natural representative). The form A² + 64B² with A, B : ℕ is equivalent to the integer version since squares only depend on absolute values, so allowing A, B ∈ ℤ gives the same set of representable p. Quantifier structure and conclusion match the informal claim.

### proofnet-289

**Informal statement**

> Show that the collection $$\mathcal{T}_\infty = \{U | X - U \text{ is infinite or empty or all of X}\}$$ does not need to be a topology on the set $X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_3b : ¬ ∀ X : Type, ∀s : Set (Set X),
  (∀ t : Set X, t ∈ s → (Set.Infinite tᶜ ∨ t = ∅ ∨ t = ⊤)) →
  (Set.Infinite (⋃₀ s)ᶜ ∨ (⋃₀ s) = ∅ ∨ (⋃₀ s) = ⊤)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show 𝒯_∞ need not be a topology. The Lean statement asserts that 𝒯_∞ is not always closed under arbitrary unions (negation of a universal closure property). Since 𝒯_∞ always contains ∅ and X by definition, and is always closed under finite intersection (the union of two infinite complements is infinite), the only topology axiom that can fail is closure under arbitrary union. Hence "is not a topology" and "fails closure under arbitrary union" are mathematically equivalent for this specific 𝒯_∞, and the Lean encoding captures exactly the content of the exercise. The types and quantifiers match (universal X, family of sets, conclusion about ⋃₀), and ⊤ for Set X equals univ.

### proofnet-290

**Informal statement**

> If $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$ is a family of topologies on $X$, show that $\bigcup \mathcal{T}_\alpha$ does not need to be a topology on $X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_4a2 :
  ∃ (X I : Type*) (T : I → Set (Set X)),
  (∀ i, is_topology X (T i)) ∧ ¬  is_topology X (⋃ i : I, T i)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show that the union of a family of topologies need not be a topology, i.e., there exists some set X and some family of topologies whose union is not a topology. The Lean signature existentially quantifies over a type X, an index type I, and a family T : I → Set (Set X) such that each T i is a topology but their union is not. This matches the standard reading of "does not need to be" as "there exists a counterexample." The types and quantifier structure align correctly.

### proofnet-291

**Informal statement**

> Let $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$ be a family of topologies on $X$. Show that there is a unique largest topology on $X$ contained in all the collections $\mathcal{T}_\alpha$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_4b2 (X I : Type*) (T : I → Set (Set X)) (h : ∀ i, is_topology X (T i)) :
  ∃! T', is_topology X T' ∧ (∀ i, T' ⊆ T i) ∧
  ∀ T'', is_topology X T'' → (∀ i, T'' ⊆ T i) → T'' ⊆ T'
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature asserts the existence of a unique T' that (a) is a topology on X, (b) is contained in every T i, and (c) contains every other topology T'' that is contained in all T i. This is precisely the statement that there is a unique largest topology on X contained in all the T_α. The family is indexed by an arbitrary type I (matching "family"), each T i is required to be a topology via the hypothesis h, and "largest" is correctly encoded by the maximality clause. Uniqueness via ∃! together with the maximality condition matches the informal "unique largest". No extra or missing hypotheses are introduced.

### proofnet-292

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $\mathcal{A}$ is a subbasis for a topology on $X$, then the topology generated by $\mathcal{A}$ equals the intersection of all topologies on $X$ that contain $\mathcal{A}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_5b {X : Type*} (A : Set (Set X)) :
  generateFrom A = generateFrom (sInter {T | is_topology X T ∧ A ⊆ T})
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that generateFrom(A) equals the intersection τ of all topologies on X containing A. In the Lean statement, the RHS wraps that intersection in `generateFrom`, but since the intersection of topologies is itself a topology containing A, applying `generateFrom` to it returns the same topology (generateFrom is the identity on topologies). Thus `generateFrom (sInter {T | is_topology X T ∧ A ⊆ T})` equals τ, and the equation `generateFrom A = generateFrom (sInter ...)` is logically equivalent to `generateFrom A = τ`, exactly the informal assertion. The omission of the hypothesis "A is a subbasis" is harmless because every collection of subsets is automatically a subbasis for the topology it generates, so that hypothesis is vacuous. No types or quantifiers are altered.

### proofnet-293

**Informal statement**

> Show that the collection $\{(a,b) \mid a < b, a \text{ and } b \text{ rational}\}$ is a basis that generates the standard topology on $\mathbb{R}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_13_8a :
  IsTopologicalBasis {S : Set ℝ | ∃ a b : ℚ, a < b ∧ S = Ioo ↑a ↑b}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show the collection of rational open intervals (a,b) with a<b rational is a basis generating the standard topology on ℝ. The Lean statement uses `IsTopologicalBasis` applied to the set of `Set ℝ` of the form `Ioo ↑a ↑b` for rationals a<b. In Mathlib, `IsTopologicalBasis` is defined relative to the ambient topology on the type, and ℝ carries the standard topology by default, so this asserts both that the collection is a basis and that it generates the standard topology. The encoding `∃ a b : ℚ, a < b ∧ S = Ioo ↑a ↑b` correctly captures rational endpoints with a<b. This matches the informal claim.

### proofnet-294  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_16_1_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $Y$ is a subspace of $X$, and $A$ is a subset of $Y$, then the topology $A$ inherits as a subspace of $Y$ is the same as the topology it inherits as a subspace of $X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Munkres_exercise_16_1_corrected {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
    (Y : Set X) (A : Set Y) :
    IsInducing (fun a : A => (a : X))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim says: for A ⊆ Y ⊆ X, the subspace topology A gets from Y equals the subspace topology it gets from X. In Lean, A : Set Y carries by default the subspace topology induced from Y (which itself has the subspace topology from X). The statement `IsInducing (fun a : A => (a : X))` says the topology on A equals the topology pulled back along the inclusion A ↪ X, i.e. the subspace topology A gets directly from X. Equating A's default (Y-subspace) topology with the X-induced topology is exactly the informal claim. No extra hypotheses are added and no quantifiers are altered, so the encoding is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-296

theorem Munkres_exercise_18_8a {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]
  [LinearOrder Y] [OrderTopology Y] {f g : X → Y}
  (hf : Continuous f) (hg : Continuous g) :
  IsClosed {x | f x ≤ g x}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The conclusion {x | f x ≤ g x} is closed is exactly the informal claim. No extra or missing hypotheses; types and quantifier structure align.

### proofnet-297

**Informal statement**

> Let $A \subset X$; let $f: A \rightarrow Y$ be continuous; let $Y$ be Hausdorff. Show that if $f$ may be extended to a continuous function $g: \bar{A} \rightarrow Y$, then $g$ is uniquely determined by $f$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_18_13
  {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] {Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace Y] [T2Space Y]
  {A : Set X} {f : X → Y} (hf : ContinuousOn f A)
  (g : X → Y) (g_con : ContinuousOn g (closure A))
  (gExt : ∀ x : A, g x = f x) :
  ∀ (g' : X → Y), (ContinuousOn g' (closure A) ∧ (∀ x : A, g' x = f x)) → (∀ x : closure A, g x = g' x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes the uniqueness claim correctly: it fixes one continuous extension g of f to closure A, then asserts that any other continuous extension g' of f agrees with g on closure A. The Hausdorff hypothesis [T2Space Y] is present, the continuity hypotheses on f, g, g' (via ContinuousOn on A and closure A) match the informal setup, and the agreement-on-A condition (∀ x : A, g' x = f x) captures "g' extends f". Encoding f : A → Y as f : X → Y with ContinuousOn f A is the standard Mathlib idiom and is equivalent. The conclusion ∀ x : closure A, g x = g' x is exactly the desired uniqueness on the closure. No extraneous or missing hypotheses.

theorem Munkres_exercise_21_6b
  (f : ℕ → I → ℝ)

### proofnet-300

**Informal statement**

> Let $p: X \rightarrow Y$ be a continuous map. Show that if there is a continuous map $f: Y \rightarrow X$ such that $p \circ f$ equals the identity map of $Y$, then $p$ is a quotient map.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_22_2a {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  [TopologicalSpace Y] (p : X → Y) (h : Continuous p) :
  (∃ (f : Y → X), Continuous f ∧ p ∘ f = id) → IsQuotientMap p
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the informal statement precisely: p : X → Y is assumed continuous, the hypothesis is the existence of a continuous f : Y → X with p ∘ f = id (i.e., p ∘ f equals the identity on Y), and the conclusion is that p is a quotient map (IsQuotientMap p in Mathlib). The topological space instances on X and Y are the natural ambient assumptions, and `id` is interpreted at type Y → Y making `p ∘ f = id` equivalent to "p ∘ f equals the identity map of Y". No extra hypotheses (like surjectivity, finiteness, or Hausdorffness) are silently added, and none are dropped. Hence the formalization is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-301

**Informal statement**

> Let $p \colon X \rightarrow Y$ be an open map. Show that if $A$ is open in $X$, then the map $q \colon A \rightarrow p(A)$ obtained by restricting $p$ is an open map.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_22_5 {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  [TopologicalSpace Y] (p : X → Y) (hp : IsOpenMap p)
  (A : Set X) (hA : IsOpen A) : IsOpenMap (p ∘ Subtype.val : A → Y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks that q : A → p(A) (with p(A) carrying the subspace topology) be an open map. The Lean version states that p ∘ Subtype.val : A → Y is an open map (into Y). Since p is open and A is open, p(A) is open in Y; therefore for any subset S ⊆ p(A), S is open in the subspace topology of p(A) iff S is open in Y. Hence "q is open as a map to p(A)" is logically equivalent to "p ∘ Subtype.val is open as a map to Y". The hypotheses (p open, A open) match exactly, so the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-302

**Informal statement**

> Let $\left\{A_{\alpha}\right\}$ be a collection of connected subspaces of $X$; let $A$ be a connected subset of $X$. Show that if $A \cap A_{\alpha} \neq \varnothing$ for all $\alpha$, then $A \cup\left(\bigcup A_{\alpha}\right)$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_23_3 {X α : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  {A : α → Set X}
  (hAa : ∀ a, IsConnected (A a))
  (A₀ : Set X)
  (hA : IsConnected A₀)
  (h : ∀ a, A₀ ∩ A a ≠ ∅) :
  IsConnected (A₀ ∪ (⋃ a, A a))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

Mathlib's `IsConnected` means "nonempty and preconnected". The informal statement assumes each $A_\alpha$ is connected and $A$ is connected, and the hypothesis $A \cap A_\alpha \neq \emptyset$ forces both $A$ and each $A_\alpha$ to be nonempty, so the extra nonemptyness baked into `IsConnected` is automatic and doesn't add a real assumption. The hypotheses (`hAa`, `hA`, `h`) and the conclusion (`IsConnected (A₀ ∪ ⋃ a, A a)`) line up exactly with the informal claim — connectedness of all pieces, pairwise nonempty intersection with $A_0$, and connectedness of the union (which is automatically nonempty since $A_0$ is). The indexed family is encoded as `A : α → Set X`, which corresponds to $\{A_\alpha\}$. No hidden hypotheses (finiteness, decidability, etc.) are added. Hence the Lean signature is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-303

**Informal statement**

> Let $A \subset X$. Show that if $C$ is a connected subspace of $X$ that intersects both $A$ and $X-A$, then $C$ intersects $\operatorname{Bd} A$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_23_6 {X : Type*}
  [TopologicalSpace X] {A C : Set X} (hc : IsConnected C)
  (hCA : C ∩ A ≠ ∅) (hCXA : C ∩ Aᶜ ≠ ∅) :
  C ∩ (frontier A) ≠ ∅
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement matches the informal one: `IsConnected C` corresponds to "C is a connected subspace" (Mathlib's `IsConnected` requires nonemptiness, but the hypothesis `C ∩ A ≠ ∅` already forces C to be nonempty, so this adds no real strength). `C ∩ A ≠ ∅` and `C ∩ Aᶜ ≠ ∅` correspond exactly to "C intersects both A and X−A". `frontier A` in Mathlib is the topological boundary `Bd A`. The conclusion `C ∩ frontier A ≠ ∅` matches "C intersects Bd A". Quantifier structure (universal over X, A, C) is preserved via Lean's implicit binders. Hence the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-304

**Informal statement**

> Let $p: X \rightarrow Y$ be a quotient map. Show that if each set $p^{-1}(\{y\})$ is connected, and if $Y$ is connected, then $X$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_23_11 {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]
  (p : X → Y) (hq : IsQuotientMap p)
  (hY : ConnectedSpace Y) (hX : ∀ y : Y, IsConnected (p ⁻¹' {y})) :
  ConnectedSpace X
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature mirrors the informal statement: `IsQuotientMap p` captures Munkres's notion of a quotient map (which is surjective), `ConnectedSpace Y` says Y is connected, and the hypothesis `∀ y, IsConnected (p ⁻¹' {y})` says each fiber is connected. The conclusion `ConnectedSpace X` matches "X is connected." One subtle point: Mathlib's `IsConnected` requires nonemptiness, but since `IsQuotientMap` includes surjectivity, every fiber is automatically nonempty, so this matches Munkres's convention without adding a real extra hypothesis. The quantifier structure and types align with the informal claim.

### proofnet-305

**Informal statement**

> Let $f \colon X \rightarrow X$ be continuous. Show that if $X = [0, 1]$, there is a point $x$ such that $f(x) = x$. (The point $x$ is called a fixed point of $f$.)

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_24_3a (f : I → I) (hf : Continuous f) :
  ∃ (x : I), f x = x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

In Mathlib, `I` denotes `unitInterval`, which is exactly `[0,1] ⊆ ℝ` with the subspace topology. The Lean signature takes a continuous function `f : I → I` and asserts the existence of a fixed point `x : I` with `f x = x`, which is precisely the informal claim. The quantifier structure (existence of a fixed point), the domain/codomain ([0,1]), and the continuity hypothesis all match. No extra or missing assumptions are present.

### proofnet-306

**Informal statement**

> Let $G$ be a topological group; let $C$ be the component of $G$ containing the identity element $e$. Show that $C$ is a normal subgroup of $G$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_25_9 {G : Type*} [TopologicalSpace G] [Group G]
  [IsTopologicalGroup G] (C : Set G) (hC : C = connectedComponent (1 : G)) :
  ∃ H : Subgroup G, (H : Set G) = C ∧ H.Normal
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that the connected component of the identity in a topological group is a normal subgroup. The Lean signature takes a topological group G, defines C to be the connected component of 1 (the identity in multiplicative notation), and asserts the existence of a subgroup H of G whose underlying set is exactly C and which is normal. This is the standard way to encode "the set C is a normal subgroup": exhibiting a Subgroup structure on the set together with a Normal instance. The hypotheses (TopologicalSpace, Group, IsTopologicalGroup) match "topological group", and 1 corresponds to the identity element e. No extra assumptions (e.g., Hausdorff, T2, etc.) are imposed, and nothing is missing. The encoding is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-307

**Informal statement**

> Let $p: X \rightarrow Y$ be a closed continuous surjective map such that $p^{-1}(\{y\})$ is compact, for each $y \in Y$. (Such a map is called a perfect map.) Show that if $Y$ is compact, then $X$ is compact.

theorem Munkres_exercise_26_12 {X Y : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [TopologicalSpace Y]

  (hY : CompactSpace Y) : CompactSpace X

The conclusion CompactSpace X matches "X is compact". The types X and Y are arbitrary topological spaces, matching the informal generality. The hypothesis names are duplicated (`hp` used twice) but this is just a naming issue and doesn't change the logical content. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-309

**Informal statement**

> Let $(X, d)$ be a metric space. If $f: X \rightarrow X$ satisfies the condition $d(f(x), f(y))=d(x, y)$ for all $x, y \in X$, then $f$ is called an isometry of $X$. Show that if $f$ is an isometry and $X$ is compact, then $f$ is bijective and hence a homeomorphism.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_28_6 {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
  [CompactSpace X] {f : X → X} (hf : Isometry f) :
  Continuous f ∧ Bijective f ∧ IsOpenMap f
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove f is bijective and a homeomorphism. The Lean signature asserts Continuous f ∧ Bijective f ∧ IsOpenMap f. A continuous bijection that is also an open map is precisely a homeomorphism (the open-map condition gives continuity of the inverse). The hypotheses match: X is a metric space, X is compact (CompactSpace), and f is an isometry. So the Lean conjunction is logically equivalent to "f is bijective and a homeomorphism," matching the informal claim.

theorem Munkres_exercise_29_4_corrected :
    ¬ LocallyCompactSpace (ℕ →ᵇ I)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that the countable product [0,1]^ω with the uniform topology is not locally compact. In Lean, `ℕ →ᵇ I` denotes bounded continuous functions from ℕ (with discrete topology) to I = [0,1]. Since ℕ is discrete, every function ℕ → I is continuous, and since I is bounded, every such function is bounded. The bounded function space carries the sup-metric topology, which is exactly the uniform topology on I^ℕ = [0,1]^ω. The conclusion `¬ LocallyCompactSpace` matches "not locally compact". Thus the Lean statement captures the same claim.

### proofnet-316

**Informal statement**

> Show that every locally compact Hausdorff space is completely regular.

theorem Munkres_exercise_33_7 {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
  (hX : LocallyCompactSpace X) (hX' : T2Space X) :

### proofnet-317

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be a compact Hausdorff space that is the union of the closed subspaces $X_1$ and $X_2$. If $X_1$ and $X_2$ are metrizable, show that $X$ is metrizable.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_34_9
  (X : Type*) [TopologicalSpace X] [CompactSpace X] [T2Space X]
  (X1 X2 : Set X) (hX1 : IsClosed X1) (hX2 : IsClosed X2)
  (hX : X1 ∪ X2 = univ) (hX1m : MetrizableSpace X1)
  (hX2m : MetrizableSpace X2) : MetrizableSpace X
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures exactly the informal hypotheses: X is a topological space with CompactSpace and T2Space (compact Hausdorff), X1 and X2 are closed subsets whose union is the whole space (X1 ∪ X2 = univ), and each carries a MetrizableSpace instance on the subtype (i.e., they are metrizable as subspaces). The conclusion MetrizableSpace X matches "X is metrizable." The quantifier structure (universal over X) and types align with the informal statement, so this is a faithful encoding.

### proofnet-318  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_43_2_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $(X, d_X)$ and $(Y, d_Y)$ be metric spaces; let $Y$ be complete. Let $A \subset X$. Show that if $f \colon A \rightarrow Y$ is uniformly continuous, then $f$ can be uniquely extended to a continuous function $g \colon \bar{A} \rightarrow Y$, and $g$ is uniformly continuous.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_43_2_corrected {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
    {Y : Type*} [MetricSpace Y] [CompleteSpace Y] (A : Set X)
    (f : X → Y) (hf : UniformContinuousOn f A) :
    ∃! (g : closure A → Y),
      Continuous g ∧ UniformContinuous g ∧
        ∀ x : A, g ⟨x, subset_closure x.property⟩ = f x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement captures the same mathematical content. (1) Modeling f as `X → Y` with `UniformContinuousOn f A` rather than `A → Y` is the standard Mathlib idiom and is logically equivalent: any uniformly continuous map on A extends arbitrarily to X (when Y is nonempty; otherwise both sides are vacuous). (2) The conclusion `∃! g : closure A → Y, Continuous g ∧ UniformContinuous g ∧ (g agrees with f on A)` matches "uniquely extended to a continuous function which is uniformly continuous": since A is dense in closure(A) and Y is Hausdorff, uniqueness of a continuous agreeing extension is automatic, so packaging UniformContinuous inside the ∃! predicate is equivalent to the informal "unique continuous extension, which happens to be uniformly continuous." (3) `Continuous g` is redundant given `UniformContinuous g` but does not change meaning. (4) Completeness of Y is correctly assumed; the domain `closure A` and the agreement condition on the subtype A are the right encodings. No hypotheses are silently added or dropped.

### proofnet-319

**Informal statement**

> Prove that a set $U \subset M$ is open if and only if none of its points are limits of its complement.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_26 {M : Type*} [TopologicalSpace M]
  (U : Set M) : IsOpen U ↔ ∀ x ∈ U, ¬ ClusterPt x (𝓟 Uᶜ)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says U is open iff no point of U is a limit point of its complement Uᶜ. The Lean version states `IsOpen U ↔ ∀ x ∈ U, ¬ ClusterPt x (𝓟 Uᶜ)`, where `ClusterPt x (𝓟 Uᶜ)` is exactly Mathlib's notion of x being a cluster/accumulation/limit point of the set Uᶜ (every neighborhood of x meets Uᶜ). The quantifier "for all x in U" matches "none of its points", and the topological space M is left general, matching the informal "set U ⊂ M" where M is a metric/topological space. This is the standard characterization and the encoding is logically equivalent.
