### proofnet-321

**Informal statement**

> Assume that $A, B$ are compact, disjoint, nonempty subsets of $M$. Prove that there are $a_0 \in A$ and $b_0 \in B$ such that for all $a \in A$ and $b \in B$ we have $d(a_0, b_0) \leq d(a, b)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_46 {M : Type*} [MetricSpace M]
  {A B : Set M} (hA : IsCompact A) (hB : IsCompact B)
  (hAB : Disjoint A B) (hA₀ : A ≠ ∅) (hB₀ : B ≠ ∅) :
  ∃ a₀ b₀, a₀ ∈ A ∧ b₀ ∈ B ∧ ∀ (a : M) (b : M),
  a ∈ A → b ∈ B → dist a₀ b₀ ≤ dist a b
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature mirrors the informal statement closely: M is a metric space, A and B are subsets with IsCompact, Disjoint, and nonempty (A ≠ ∅, B ≠ ∅) hypotheses, matching "compact, disjoint, nonempty subsets of M." The conclusion asserts the existence of a₀ ∈ A and b₀ ∈ B such that for all a ∈ A and b ∈ B, dist a₀ b₀ ≤ dist a b, which is exactly the informal claim. The quantifier structure (∃ a₀ b₀, ∀ a b) and the inequality direction match. No extra or missing hypotheses materially change the meaning.

### proofnet-322  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Pugh_exercise_2_92_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Give a direct proof that the nested decreasing intersection of nonempty covering compact sets is nonempty.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_92_corrected {α : Type*} [TopologicalSpace α] [T2Space α]
    {s : ℕ → Set α}
    (hcomp : ∀ i, IsCompact (s i))
    (hne : ∀ i, (s i).Nonempty)
    (hmono : ∀ i, s i ⊇ s (i + 1)) :
    (⋂ i, s i).Nonempty
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is the standard nested compact sets theorem: a decreasing sequence of nonempty compact sets has nonempty intersection. The Lean signature captures this with `hcomp` (each `s i` compact), `hne` (each nonempty), `hmono` (decreasing: `s i ⊇ s (i+1)`), and concludes `(⋂ i, s i).Nonempty`. The T2Space assumption is mild and consistent with Pugh's setting (compact sets in Hausdorff spaces are closed, ensuring the standard proof works); the informal statement implicitly works in such ambient spaces. Quantifier structure and types match exactly. No strengthening or weakening is introduced.

### proofnet-323

**Informal statement**

> Assume that $f \colon \mathbb{R} \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ satisfies $|f(t)-f(x)| \leq|t-x|^{2}$ for all $t, x$. Prove that $f$ is constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_3_1 {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hf : ∀ x y, |f x - f y| ≤ |x - y| ^ 2) :
  ∃ c, f = λ _x => c
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says f is constant, given |f(t)-f(x)| ≤ |t-x|^2. The Lean statement asserts ∃ c, f = (fun _ => c), which is exactly the definition of f being a constant function. The hypothesis ∀ x y, |f x - f y| ≤ |x - y|^2 matches the informal hypothesis (with variable names swapped, which doesn't matter due to symmetry). The types are ℝ → ℝ as in the informal statement. ### proofnet-326  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_1998_b6`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that, for any integers $a, b, c$, there exists a positive integer $n$ such that $\sqrt{n^3+a n^2+b n+c}$ is not an integer.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_1998_b6 (a b c : ℤ) :
    ∃ n : ℤ, n > 0 ∧ ¬ ∃ m : ℤ, Real.sqrt (n^3 + a*n^2 + b*n + c) = m
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim: for any integers a,b,c, there exists a positive integer n such that √(P(n)) is not an integer, where P(n)=n³+an²+bn+c. The Lean statement quantifies a,b,c over ℤ, then asserts ∃ n:ℤ with n>0 and no integer m with Real.sqrt(P(n)) = m, i.e., the square root is not equal to any integer. The integer n with n>0 corresponds exactly to "positive integer," and "¬∃ m:ℤ, Real.sqrt(...) = m" expresses "the square root is not an integer." There is a minor edge case: Real.sqrt of a negative radicand returns 0 (an integer) in Lean, whereas informally a negative radicand has no real square root and so is "not an integer"; but since the claim is existential over n>0 and one can always take n large enough that P(n)>0, this does not change the meaning of the overall statement. The encoding is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-327  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_1999_b4`

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ be a real function with a continuous third derivative such that $f(x), f^{\prime}(x), f^{\prime \prime}(x), f^{\prime \prime \prime}(x)$ are positive for all $x$. Suppose that $f^{\prime \prime \prime}(x) \leq f(x)$ for all $x$. Show that $f^{\prime}(x)<2 f(x)$ for all $x$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_1999_b4 (f : ℝ → ℝ) (hf: ContDiff ℝ 3 f)
  (hf1 : ∀ n ≤ 3, ∀ x : ℝ, iteratedDeriv n f x > 0)
  (hf2 : ∀ x : ℝ, iteratedDeriv 3 f x ≤ f x) :
  ∀ x : ℝ, deriv f x < 2 * f x
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement matches the informal one. `ContDiff ℝ 3 f` encodes "continuous third derivative". The hypothesis `∀ n ≤ 3, ∀ x, iteratedDeriv n f x > 0` covers n = 0, 1, 2, 3, i.e., f, f', f'', f''' all positive (note iteratedDeriv 0 f = f, iteratedDeriv 1 f = deriv f, etc.). The hypothesis `iteratedDeriv 3 f x ≤ f x` is exactly f'''(x) ≤ f(x). The conclusion `deriv f x < 2 * f x` is f'(x) < 2 f(x) for all x. Quantifiers and types align.

### proofnet-328  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2001_a5`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that there are unique positive integers $a, n$ such that $a^{n+1}-(a+1)^n=2001$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Putnam_exercise_2001_a5 :
    ∃! p : ℕ × ℕ, p.1 > 0 ∧ p.2 > 0 ∧ p.1^(p.2 + 1) - (p.1 + 1)^(p.2) = 2001
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes the pair (a, n) as p : ℕ × ℕ with p.1, p.2 > 0, exactly matching "positive integers a, n". The equation a^(n+1) - (a+1)^n = 2001 uses ℕ truncated subtraction, but since the required value is the positive number 2001, any solution in ℕ must satisfy a^(n+1) ≥ (a+1)^n + 2001, in which case the truncated subtraction agrees with integer subtraction; conversely any integer solution gives a ℕ solution. So the solution sets coincide. The use of ∃! correctly captures "unique" existence of such a pair. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-329  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2014_a5_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $P_n(x)=1+2 x+3 x^2+\cdots+n x^{n-1} .$ Prove that the polynomials $P_j(x)$ and $P_k(x)$ are relatively prime for all positive integers $j$ and $k$ with $j \neq k$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Putnam_exercise_2014_a5_corrected
    (P : ℕ → Polynomial ℚ)
    (hP : ∀ n, P n = ∑ i : Fin n, (↑(i : ℕ) + 1) * Polynomial.X ^ (i : ℕ)) :
    ∀ {j k : ℕ}, 0 < j → 0 < k → j ≠ k → IsCoprime (P j) (P k)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The definition `P n = ∑ i : Fin n, (i+1) * X^i` expands to (1) + 2X + 3X^2 + ... + n X^{n-1}, matching P_n(x). The conclusion `IsCoprime (P j) (P k)` in ℚ[X] is the standard Mathlib encoding of "relatively prime polynomials". The hypotheses `0 < j`, `0 < k`, `j ≠ k` correspond exactly to "positive integers j and k with j ≠ k". The quantification ∀ {j k} matches the universal claim. Working over ℚ is appropriate (the polynomial has integer coefficients but coprimality in ℚ[X] is the natural statement, and equivalent to coprimality in ℤ[X] via Gauss's lemma since contents are 1). No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-330

**Informal statement**

> Let $f: \mathbb{R} \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ be an infinitely differentiable function satisfying $f(0)=0, f(1)=1$, and $f(x) \geq 0$ for all $x \in$ $\mathbb{R}$. Show that there exist a positive integer $n$ and a real number $x$ such that $f^{(n)}(x)<0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Putnam_exercise_2018_a5 (f : ℝ → ℝ) (hf : ContDiff ℝ ⊤ f)
  (hf0 : f 0 = 0) (hf1 : f 1 = 1) (hf2 : ∀ x, f x ≥ 0) :
  ∃ (n : ℕ+) (x : ℝ), iteratedDeriv n f x < 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature matches the informal statement: `ContDiff ℝ ⊤ f` encodes "infinitely differentiable" (smooth) on ℝ, `hf0`/`hf1` give f(0)=0 and f(1)=1, and `hf2` gives f(x) ≥ 0 for all x ∈ ℝ. The conclusion uses `n : ℕ+` (a positive integer) and `iteratedDeriv n f x < 0`, which is exactly "there exist a positive integer n and a real x with f^{(n)}(x) < 0". Quantifier structure (∃ n, ∃ x) and types (ℝ → ℝ) match. No extra or missing hypotheses.

### proofnet-331  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Putnam_exercise_2018_b4`

**Informal statement**

> Given a real number $a$, we define a sequence by $x_{0}=1$, $x_{1}=x_{2}=a$, and $x_{n+1}=2 x_{n} x_{n-1}-x_{n-2}$ for $n \geq 2$. Prove that if $x_{n}=0$ for some $n$, then the sequence is periodic.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Putnam_exercise_2018_b4 (a : ℝ) (x : ℕ → ℝ) (hx0 : x 0 = 1)
  (hx1 : x 1 = a) (hx2 : x 2 = a)
  (hxn : ∀ n : ℕ, n ≥ 2 → x (n+1) = 2*(x n)*(x (n-1)) - x (n-2))
  (h : ∃ n, x n = 0) :
  ∃ c ≠ 0, Periodic x c
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature mirrors the informal statement closely: it fixes x 0 = 1, x 1 = x 2 = a, and imposes the recurrence x_{n+1} = 2 x_n x_{n-1} - x_{n-2} for n ≥ 2 (matching the informal indexing). The hypothesis ∃ n, x n = 0 corresponds to "x_n = 0 for some n". The conclusion ∃ c ≠ 0, Periodic x c states that the sequence has a nonzero period, which is the standard meaning of "the sequence is periodic." The quantifier structure (universal over a via the parameter, existential over the period) is correct, and no extra hypotheses (such as nonzero a) are silently added or removed.

### proofnet-332

**Informal statement**

> If $r$ is rational $(r \neq 0)$ and $x$ is irrational, prove that $rx$ is irrational.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_1b
(x : ℝ)
(y : ℚ)
(h : y ≠ 0)
: ( Irrational x ) -> Irrational ( x * y )
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says: for nonzero rational r and irrational x, rx is irrational. The Lean signature takes x : ℝ, y : ℚ with y ≠ 0, and concludes that if x is irrational, then x * y is irrational. Multiplication of reals is commutative, so x * y = y * x = rx. The hypothesis y ≠ 0 matches r ≠ 0, and Irrational x captures x being irrational. This is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-334  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_1_8_corrected`

> Prove that no order can be defined in the complex field that turns it into an ordered field.

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_1_8_corrected : ¬ complexHasStrictOrderedRing
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The informal claim is that no ordering can be defined on ℂ making it an ordered field. The Lean signature negates `complexHasStrictOrderedRing`, which presumably asserts the existence of a StrictOrderedRing (equivalently, ordered field) structure on ℂ. Negating this gives "there is no such structure", matching the informal statement. Since ℂ is already a field, a StrictOrderedRing structure on it is equivalent to an ordered field structure. Assuming `complexHasStrictOrderedRing` is the standard "∃ order making ℂ a strict ordered ring/field" predicate, the formalization is faithful. There is mild uncertainty since the definition is not shown, but the naming strongly suggests the intended meaning.

### proofnet-335

**Informal statement**

> If $z_1, \ldots, z_n$ are complex, prove that $|z_1 + z_2 + \ldots + z_n| \leq |z_1| + |z_2| + \cdots + |z_n|$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_12 (n : ℕ) (f : ℕ → ℂ) :
  ‖∑ i ∈ range n, f i‖ ≤ ∑ i ∈ range n, ‖f i‖
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is the triangle inequality for a finite sum of complex numbers. The Lean version states ‖∑ i ∈ range n, f i‖ ≤ ∑ i ∈ range n, ‖f i‖ for any function f : ℕ → ℂ and any n : ℕ. This is logically equivalent: indexing n complex numbers z_1,...,z_n by a function f : ℕ → ℂ over Finset.range n captures exactly the same finite sum. The norm ‖·‖ on ℂ equals the absolute value. So the Lean statement faithfully encodes the claim.

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $|\mathbf{x}+\mathbf{y}|^{2}+|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{y}|^{2}=2|\mathbf{x}|^{2}+2|\mathbf{y}|^{2}$ if $\mathbf{x} \in R^{k}$ and $\mathbf{y} \in R^{k}$.

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_1_17
  (n : ℕ)
  (x y : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n)) -- R^n
  : ‖x + y‖^2 + ‖x - y‖^2 = 2*‖x‖^2 + 2*‖y‖^2
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement is the parallelogram identity in R^k for arbitrary x, y. The Lean signature universally quantifies over n : ℕ and over x, y in EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin n), which is exactly R^n with the standard Euclidean norm. The conclusion ‖x+y‖² + ‖x-y‖² = 2‖x‖² + 2‖y‖² matches the informal equation. The renaming k → n is cosmetic, and no extra hypotheses are imposed nor required ones omitted.

### proofnet-340

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $E\subset\mathbb{R}^k$ is uncountable, and let $P$ be the set of condensation points of $E$. Prove that at most countably many points of $E$ are not in $P$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_2_27b (k : ℕ) (E P : Set (EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin k)))
  (hE : ¬ Set.Countable E)
  (hP : P = {x | ∀ U ∈ 𝓝 x, ¬ Set.Countable (U ∩ E)}) :
  Set.Countable (E \ P)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says E ⊂ ℝ^k is uncountable, P is the set of condensation points of E, and asks to show at most countably many points of E are not in P, i.e., E \ P is countable. The Lean signature uses EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin k) which is the standard formalization of ℝ^k, assumes E is uncountable (¬ Countable E), defines P as the set of points x such that every neighborhood U of x has uncountable intersection with E — this is the standard definition of condensation point of E. The conclusion Set.Countable (E \ P) matches "at most countably many points of E are not in P." The encoding is faithful.

### proofnet-342

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\lim_{n \rightarrow \infty}\sqrt{n^2 + n} -n = 1/2$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_2a
  : Tendsto (λ (n : ℝ) => (sqrt (n^2 + n) - n)) atTop (𝓝 (1/2))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that sqrt(n²+n) - n tends to 1/2 as n → ∞. The Lean version expresses this as a limit of a real-valued function with n : ℝ tending to atTop in ℝ, with the limit being 1/2 in the standard topology. While the informal "n" is often read as a natural number, the function sqrt(n²+n) - n is real-analytic and the limit value and behavior are identical; the real-variable version implies the natural-variable version and is the standard Mathlib encoding. The square root, subtraction, and target value 1/2 all match. No extra hypotheses are introduced and the conclusion is the exact equality of the limit. This is the standard faithful formalization.

### proofnet-344

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the convergence of $\Sigma a_{n}$ implies the convergence of $\sum \frac{\sqrt{a_{n}}}{n}$ if $a_n\geq 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_7
  (a : ℕ → ℝ)
  (hnneg : ∀ n, a n ≥ 0)
  (h : ∃ y, (Tendsto (λ n => (∑ i ∈ (range n), a i)) atTop (𝓝 y))) :
  ∃ y, Tendsto (λ n => (∑ i ∈ (range n), sqrt (a i) / (i + 1))) atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures the standard reading of Rudin's exercise: a nonnegative sequence whose series converges, with conclusion that the related series converges. The use of `i + 1` in the denominator is the conventional Lean device to avoid division by zero when indexing from 0; it corresponds to summing over n ≥ 1 in the informal version. The hypothesis `∀ n, a n ≥ 0` matches `a_n ≥ 0`, and the existence of a limit of partial sums is the standard formalization of "Σ a_n converges". The conclusion likewise asserts existence of a limit of partial sums of √(a_i)/(i+1), which (under reindexing m = i+1) is the convergence of Σ √(a_n)/n for a nonnegative sequence — the same mathematical content as the informal claim.

### proofnet-345  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_3_13_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the Cauchy product of two absolutely convergent series converges absolutely.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_13_corrected
   (a b : ℕ → ℝ)
   (ha : ∃ y, Tendsto (fun n => ∑ i ∈ range n, |a i|) atTop (𝓝 y))
   (hb : ∃ y, Tendsto (fun n => ∑ i ∈ range n, |b i|) atTop (𝓝 y)) :
   ∃ y,
     Tendsto
       (fun n =>
         ∑ i ∈ range n,
           |∑ j ∈ range (i + 1), a j * b (i - j)|)
       atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that if Σa_n and Σb_n converge absolutely, then their Cauchy product Σc_n with c_n = Σ_{j=0}^{n} a_j b_{n-j} also converges absolutely. The Lean hypotheses ha, hb encode that the partial sums of |a i| and |b i| converge (i.e., absolute convergence of the two series). The conclusion asserts the existence of a limit for the partial sums of |Σ_{j ∈ range(i+1)} a_j · b(i-j)|, which is exactly Σ |c_i| converges — the absolute convergence of the Cauchy product. The index manipulation `range (i+1)` and `b (i-j)` correctly captures j = 0..i with b_{i-j}. Restricting to ℝ matches the most natural reading of the informal statement, and quantifier structure/types are correct. This is a logically equivalent formalization.

### proofnet-346

**Informal statement**

> If $\left\{E_{n}\right\}$ is a sequence of closed nonempty and bounded sets in a complete metric space $X$, if $E_{n} \supset E_{n+1}$, and if $\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \operatorname{diam} E_{n}=0,$ then $\bigcap_{1}^{\infty} E_{n}$ consists of exactly one point.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_21
  {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X] [CompleteSpace X]
  (E : ℕ → Set X)
  (hEcl : ∀ n, IsClosed (E n))
  (hEne : ∀ n, (E n).Nonempty)
  (hEbdd : ∀ n, Bornology.IsBounded (E n))
  (hE : ∀ n, E n ⊇ E (n + 1))
  (hE' : Tendsto (λ n => Metric.diam (E n)) atTop (𝓝 0)) :
  ∃ a, Set.iInter E = {a}
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all hypotheses of the informal statement: X is a complete metric space, each E n is closed, nonempty, and bounded, the sets are nested (E n ⊇ E (n+1)), and diam(E n) → 0. The conclusion `∃ a, Set.iInter E = {a}` asserts the intersection equals a singleton, which is exactly "consists of exactly one point". The quantifier structure and types align with the informal claim, so the formalization is logically equivalent.

### proofnet-347

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is a real function defined on $\mathbb{R}$ which satisfies $\lim_{h \rightarrow 0} f(x + h) - f(x - h) = 0$ for every $x \in \mathbb{R}$. Show that $f$ does not need to be continuous.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_1a
  : ∃ (f : ℝ → ℝ), (∀ (x : ℝ), Tendsto (λ y => f (x + y) - f (x - y)) (𝓝 0) (𝓝 0)) ∧ ¬ Continuous f
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show that the property "lim_{h→0}(f(x+h)-f(x-h))=0 for every x" does not force f to be continuous, i.e. to exhibit some f : ℝ → ℝ satisfying the limit condition but not continuous. The Lean signature asserts ∃ f : ℝ → ℝ such that for every x the function y ↦ f(x+y) − f(x−y) tends to 0 as y → 0, and f is not continuous. The quantifier structure (∃ f, ∀ x), the limit encoding via Tendsto with 𝓝 0 → 𝓝 0, and the negation of Continuous f all directly correspond to the informal claim. No extra hypotheses (e.g. measurability, boundedness) are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-348

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ be a continuous real function on a metric space $X$. Let $Z(f)$ (the zero set of $f$ ) be the set of all $p \in X$ at which $f(p)=0$. Prove that $Z(f)$ is closed.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_3
  {α : Type} [MetricSpace α]
  (f : α → ℝ) (h : Continuous f) (z : Set α) (g : z = f⁻¹' {0})
  : IsClosed z
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature takes a metric space α, a continuous function f : α → ℝ, and defines z as the preimage f⁻¹'{0}, which is exactly Z(f) = {p ∈ X : f(p) = 0}. The conclusion IsClosed z matches the informal claim that Z(f) is closed. The hypotheses (MetricSpace, Continuous f) match the informal setup, and no extra assumptions are introduced. This is a faithful formalization.

### proofnet-349

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ and $g$ be continuous mappings of a metric space $X$ into a metric space $Y$, and let $P$ be a dense subset of $X$. Prove that if $g(p) = f(p)$ for all $p \in P$ then $g(p) = f(p)$ for all $p \in X$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_4b
  {α : Type} [MetricSpace α]
  {β : Type} [MetricSpace β]
  (f g : α → β)
  (s : Set α)
  (h₁ : Continuous f)
  (h₂ : Continuous g)
  (h₃ : Dense s)
  (h₄ : ∀ x ∈ s, f x = g x)
  : f = g
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show f(p) = g(p) for all p ∈ X given that f, g are continuous maps between metric spaces and agree on a dense subset P. The Lean signature introduces metric spaces α and β, continuous functions f and g, a set s with `Dense s`, and the hypothesis that f x = g x on s, concluding `f = g`. By function extensionality, `f = g` is equivalent to ∀ x, f x = g x, matching the informal conclusion. All hypotheses (continuity of both, density of s, agreement on s) correspond exactly. No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-350

**Informal statement**

> Show that there exist a set $E \subset \mathbb{R}$ and a real continuous function $f$ defined on $E$, such that there does not exist a continuous real function $g$ on $\mathbb{R}$ such that $g(x)=f(x)$ for all $x \in E$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_5b
  : ∃ (E : Set ℝ) (f : ℝ → ℝ), (ContinuousOn f E) ∧
  (¬ ∃ (g : ℝ → ℝ), Continuous g ∧ ∀ x ∈ E, f x = g x)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks for a set E ⊂ ℝ and a continuous function f on E that does not extend to a continuous function on all of ℝ. The Lean version provides E : Set ℝ, f : ℝ → ℝ with ContinuousOn f E (which captures "f continuous on E" — values of f outside E are irrelevant since the extension condition only constrains x ∈ E), and asserts no continuous g : ℝ → ℝ satisfies g(x) = f(x) for all x ∈ E. Encoding f as a total function while only requiring continuity on E is logically equivalent to the informal "f defined on E" since any partial continuous function on E can be extended set-theoretically (without continuity) to a total ℝ → ℝ, and conversely. The non-extension condition matches exactly. Hence the formalization is faithful.

### proofnet-351

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ be a real uniformly continuous function on the bounded set $E$ in $R^{1}$. Prove that $f$ is bounded on $E$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_8a
  (E : Set ℝ) (f : ℝ → ℝ) (hf : UniformContinuousOn f E)
  (hE : Bornology.IsBounded E) : Bornology.IsBounded (Set.image f E)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement assumes E ⊆ ℝ is bounded (Bornology.IsBounded E), that f : ℝ → ℝ is uniformly continuous on E (UniformContinuousOn f E), and concludes that f '' E is bounded (Bornology.IsBounded (Set.image f E)). This is exactly the content of Rudin's exercise: "f bounded on E" is precisely "f(E) is a bounded subset of ℝ". The hypothesis and quantifier structure match the informal claim, no extra assumptions (e.g., compactness, closedness) are introduced, and no required hypotheses are dropped.

### proofnet-354

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $K$ and $F$ are disjoint sets in a metric space $X, K$ is compact, $F$ is closed. Prove that there exists $\delta>0$ such that $d(p, q)>\delta$ if $p \in K, q \in F$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_21a {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
  (K F : Set X) (hK : IsCompact K) (hF : IsClosed F) (hKF : Disjoint K F) :
  ∃ (δ : ℝ), δ > 0 ∧ ∀ (p q : X), p ∈ K → q ∈ F → dist p q > δ
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The conclusion asserts the existence of δ > 0 such that for all p ∈ K and q ∈ F, dist p q > δ, which is exactly the informal claim. The quantifier structure (∃δ > 0, ∀ p q, ...) matches "there exists δ > 0 such that d(p,q) > δ if p ∈ K, q ∈ F." No extra or missing assumptions.

### proofnet-355

**Informal statement**

> Let $f$ be defined for all real $x$, and suppose that $|f(x)-f(y)| \leq (x-y)^{2}$ for all real $x$ and $y$. Prove that $f$ is constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_1
  {f : ℝ → ℝ} (hf : ∀ x y : ℝ, |(f x - f y)| ≤ (x - y) ^ 2) :
  ∃ c, f = λ _x => c
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

The informal statement says f is constant, given |f(x)-f(y)| ≤ (x-y)² for all x,y. The Lean signature assumes exactly this hypothesis and concludes ∃ c, f = λ _ => c, which is precisely the assertion that f is a constant function. The types (ℝ → ℝ) and quantifier structure match.
