### proofnet-357

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is defined and differentiable for every $x>0$, and $f^{\prime}(x) \rightarrow 0$ as $x \rightarrow+\infty$. Put $g(x)=f(x+1)-f(x)$. Prove that $g(x) \rightarrow 0$ as $x \rightarrow+\infty$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_5
  {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hfd : DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Ioi 0))
  (hf : Tendsto (deriv f) atTop (𝓝 0)) :
  Tendsto (λ x => f (x + 1) - f x) atTop (𝓝 0)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all the informal hypotheses and the conclusion. `DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Ioi 0)` matches "f is defined and differentiable for every x > 0". Since `Ioi 0` is open, `DifferentiableOn` on it implies `DifferentiableAt` at every x > 0, so `deriv f x` agrees with the true derivative for x > 0; `Tendsto (deriv f) atTop (𝓝 0)` therefore correctly encodes f'(x) → 0 as x → ∞ (only the tail behavior matters). The conclusion `Tendsto (λ x => f (x+1) - f x) atTop (𝓝 0)` is exactly g(x) → 0 as x → ∞. No extra hypotheses are imposed and none are dropped.

### proofnet-358  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_5_7_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f^{\prime}(x), g^{\prime}(x)$ exist, $g^{\prime}(x) \neq 0$, and $f(x)=g(x)=0$. Prove that $\lim _{t \rightarrow x} \frac{f(t)}{g(t)}=\frac{f^{\prime}(x)}{g^{\prime}(x)}.$

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_5_7_corrected
  {f g : ℝ → ℝ} {x : ℝ}
  (hf' : DifferentiableAt ℝ f x)
  (hg' : DifferentiableAt ℝ g x)
  (hg'_ne_0 : deriv g x ≠ 0)
  (f0 : f x = 0) (g0 : g x = 0) :
  Tendsto (λ t => f t / g t) (𝓝[≠] x) (𝓝 (deriv f x / deriv g x))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature captures all the hypotheses of the informal statement: `DifferentiableAt ℝ f x` and `DifferentiableAt ℝ g x` correspond to existence of f'(x) and g'(x); `deriv g x ≠ 0` matches g'(x) ≠ 0; and `f x = 0`, `g x = 0` match f(x)=g(x)=0. The conclusion uses `Tendsto ... (𝓝[≠] x) (𝓝 (deriv f x / deriv g x))`, i.e. the limit through the punctured neighborhood of x, which is exactly Rudin's convention for lim_{t→x} (t ≠ x). The value of the limit f'(x)/g'(x) is encoded as `deriv f x / deriv g x`, which equals the Fréchet derivative under `DifferentiableAt`. No hypothesis is added or dropped, so this is a strictly faithful formalization.

### proofnet-359

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is a real, three times differentiable function on $[-1,1]$, such that $f(-1)=0, \quad f(0)=0, \quad f(1)=1, \quad f^{\prime}(0)=0 .$ Prove that $f^{(3)}(x) \geq 3$ for some $x \in(-1,1)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_17
  {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hf' : DifferentiableOn ℝ f (Icc (-1) 1))
  (hf'' : DifferentiableOn ℝ (deriv f) (Icc (-1) 1))
  (hf''' : DifferentiableOn ℝ (deriv (deriv f)) (Icc (-1) 1))
  (hf0 : f (-1) = 0)
  (hf1 : f 0 = 0)
  (hf2 : f 1 = 1)
  (hf3 : deriv f 0 = 0) :
  ∃ x, x ∈ Ioo (-1 : ℝ) 1 ∧ deriv (deriv (deriv f)) x ≥ 3
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes the four point conditions (f(-1)=0, f(0)=0, f(1)=1, f'(0)=0) verbatim, and asserts the existence of x in the open interval (-1,1) where the third derivative is ≥ 3, matching the informal claim. The three differentiability hypotheses on Icc (-1) 1 capture "three times differentiable on [-1,1]". A minor technicality is the use of `deriv` (the global derivative) rather than `derivWithin` for the higher-derivative hypotheses, but on the interior of Icc these agree, and since the conclusion is at an interior point this does not change the mathematical content. Overall the Lean theorem is logically equivalent to the informal statement.

### proofnet-361

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the power series $\sum nz^n$ does not converge on any point of the unit circle.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_19a (z : ℂ) (hz : ‖z‖ = 1) (s : ℕ → ℂ)
    (h : s = (λ n => ∑ i ∈ (range n), i * z ^ i)) :
    ¬ ∃ y, Tendsto s atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement fixes an arbitrary z ∈ ℂ on the unit circle (‖z‖ = 1) and asserts that the sequence of partial sums s_n = ∑_{i<n} i·z^i has no limit in ℂ, which is precisely the assertion that ∑ n z^n diverges at every point of the unit circle. The quantifier "for any point on the unit circle" is captured by the universally quantified z with hypothesis ‖z‖ = 1, and "does not converge" is captured by ¬∃ y, Tendsto s atTop (𝓝 y). The partial sum encoding via Finset.range n with summand i · z^i correctly represents the series ∑ n z^n. No extra hypotheses are added and none are dropped.

### proofnet-362

**Informal statement**

> Prove that the power series $\sum z^n/n$ converges at every point of the unit circle except $z = 1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_19c (z : ℂ) (hz : ‖z‖ = 1) (s : ℕ → ℂ)
    (h : s = (λ n => ∑ i ∈ (range n), z ^ (i + 1) / (i + 1))) :
    (∃ z₀, Tendsto s atTop (𝓝 z₀)) ↔ z ≠ 1
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement encodes the partial sums s_n = ∑_{i=0}^{n-1} z^(i+1)/(i+1) = z + z²/2 + ... + z^n/n, exactly the series ∑ z^n/n (from n=1). It restricts to z on the unit circle (‖z‖=1) and asserts that the sequence of partial sums has a limit iff z ≠ 1. The informal phrasing "converges at every point of the unit circle except z = 1" naturally reads as: convergence holds precisely when z ≠ 1 (it converges for z≠1, and at z=1 it is the harmonic series, which diverges). The iff captures both directions, matching the "except" reading. Existence of a limit (∃ z₀, Tendsto s atTop (𝓝 z₀)) is the standard encoding of sequence convergence. No extra hypotheses or weakened conclusions are introduced.

### proofnet-363  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_2_2`

**Informal statement**

> Show that $\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\sin x}{x} d x=\frac{\pi}{2}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_2_2 :
    Tendsto (fun y => ∫ x in (0 : ℝ)..y, Real.sin x / x) atTop (𝓝 (Real.pi / 2))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts the improper integral ∫₀^∞ sin(x)/x dx = π/2, which by definition means the limit as y → ∞ of ∫₀^y sin(x)/x dx equals π/2. The Lean signature expresses exactly this: `Tendsto (fun y => ∫ x in 0..y, sin x / x) atTop (𝓝 (π/2))`, using the interval integral and the limit at infinity. This matches the standard meaning of an improper Riemann integral, and π/2 is the correct value. No hypotheses are added or omitted, and the quantifier structure (limit of partial integrals) faithfully captures the improper integral.

### proofnet-365  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_3_4`

**Informal statement**

> Show that $ \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{x \sin x}{x^2 + a^2} dx = \pi e^{-a}$ for $a > 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_3_4 (a : ℝ) (ha : 0 < a) :
    Tendsto (λ y => ∫ x in -y..y, x * Real.sin x / (x ^ 2 + a ^ 2))
    atTop (𝓝 (Real.pi * (Real.exp (-a))))
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is the improper integral ∫_{-∞}^{∞} x sin x/(x²+a²) dx = π e^{-a}. Since |x sin x|/(x²+a²) ~ |sin x|/|x| is not Lebesgue integrable, the integral is conditionally convergent and is naturally interpreted as the symmetric limit lim_{y→∞} ∫_{-y}^{y}. The Lean signature encodes precisely this: Tendsto of ∫_{-y}^{y} as y → ∞ to π·e^{-a}, with the same hypothesis a > 0. The integrand and target value match exactly. This is the standard formalization of an improper symmetric integral, so the Lean version is logically equivalent to the informal claim.

### proofnet-366

**Informal statement**

> Prove that all entire functions that are also injective take the form $f(z) = az + b$, $a, b \in \mathbb{C}$ and $a \neq 0$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_3_14 {f : ℂ → ℂ} (hf : Differentiable ℂ f)
   (hf_inj : Function.Injective f) :
   ∃ (a b : ℂ), f = (λ z => a * z + b) ∧ a ≠ 0
```

**Verdict:** `faithful`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says every entire injective function has the form f(z) = az + b with a ≠ 0. The Lean signature assumes f : ℂ → ℂ is differentiable on all of ℂ (i.e., entire) and injective, and concludes there exist a, b ∈ ℂ with f = (λ z => a*z + b) and a ≠ 0. This matches the informal statement exactly: hypotheses (entire + injective), conclusion (linear form with nonzero leading coefficient), and quantifier structure all align.

## weaker (23)

### proofnet-14  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Artin_exercise_13_4_10_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if a prime integer $p$ has the form $2^r+1$, then it actually has the form $2^{2^k}+1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Artin_exercise_13_4_10_corrected
    {p : ℕ} (hp : Nat.Prime p)
    (h : ∃ r : ℕ, 1 ≤ r ∧ p = 2 ^ r + 1) :
    ∃ k : ℕ, p = 2 ^ (2 ^ k) + 1
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says "if a prime p has the form 2^r + 1, then p = 2^(2^k) + 1," with no lower bound on r. The Lean signature adds the extra hypothesis `1 ≤ r`, restricting the claim to r ≥ 1. This makes the Lean statement strictly weaker: the informal version (as literally written) would also need to cover r = 0, where p = 2^0 + 1 = 2 is prime — a case the Lean theorem excludes (and which is in fact a counterexample to the literal informal statement, since 2 cannot be written as 2^(2^k) + 1). The "_corrected" suffix in the theorem name signals that the author intentionally strengthened the hypothesis to repair the informal claim, so the Lean version proves less than what was asked.

### proofnet-85

**Informal statement**

> Let $R$ be a ring in which $x^3 = x$ for every $x \in R$. Prove that $R$ is commutative.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_4_2_5 {R : Type*} [Ring R]
  (h : ∀ x : R, x ^ 3 = x) : Nonempty (CommRing R)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts that the given ring R (with its given multiplication) is commutative, i.e., ∀ x y : R, x * y = y * x with respect to the supplied `Ring R` instance. The Lean signature instead concludes `Nonempty (CommRing R)`, which only asserts that some commutative ring structure can be put on the type R — this CommRing instance has its own +, *, 0, 1 fields and is not required to agree with the supplied `Ring R` instance. Thus a proof of the informal claim trivially yields `Nonempty (CommRing R)` (use the original operations), but a proof of `Nonempty (CommRing R)` does not establish that the given multiplication is commutative; one could in principle equip R with an unrelated commutative ring structure. Hence the Lean claim is strictly weaker than the informal one.

### proofnet-123  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_26_11_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be a compact Hausdorff space. Let $\mathcal{A}$ be a collection of closed connected subsets of $X$ that is simply ordered by proper inclusion. Then $Y=\bigcap_{A \in \mathcal{A}} A$ is connected.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Munkres_exercise_26_11_corrected
  {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [CompactSpace X] [T2Space X]
  (A : Set (Set X))
  (_hChain : ∀ (a b : Set X), a ∈ A → b ∈ A → a ⊆ b ∨ b ⊆ a)
  (_hClosed : ∀ a ∈ A, IsClosed a) (hA'' : ∀ a ∈ A, IsConnected a)
  (hMin : ∃ a₀ ∈ A, ∀ a ∈ A, a₀ ⊆ a) :
  IsConnected (⋂₀ A)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement adds an extra hypothesis `hMin` asserting that the chain `A` has a minimum element `a₀` (with `a₀ ⊆ a` for all `a ∈ A`). The informal Munkres statement makes no such assumption — the result is interesting precisely when there is no minimum (otherwise ⋂₀ A = a₀ and connectedness is immediate from `hA''`). With this extra hypothesis the Lean theorem becomes a trivial corollary, strictly weaker than the informal claim. (The other ingredients — chain condition via pairwise comparability, closedness, connectedness, and `IsConnected (⋂₀ A)` which also captures nonemptiness as expected for a nested intersection of closed sets in a compact Hausdorff space — are fine.)

### proofnet-133  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_33_8_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be completely regular, let $A$ and $B$ be disjoint closed subsets of $X$. Show that if $A$ is compact, there is a continuous function $f \colon X \rightarrow [0, 1]$ such that $f(A) = \{0\}$ and $f(B) = \{1\}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Munkres_exercise_33_8_corrected
  (X : Type*) [TopologicalSpace X] [CompletelyRegularSpace X]
  (A B : Set X) (_hA : IsClosed A) (hB : IsClosed B)
  (hAB : Disjoint A B) (hAc : IsCompact A)
  (hAn : A.Nonempty) (hBn : B.Nonempty) :
  ∃ (f : X → I), Continuous f ∧ f '' A = {0} ∧ f '' B = {1}
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature adds two extra hypotheses, `hAn : A.Nonempty` and `hBn : B.Nonempty`, that are not present in Munkres's informal statement. The informal statement applies to any disjoint closed A, B (with A compact), including degenerate cases where A or B might be empty (in which case the conclusion is either vacuous, under the "f = 0 on A" reading, or trivially provable by a constant function on the other set). By restricting to nonempty A and B, the Lean theorem only covers a subset of the cases the informal statement claims. The remaining structure (CompletelyRegularSpace, IsClosed A, IsClosed B, Disjoint A B, IsCompact A, continuous f : X → I with images {0} and {1}) matches the informal claim correctly. So Lean is implied by, but does not imply, the informal statement.

### proofnet-137

**Informal statement**

> Let $\|\cdot\|$ be any norm on $\mathbb{R}^{m}$ and let $B=\left\{x \in \mathbb{R}^{m}:\|x\| \leq 1\right\}$. Prove that $B$ is compact.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_41 (m : ℕ) [NormedSpace ℝ ((Fin m) → ℝ)] :
  IsCompact (Metric.closedBall (0 : EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin m)) 1)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement quantifies over ANY norm on ℝ^m and asks to show the closed unit ball in that arbitrary norm is compact. The Lean statement, however, fixes the space to `EuclideanSpace ℝ (Fin m)`, which carries the specific Euclidean (ℓ²) norm, and shows compactness of the closed unit ball in that fixed norm. The unused `[NormedSpace ℝ ((Fin m) → ℝ)]` hypothesis does not introduce a variable norm on the relevant type, and there is no quantification over an arbitrary `NormedAddCommGroup`/norm structure. Hence the Lean theorem only proves a single special case (the Euclidean norm) of the informal claim, making it strictly weaker.

### proofnet-139

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $E$ is an uncountable subset of $\mathbb{R}$. Prove that there exists a point $p \in \mathbb{R}$ at which $E$ condenses.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_2_126 {E : Set ℝ}
  (hE : ¬ Set.Countable E) : ∃ (p : ℝ), ClusterPt p (𝓟 E)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks for a *condensation point* of E: a point p such that every neighborhood of p contains uncountably many points of E. The Lean signature uses `ClusterPt p (𝓟 E)`, which only says p is a cluster (accumulation) point — every neighborhood of p meets E in a point other than p, i.e. contains at least one (hence infinitely many) points of E. This is strictly weaker than condensing: a cluster point need not have uncountably many points of E in every neighborhood. Since any uncountable subset of ℝ has a condensation point, the informal claim implies the Lean claim, but not conversely. The correct Mathlib notion would be something like requiring `¬ Set.Countable (E ∩ U)` for every neighborhood U of p (or `AccPt`/condensation point predicates), not `ClusterPt`.

### proofnet-158

**Informal statement**

> Prove that convergence of $\left\{s_{n}\right\}$ implies convergence of $\left\{\left|s_{n}\right|\right\}$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_1a
  (f : ℕ → ℝ)
  (h : ∃ (a : ℝ), Tendsto (λ (n : ℕ) => f n) atTop (𝓝 a))
  : ∃ (a : ℝ), Tendsto (λ (n : ℕ) => |f n|) atTop (𝓝 a)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

Rudin Exercise 3.1 is stated in the context of Chapter 3 sequences, which in Rudin range over complex numbers (or more generally metric spaces) — the use of |s_n| signals complex sequences in particular. The Lean signature restricts the sequence to ℝ → ℝ, which is a proper special case of the general claim. Proving the result for real sequences does not establish it for complex sequences, so the Lean statement is implied by, but does not imply, the informal claim. The internal structure (hypothesis: ∃ limit a; conclusion: ∃ limit for |s_n|) is otherwise correctly encoded, so the only mismatch is the underlying scalar type, making the formalization logically weaker rather than different.

### proofnet-161

**Informal statement**

> If $\Sigma a_{n}$ converges, and if $\left\{b_{n}\right\}$ is monotonic and bounded, prove that $\Sigma a_{n} b_{n}$ converges.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_8
  (a b : ℕ → ℝ)
  (h1 : ∃ y, (Tendsto (λ n => (∑ i ∈ (range n), a i)) atTop (𝓝 y)))
  (h2 : Monotone b)
  (h3 : Bornology.IsBounded (Set.range b)) :
  ∃ y, Tendsto (λ n => (∑ i ∈ (range n), (a i) * (b i))) atTop (𝓝 y)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement requires {b_n} to be monotonic, which in standard mathematical usage means either monotonically increasing or monotonically decreasing. The Lean signature uses `Monotone b`, which in Mathlib means only monotonically non-decreasing. This omits the decreasing case, so the Lean theorem covers strictly fewer instances than the informal claim. Since the informal statement implies the Lean one (it covers both directions), but the Lean one does not imply the informal claim (it doesn't handle decreasing sequences), the Lean formalization is logically weaker.

### proofnet-162

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $\left\{p_{n}\right\}$ is a Cauchy sequence in a metric space $X$, and some sequence $\left\{p_{n l}\right\}$ converges to a point $p \in X$. Prove that the full sequence $\left\{p_{n}\right\}$ converges to $p$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_3_20 {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
  (p : ℕ → X) (l : ℕ+) (r : X)
  (hp : CauchySeq p)
  (hpl : Tendsto (λ n => p (l * n)) atTop (𝓝 r)) :
  Tendsto p atTop (𝓝 r)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement assumes an arbitrary subsequence {p_{n_k}} of the Cauchy sequence converges to p. The Lean signature, however, restricts the hypothesis to subsequences of the very specific arithmetic form n ↦ p(l·n) for some fixed l : ℕ+. This is only a special case of "some subsequence converges": e.g. a subsequence indexed by n_k = k² (or any non-arithmetic-progression index function) is allowed in the informal statement but cannot be expressed via l·n. Since the Lean hypothesis is strictly more restrictive than the informal hypothesis (while the conclusion is the same), the Lean theorem is implied by the informal claim but does not imply it. Hence the formalization is logically weaker.

### proofnet-171

**Informal statement**

> Assume that $f$ is a continuous real function defined in $(a, b)$ such that $f\left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right) \leq \frac{f(x)+f(y)}{2}$ for all $x, y \in(a, b)$. Prove that $f$ is convex.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_24 {f : ℝ → ℝ}
  (hf : Continuous f) (a b : ℝ) (_hab : a < b)
  (h : ∀ x y : ℝ, a < x → x < b → a < y → y < b → f ((x + y) / 2) ≤ (f x + f y) / 2) :
  ConvexOn ℝ (Set.Ioo a b) f
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement only requires f to be continuous on the open interval (a,b). The Lean signature instead assumes `Continuous f` (continuity on all of ℝ), which is a strictly stronger hypothesis. Thus the Lean theorem applies only to a proper subclass of the functions covered by the informal statement: it does not, for instance, address an f defined only on (a,b) (or extended arbitrarily off it) that is continuous on (a,b) but not on ℝ. The conclusion `ConvexOn ℝ (Set.Ioo a b) f` and the midpoint hypothesis correctly correspond to the informal versions, so the only mismatch is the strengthened continuity assumption, which makes the formalized claim logically weaker than the informal one.

### proofnet-172  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_5_2_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f^{\prime}(x)>0$ in $(a, b)$. Prove that $f$ is strictly increasing in $(a, b)$, and let $g$ be its inverse function. Prove that $g$ is differentiable, and that $g^{\prime}(f(x))=\frac{1}{f^{\prime}(x)} \quad(a < x < b)$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_2_corrected :
    ∀ (a b : ℝ) (f g : ℝ → ℝ),
      (∀ x ∈ Set.Ioo a b, deriv f x > 0) →
      (∀ x ∈ Set.Ioo a b, g (f x) = x) →
      (∀ x ∈ Set.Ioo a b, ContinuousAt g (f x)) →
      (∀ x ∈ Set.Ioo a b, ∀ᶠ y in 𝓝 (f x), f (g y) = y) →
      StrictMonoOn f (Set.Ioo a b) ∧
        DifferentiableOn ℝ g (f '' Set.Ioo a b) ∧
          ∀ x ∈ Set.Ioo a b, deriv g (f x) = 1 / deriv f x
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement only assumes f'(x) > 0 on (a,b); from this one must derive that f is strictly increasing, that an inverse g exists, that g is continuous, and that g is differentiable with the stated derivative. The Lean signature instead takes as additional hypotheses that g is a left inverse of f on (a,b), that g is continuous at each f(x), and that f∘g equals the identity in a neighborhood of f(x) (a local right-inverse condition). These continuity and inverse-function facts are precisely what the informal exercise asks the prover to establish; assuming them turns the theorem into a substantially easier claim. Hence the Lean version is implied by the informal statement (under those assumptions the conclusions follow) but does not imply it, so it is logically weaker.

### proofnet-173

**Informal statement**

> If $C_{0}+\frac{C_{1}}{2}+\cdots+\frac{C_{n-1}}{n}+\frac{C_{n}}{n+1}=0,$ where $C_{0}, \ldots, C_{n}$ are real constants, prove that the equation $C_{0}+C_{1} x+\cdots+C_{n-1} x^{n-1}+C_{n} x^{n}=0$ has at least one real root between 0 and 1.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_5_4 {n : ℕ}
  (C : ℕ → ℝ)
  (hC : ∑ i ∈ (range (n + 1)), (C i) / (i + 1) = 0) :
  ∃ x, x ∈ (Set.Icc (0 : ℝ) 1) ∧ ∑ i ∈ range (n + 1), (C i) * (x^i) = 0
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement (Rudin Exercise 5.4) asserts the existence of a real root strictly between 0 and 1, i.e., in the open interval (0,1). The Lean signature uses `Set.Icc (0 : ℝ) 1`, which is the closed interval [0,1] including the endpoints. This is logically weaker: if C₀ = 0, then x = 0 trivially satisfies the polynomial equation and would satisfy the Lean conclusion without giving a root in the open interval required by the informal claim. Thus the informal statement implies the Lean statement, but not conversely. (The hypothesis and polynomial encoding otherwise match correctly.)

### proofnet-180  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_2_9_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $\Omega$ be a bounded open subset of $\mathbb{C}$, and $\varphi: \Omega \rightarrow \Omega$ a holomorphic function. Prove that if there exists a point $z_{0} \in \Omega$ such that $\varphi\left(z_{0}\right)=z_{0} \quad \text { and } \quad \varphi^{\prime}\left(z_{0}\right)=1$ then $\varphi$ is linear.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Shakarchi_exercise_2_9_corrected
    {f : ℂ → ℂ} {c : ℂ} {R : ℝ} (hR : 0 < R)
    (hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f (Metric.ball c R))
    (h_maps : Set.MapsTo f (Metric.ball c R) (Metric.ball c R))
    (hfix : f c = c) (hderiv : deriv f c = 1) :
    ∃ (f_lin : ℂ →L[ℂ] ℂ), ∀ x ∈ Metric.ball c R, f x = f_lin x
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement assumes Ω is an arbitrary bounded open subset of ℂ. The Lean signature replaces this generality with a metric ball `Metric.ball c R` of radius R > 0 around c, which is a very special kind of bounded open set. So the Lean theorem is a special case of the informal claim and does not establish the result for arbitrary bounded open Ω. Additionally, the conclusion "f equals some ℂ-linear continuous map on the ball" is logically equivalent (given f(c)=c) to f being the identity, which matches the standard reading of "φ is linear" in this exercise, so the conclusion is fine. The strict mismatch is on the domain hypothesis: ball vs. arbitrary bounded open set, making the Lean version strictly weaker.

### proofnet-257

**Informal statement**

> If $G$ is a group in which $(a b)^{i}=a^{i} b^{i}$ for three consecutive integers $i$, prove that $G$ is abelian.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_2_3 {G : Type*} [Group G]
  {P : ℕ → Prop} {hP : P = λ i => ∀ a b : G, (a*b)^i = a^i * b^i}
  (hP1 : ∃ n : ℕ, P n ∧ P (n+1) ∧ P (n+2)) : 
  ∀ a b : G, a*b = b*a
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement quantifies over "three consecutive integers", i.e. ∃ i ∈ ℤ such that the identity holds for i, i+1, i+2. The Lean signature instead uses ∃ n : ℕ, restricting the witness to a non-negative starting point. Every ℕ-witness is also a ℤ-witness, so the Lean hypothesis is a strict special case of the informal one; therefore the informal theorem implies the Lean theorem, but not conversely (the Lean version says nothing about cases where the three consecutive integers are e.g. -2, -1, 0). The conclusion "G is abelian" matches, and the typeclass [Group G] with universal quantification over a, b is fine. The mismatch is solely in the type of the index i (ℕ vs ℤ), which makes the Lean statement logically weaker than the informal claim.

### proofnet-259  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Herstein_exercise_2_3_16_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> If a group $G$ has no proper subgroups, prove that $G$ is cyclic of order $p$, where $p$ is a prime number.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_3_16_corrected
    {G : Type*} [Group G] [Fintype G]
    (hG : ∀ H : Subgroup G, H = ⊤ ∨ H = ⊥)
    (h_nontrivial : ∃ g : G, g ≠ (1 : G)) :
    IsCyclic G ∧ ∃ p : ℕ, Nat.Prime p ∧ Fintype.card G = p
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement only assumes "G has no proper subgroups" (typically read as: the only subgroups are {e} and G) and, implicitly, that G is nontrivial; finiteness is part of the conclusion (order p prime), not a hypothesis. The Lean signature, however, adds `[Fintype G]` as an ambient hypothesis, so it asks the prover to derive cyclicity and prime-order while already given finiteness — this is logically weaker than the informal task, which requires deducing finiteness from the subgroup hypothesis. The other components (∀ H, H = ⊤ ∨ H = ⊥; the existence of a nonidentity element; IsCyclic G ∧ ∃ p prime, |G| = p) faithfully capture the rest of the claim. Because the extra `Fintype G` hypothesis strictly reduces what must be proved, the Lean version is implied by the informal claim but does not imply it.

### proofnet-268

**Informal statement**

> If $\sigma, \tau$ are two permutations that disturb no common element and $\sigma \tau = e$, prove that $\sigma = \tau = e$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_3_2_21 {α : Type*} [Fintype α] {σ τ: Equiv.Perm α}
  (h1 : ∀ a : α, σ a = a ↔ τ a ≠ a) (h2 : τ ∘ σ = id) :
  σ = 1 ∧ τ = 1
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal hypothesis "σ and τ disturb no common element" means ∀a, ¬(σ a ≠ a ∧ τ a ≠ a) — i.e., no element is moved by both. The Lean hypothesis `σ a = a ↔ τ a ≠ a` is strictly stronger: it requires that every element is moved by exactly one of σ, τ (so they cannot both fix a common element). This rules out, for example, the situation where σ and τ jointly fix some element, which the informal statement explicitly allows. Because the Lean theorem assumes a stronger premise to reach the same conclusion, it covers fewer cases than the informal claim and is therefore logically weaker. (The conclusion σ = 1 ∧ τ = 1 and the relation στ = e are encoded faithfully.)

### proofnet-285  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_6_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> If $p=2^{n}+1$ is a Fermat prime, show that 3 is a primitive root modulo $p$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_6_corrected {p n : ℕ} (hp : p.Prime) (hpn : p = 2 ^ n + 1)
    (hpep : (3 : ZMod p) ^ ((p - 1) / 2) = (-1 : ZMod p)) :
    IsPrimitiveRoot (3 : ZMod p) (p - 1)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature carries an extra hypothesis `hpep : (3 : ZMod p) ^ ((p - 1) / 2) = -1`, which is not present in the informal statement. For a Fermat prime p = 2^n + 1, this condition is in fact essentially equivalent to "3 is a primitive root mod p" (since the multiplicative order of any element divides p−1 = 2^n, so non-primitivity means the order divides 2^(n−1), which forces 3^((p−1)/2) = 1, not −1). Thus the Lean theorem assumes (almost) what should be proved: it states only the conditional implication, while the informal task is to establish this fact unconditionally for every Fermat prime. The informal claim implies the Lean claim, but not vice versa, so the Lean version is logically weaker.

### proofnet-308  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_28_4_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> A space $X$ is said to be countably compact if every countable open covering of $X$ contains a finite subcollection that covers $X$. Show that for a $T_1$ space $X$, countable compactness is equivalent to limit point compactness.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_28_4_corrected {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X]
    (hT1 : T1Space X) : countably_compact X → limit_point_compact_strong X
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to show that countable compactness is *equivalent* to limit point compactness for a T1 space, i.e., a biconditional. The Lean signature only states one direction: `countably_compact X → limit_point_compact_strong X`. The reverse implication (limit point compactness implies countable compactness in a T1 space) is omitted. Hence the Lean statement is implied by the informal one but does not imply it, making it strictly weaker.

### proofnet-341  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_2_29_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that every open set in $\mathbb{R}$ is the union of an at most countable collection of disjoint segments.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_2_29_corrected
    (U : Set ℝ) (hU : IsOpen U) (hConn : IsConnected U) :
    ∃ (f : ℕ → Set ℝ),
      (∀ n, ∃ a b : EReal, f n = {x : ℝ | a < (x : EReal) ∧ (x : EReal) < b}) ∧
      (∀ n, f n ⊆ U) ∧
      (∀ n m, n ≠ m → f n ∩ f m = ∅) ∧
      U = ⋃ n, f n
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The Lean statement adds the hypothesis `IsConnected U`, which is not present in the informal statement. The informal claim is about *every* open set in ℝ, while the Lean version restricts attention to connected open sets — which are themselves just single open intervals, making the conclusion almost trivial. This is a strict special case of the informal claim, so the Lean theorem is logically weaker. (The ℕ-indexing with allowed empty repeats is a fine way to encode "at most countable", but the spurious connectedness hypothesis is the decisive issue.)

### proofnet-343  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_3_5_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> For any two real sequences $\left\{a_{n}\right\},\left\{b_{n}\right\}$, prove that $\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty}\left(a_{n}+b_{n}\right) \leq \limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} a_{n}+\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} b_{n},$ provided the sum on the right is not of the form $\infty-\infty$.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_3_5_corrected
    (a b : ℕ → EReal)
    (_hareal : ∀ n, a n ≠ ⊤ ∧ a n ≠ ⊥)
    (_hbreal : ∀ n, b n ≠ ⊤ ∧ b n ≠ ⊥)
    (_ha : limsup a ≠ ⊤)
    (hb : limsup b ≠ ⊥)
    (havoid : limsup a ≠ ⊥ ∨ limsup b ≠ ⊤) :
    limsup (λ n => a n + b n) ≤ limsup a + limsup b
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim's only side condition is that limsup a + limsup b is not of the form ∞−∞, i.e., the pair (limsup a, limsup b) is not (+∞,−∞) or (−∞,+∞). The Lean signature instead imposes the stronger hypotheses limsup a ≠ ⊤ and limsup b ≠ ⊥, plus a disjunction ruling out (−∞,+∞). This excludes legitimate cases such as limsup a = +∞ with limsup b = +∞ (sum = +∞, no ∞−∞ issue), or limsup a finite with limsup b = −∞, which the informal statement covers. So the Lean theorem is logically weaker: it is a special case implied by the informal claim but does not imply it.

### proofnet-356  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_5_3_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $g$ is a real function on $R^{1}$, with bounded derivative (say $\left|g^{\prime}\right| \leq M$ ). Fix $\varepsilon>0$, and define $f(x)=x+\varepsilon g(x)$. Prove that $f$ is one-to-one if $\varepsilon$ is small enough.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_5_3_corrected
  {g : ℝ → ℝ} (hgDiff : Differentiable ℝ g)
  (hg' : ∃ M : ℝ, ∀ x : ℝ, |deriv g x| ≤ M) :
  ∃ N, ∀ ε > 0, ε < N → Function.Injective (λ x : ℝ => x + ε * g x)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The hypotheses (g differentiable on ℝ, |g'| bounded by some M) and the structure "for ε small enough, f is injective" are encoded with the right shape. However, the existential `∃ N, ∀ ε > 0, ε < N → Injective …` does not require N to be positive. Taking N = 0 (or any N ≤ 0) makes the implication `ε > 0 ∧ ε < N` vacuously false, so the Lean statement is trivially true regardless of g. This makes the formalization strictly weaker than the informal claim, which asserts the existence of a genuine positive threshold below which f is injective.

### proofnet-360  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_1_13b_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose that $f$ is holomorphic in an open set $\Omega$. Prove that if $\text{Im}(f)$ is constant, then $f$ is constant.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Shakarchi_exercise_1_13b_corrected {f : ℂ → ℂ} {Ω : Set ℂ}
    (hΩ : IsOpen Ω) (hConn : IsPreconnected Ω)
    (hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f Ω)
    (hc : ∃ c : ℝ, ∀ z ∈ Ω, (f z).im = c) :
    ∀ a b : Ω, f a = f b
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The conclusion `∀ a b : Ω, f a = f b` faithfully encodes "f is constant on Ω", and the hypothesis `(f z).im = c` correctly captures "Im(f) is constant on Ω". However, the Lean version adds an extra hypothesis `IsPreconnected Ω` that is not present in the informal statement (the informal only says "open set Ω", not "region"). The author's `_corrected` suffix even signals this was added. Strictly speaking, this makes the Lean theorem a special case of the literal informal claim (which is actually false without connectedness, but the literal text does not include it), so the Lean statement is logically weaker — it is implied by the informal claim but does not imply the unrestricted version.

### proofnet-367  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_5_1_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $f$ is holomorphic in the unit disc, bounded and not identically zero, and $z_{1}, z_{2}, \ldots, z_{n}, \ldots$ are its zeros $\left(\left|z_{k}\right|<1\right)$, then $\sum_{n}\left(1-\left|z_{n}\right|\right)<\infty$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_5_1_corrected
    (f : ℂ → ℂ) (_hf : DifferentiableOn ℂ f (ball 0 1))
    (_hb : Bornology.IsBounded (Set.range f)) (_h0 : f ≠ 0) (zeros : ℕ → ℂ)
    (_hz : Set.range zeros = {z | f z = 0 ∧ z ∈ (ball (0 : ℂ) 1)})
    (hsumm : Summable fun n : ℕ => (1 - ‖zeros n‖)) :
    ∃ (l : ℝ), Tendsto (fun n => ∑ i ∈ range n, (1 - ‖zeros i‖)) atTop (𝓝 l)
```

**Verdict:** `weaker`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asks to prove that the sum ∑(1 − |z_n|) converges (i.e., that the sequence is summable). The Lean signature instead takes `hsumm : Summable (fun n => 1 − ‖zeros n‖)` as a hypothesis and merely concludes that the partial sums tend to some limit — which is an immediate triviality from `Summable`. Thus the Lean theorem assumes the very fact that was to be proved, and its actual content is vacuous compared to the informal claim. The substantive conclusion (summability of (1 − |z_n|) for zeros of a bounded holomorphic function on the disc) is missing, so the Lean version is strictly weaker than the informal statement.

## stronger (5)

### proofnet-24  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Axler_exercise_6_16_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $U$ is a subspace of $V$. Prove that $U^{\perp}=\{0\}$ if and only if $U=V$

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Axler_exercise_6_16_corrected {K V : Type*} [RCLike K] [NormedAddCommGroup V]
  [InnerProductSpace K V] [CompleteSpace V] {U : Submodule K V}
  (hU : IsClosed (U : Set V)) :
  U.orthogonal = ⊥ ↔ U = ⊤
```

**Verdict:** `stronger`

**Reasoning**

Axler's exercise 6.16 sits in a finite-dimensional inner product space context, where every subspace is automatically closed and V is automatically complete. The Lean signature drops finite-dimensionality and instead carries the explicit hypotheses `CompleteSpace V` and `IsClosed (U : Set V)`, which are exactly what is needed to make the equivalence true in the broader Hilbert-space setting (without closedness the forward direction fails for dense proper subspaces). The conclusion `U.orthogonal = ⊥ ↔ U = ⊤` matches `U⊥ = {0} ↔ U = V` precisely, and `RCLike K` correctly captures Axler's ℝ or ℂ scalars. Specializing the Lean theorem to finite dimensions recovers the informal claim (since the added hypotheses become automatic), but the informal finite-dim statement does not by itself yield the more general Hilbert-space version; hence the Lean statement is logically stronger, as the `_corrected` suffix also hints.

### proofnet-119  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_23_4_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $X$ is an infinite set, it is connected in the finite complement topology.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Munkres_exercise_23_4_corrected {X : Type*}
    (s : Set (CofiniteTopology X)) :
    s.Infinite → IsConnected s
```

**Verdict:** `stronger`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts only that the whole space X (when infinite) is connected in the cofinite topology. The Lean signature instead asserts that EVERY infinite subset s of CofiniteTopology X is connected (with the subspace topology). This is a strictly stronger claim: applying it to s = Set.univ (under the assumption X is infinite) recovers the informal statement, but the Lean version additionally claims connectedness of every infinite subset. Note also that the Lean version drops the explicit hypothesis "X is infinite" and replaces it with "s is infinite" (which is a different, though related, hypothesis). Hence the Lean theorem implies the informal one but says more.

### proofnet-155  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_2_24_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $X$ be a metric space in which every infinite subset has a limit point. Prove that $X$ is separable.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Rudin_exercise_2_24_corrected
    {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
    (hTB : TotallyBounded (Set.univ : Set X)) :
    SeparableSpace X
```

**Verdict:** `stronger`

**Reasoning**

The informal hypothesis is "every infinite subset of X has a limit point" (limit point compactness / Bolzano–Weierstrass property). The Lean signature instead assumes `TotallyBounded (Set.univ : Set X)`. These are not the same hypothesis. In a metric space, limit point compactness implies sequential compactness, hence compactness, hence total boundedness; so the informal hypothesis implies the Lean one. The converse fails: e.g. the open interval (0,1) is totally bounded but the infinite subset {1/n} has no limit point in (0,1). Thus the Lean statement uses a strictly weaker hypothesis to derive the same conclusion (separability), so it logically implies the informal claim but is not implied by it.

### proofnet-333

**Informal statement**

> Let $E$ be a nonempty subset of an ordered set; suppose $\alpha$ is a lower bound of $E$ and $\beta$ is an upper bound of $E$. Prove that $\alpha \leq \beta$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_1_4
(α : Type*) [PartialOrder α]
(s : Set α)
(x y : α)
(h₀ : Set.Nonempty s)
(h₁ : x ∈ lowerBounds s)
(h₂ : y ∈ upperBounds s)
: x ≤ y
```

**Verdict:** `stronger`

**Reasoning**

In Rudin, an "ordered set" means a linearly (totally) ordered set, whereas the Lean signature only assumes `[PartialOrder α]`. Since every linear order is a partial order, the Lean theorem implies the informal claim, but it actually proves more: it establishes the same conclusion under the weaker hypothesis of a partial order. The hypotheses about `lowerBounds`, `upperBounds`, and nonemptiness of `s` correctly mirror the informal statement, and the conclusion `x ≤ y` matches. The only deviation is the strengthening from linear order to partial order, making the Lean version logically stronger than what was asked.

### proofnet-338

**Informal statement**

> If $A$ and $B$ are disjoint closed sets in some metric space $X$, prove that they are separated.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_2_19a {X : Type*} [MetricSpace X]
  (A B : Set X) (hA : IsClosed A) (hB : IsClosed B) (hAB : Disjoint A B) :
  SeparatedNhds A B
```

**Verdict:** `stronger`

**Reasoning**

Rudin's Definition 2.45 says two sets A and B are "separated" iff A ∩ closure(B) = ∅ and closure(A) ∩ B = ∅. Mathlib's `SeparatedNhds A B` asserts the existence of disjoint open neighborhoods U ⊇ A, V ⊇ B. In general topological spaces, `SeparatedNhds` strictly implies Rudin's separation (disjoint open neighborhoods force the closures to miss the other set), but the converse fails. So the Lean conclusion is stronger than what the informal statement asks — even though in a metric space (which is normal) disjoint closed sets do satisfy both, the Lean theorem asserts the stronger property `SeparatedNhds` rather than the weaker "separated" notion Rudin defines. Therefore the Lean statement is logically stronger than the informal one.

## different (12)

### proofnet-67

**Informal statement**

> Prove that if $f(x)$ and $g(x)$ are polynomials with rational coefficients whose product $f(x) g(x)$ has integer coefficients, then the product of any coefficient of $g(x)$ with any coefficient of $f(x)$ is an integer.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Dummit_Foote_exercise_9_3_2 {f g : Polynomial ℚ} (i j : ℕ)
  (hfg : ∀ _n : ℕ, ∃ a : ℤ, (f*g).coeff = a) :
  ∃ a : ℤ, f.coeff i * g.coeff j = a
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The hypothesis `hfg : ∀ _n : ℕ, ∃ a : ℤ, (f*g).coeff = a` is malformed relative to the intended meaning. `(f*g).coeff` has type `ℕ → ℚ`, not `ℚ`, so equating it to `a : ℤ` does not express "the n-th coefficient of f*g is an integer." The variable `_n` is unused, and the intended `(f*g).coeff n = a` is missing the argument `n`. Either the statement fails to typecheck, or — if some coercion makes it parse — it forces the entire coefficient function to be a constant integer, which (since polynomial coefficients vanish eventually) is essentially the assumption that f*g = 0, vastly stronger than "f*g has integer coefficients." Thus the Lean signature does not faithfully encode the informal hypothesis.

### proofnet-77

**Informal statement**

> If $G$ is a nonabelian group of order 6, prove that $G \simeq S_3$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_5_37 (G : Type*) [Group G] [Fintype G]
  (hG : card G = 6) (hG' : IsEmpty (CommGroup G)) :
  Nonempty (G ≃* Equiv.Perm (Fin 3))
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal hypothesis "G is nonabelian" should be encoded as something like `¬ ∀ a b : G, a * b = b * a`. Instead, the Lean signature uses `IsEmpty (CommGroup G)`, which asserts that the type `CommGroup G` (the type of *all* commutative group structures on the carrier type G) is empty. This is not the same as saying the given `Group G` instance is noncommutative: any 6-element type admits *some* commutative group structure (transfer the structure of ℤ/6ℤ across a bijection G ≃ Fin 6), so `IsEmpty (CommGroup G)` is in fact always false for |G| = 6. Hence the Lean hypothesis is vacuous and the theorem becomes trivial, capturing none of the actual mathematical content of "the given group G is nonabelian ⟹ G ≃ S₃". This is a genuine semantic mismatch, not a logically equivalent reformulation.

### proofnet-80

**Informal statement**

> Prove that any two nonabelian groups of order 21 are isomorphic.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Herstein_exercise_2_8_12 {G H : Type*} [Fintype G] [Fintype H]
  [Group G] [Group H] (hG : card G = 21) (_hH : card H = 21)
  (hG1 : IsEmpty (CommGroup G)) (_hH1 : IsEmpty (CommGroup H)) :
  Nonempty (G ≃* H)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature encodes "G is nonabelian" as `IsEmpty (CommGroup G)`, which asserts that the type `G` admits no commutative group structure at all — not that the given `Group G` instance is noncommutative. For any type `G` of cardinality 21, one can transport the abelian group structure of `ℤ/21` onto `G`, so `CommGroup G` is inhabited; hence `IsEmpty (CommGroup G)` is false for every type of size 21, and the hypothesis is vacuous. The intended hypothesis should be something like `¬ ∀ a b : G, a * b = b * a` referring to the given group structure. Because the Lean theorem is vacuously true regardless of the actual mathematical content, it does not capture the informal claim about nonabelian groups of order 21, and it is not logically equivalent to it.

### proofnet-141  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Pugh_exercise_3_63b_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\sum 1/k(\log(k))^p$ diverges when $p \leq 1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_3_63b_corrected (p : ℝ) (f : ℕ → ℝ) (_hp : p ≤ 1)
    (h : f = fun k : ℕ => 1 / (k * (Real.log k) ^ p)) :
    Tendsto f atTop (𝓝 0)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts the divergence of the series ∑ 1/(k(log k)^p) for p ≤ 1. The Lean signature instead asserts that the sequence f(k) = 1/(k(log k)^p) tends to 0 at infinity. Convergence of the terms to 0 is a completely different claim from divergence of the series — it is neither implied by nor implies divergence of the series (in fact, divergence of this series is consistent with the terms going to 0, which is what makes the example interesting). So the Lean statement is not comparable to the informal one.

### proofnet-168  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Rudin_exercise_4_8b_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Let $E$ be a bounded set in $R^{1}$. Prove that there exists a real function $f$ such that $f$ is uniformly continuous and is not bounded on $E$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Rudin_exercise_4_8b_corrected
  (E : Set ℝ) (h : Bornology.IsBounded E) :
  ∀ f : ℝ → ℝ, UniformContinuousOn f E → Bornology.IsBounded (Set.image f E)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement (as written) claims: for any bounded E ⊆ ℝ, there EXISTS a uniformly continuous function f that is UNBOUNDED on E. The Lean signature states the opposite/converse: for every uniformly continuous f on a bounded E, the image f(E) is BOUNDED. These are not logically related in the same direction — the Lean theorem is essentially the negation of the existential claim in the informal statement (and is actually the true Rudin 4.8(a), as suggested by the "_corrected" suffix). The Lean version neither implies nor is implied by the informal claim; it asserts a different (in fact contradictory) proposition. Hence the formalization is not faithful but "different."

### proofnet-202

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $T \in \mathcal{L}(V)$. Prove that if $U_{1}, \ldots, U_{m}$ are subspaces of $V$ invariant under $T$, then $U_{1}+\cdots+U_{m}$ is invariant under $T$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Axler_exercise_5_1 {F V : Type*} [AddCommGroup V] [Field F]
  [Module F V] {L : V →ₗ[F] V} {n : ℕ} (U : Fin n → Submodule F V)
  (hU : ∀ i : Fin n, Submodule.map L (U i) = U i) :
  Submodule.map L (∑ i : Fin n, U i : Submodule F V) =
  (∑ i : Fin n, U i : Submodule F V)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement says each U_i is T-invariant, which standardly means T(U_i) ⊆ U_i, and the conclusion is T(U_1+⋯+U_m) ⊆ U_1+⋯+U_m. The Lean signature instead encodes both the hypothesis and the conclusion using `Submodule.map L (U i) = U i`, i.e. T(U_i) = U_i (set-theoretic equality, equivalent to T being surjective onto each U_i when restricted). This is a strictly stronger notion than invariance in both places. So the Lean theorem has a stronger hypothesis (does not follow from the informal claim) and a stronger conclusion (the informal proof would not establish equality). The two theorems are not logically comparable: the informal one is about ⊆-invariance while the Lean one is about exact preservation, so neither implies the other.

### proofnet-279  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_1_30_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{3}+\cdots+\frac{1}{n}$ is not an integer.

**Lean signature**

```lean
theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_1_30_corrected (n : ℕ) (hn : 0 < n) :
  ¬ ∃ a : ℤ, ∑ _ : Fin n, (1 : ℚ) / (n+2) = a
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal claim is that the harmonic-like sum 1/2 + 1/3 + ... + 1/n is not an integer. The Lean signature instead sums the constant 1/(n+2) over a Fin n index (the bound variable is anonymous `_`), so the expression equals n · 1/(n+2) = n/(n+2), which has nothing to do with the harmonic sum. Moreover, the summand does not vary with the index, the denominators 2, 3, ..., n never appear, and the statement is parametrized incorrectly (using n+2 as a fixed denominator). Hence the Lean theorem asserts a different mathematical claim entirely (and one that, e.g., for n=1 reduces to "1/3 is not an integer", unrelated to the original).

### proofnet-284

**Informal statement**

> Consider a prime $p$ of the form $4 t+1$. Show that $a$ is a primitive root modulo $p$ iff $-a$ is a primitive root modulo $p$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_4_4 {p t: ℕ} (hp0 : p.Prime) (hp1 : p = 4*t + 1)
  (a : ZMod p) :
  IsPrimitiveRoot a p ↔ IsPrimitiveRoot (-a) p
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

In Mathlib, `IsPrimitiveRoot a n` means `a` is a primitive n-th root of unity: `a^n = 1` and no smaller positive power equals 1. A primitive root modulo a prime `p` should have multiplicative order `p - 1 = φ(p)`, not `p`. So the correct encoding would be `IsPrimitiveRoot a (p - 1)`. As written, `IsPrimitiveRoot a p` would require `a^p = 1` in `ZMod p`, which by Fermat's little theorem gives `a^p = a`, forcing `a = 1` (and even then 1 is not a primitive p-th root unless p = 1). Thus the Lean iff is essentially vacuous/wrong rather than expressing the intended claim about primitive roots mod p. This is a genuine semantic mismatch.

### proofnet-312  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Munkres_exercise_31_1_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Show that if $X$ is regular, every pair of points of $X$ have neighborhoods whose closures are disjoint.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Munkres_exercise_31_1_corrected {X : Type*} [TopologicalSpace X] [T25Space X]
    (x y : X) (hxy : x ≠ y) :
    ∃ U V : Set X, IsOpen U ∧ IsOpen V ∧ x ∈ U ∧ y ∈ V ∧
      closure U ∩ closure V = (∅ : Set X)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement's hypothesis is that X is regular (in Munkres' sense, T1 + regular, i.e. T3), and the conclusion is that any two distinct points have neighborhoods with disjoint closures (the T2.5 property). The Lean signature replaces the hypothesis "regular" with `[T25Space X]`, which already asserts exactly the conclusion of the theorem. So the Lean statement is essentially "T2.5 implies T2.5" — a near-tautology obtainable by unfolding the definition — rather than the substantive content "regular implies T2.5". This is a genuine change in mathematical meaning: the Lean theorem neither requires regularity nor establishes the informal implication from regularity. Hence the hypothesis has been swapped for something incomparable in spirit, making the formalization not a faithful encoding.

### proofnet-324

**Informal statement**

> Prove that $\sum 1/k(\log(k))^p$ converges when $p > 1$.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_3_63a (p : ℝ) (f : ℕ → ℝ) (hp : p > 1)
  (h : f = λ (k : ℕ) => (1 : ℝ) / (k * (log k) ^ p)) :
  ∃ l, Tendsto f atTop (𝓝 l)
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal statement asserts convergence of the series ∑ 1/(k (log k)^p) for p > 1. The Lean statement instead asserts that the sequence f(k) = 1/(k (log k)^p) tends to some limit l (i.e., that the terms converge), not that the partial sums converge. These are very different claims: the sequence of terms tends to 0 trivially (even for p ≤ 1, where the series diverges), so the Lean statement does not capture series convergence. There is no use of `Summable`, `HasSum`, or partial sums. This is a different (and much weaker) mathematical claim.

### proofnet-325

**Informal statement**

> A continuous, strictly increasing function $\mu \colon (0, \infty) \rightarrow (0, \infty)$ is a modulus of continuity if $\mu(s) \rightarrow 0$ as $s \rightarrow 0$. A function $f \colon [a, b] \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ has modulus of continuity $\mu$ if $|f(s) - f(t)| \leq \mu(|s - t|)$ for all $s, t \in [a, b]$. Prove that a function is uniformly continuous if and only if it has a modulus of continuity.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Pugh_exercise_4_15a (a b : ℝ) (hab : a ≤ b) (f : Icc a b → ℝ) :
  UniformContinuous f ↔ ∃ (μ : ℝ → ℝ), ∀ (x : ℝ), (x > 0 → μ x > 0) ∧
  StrictMonoOn μ (Ioi 0) ∧ Tendsto μ (𝓝 0) (𝓝 0) ∧
  (∀ s t : Icc a b, |f s - f t| ≤ μ (|s - t|))
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal definition of "modulus of continuity" requires μ to be continuous on (0,∞), but the Lean signature only encodes strict monotonicity on (0,∞), positivity, and Tendsto at 0 — it omits continuity on (0,∞) entirely (strict monotonicity does not imply continuity). This makes the existential RHS strictly weaker than the informal one. As a result, the forward direction (UC → ∃μ) of Lean is weaker than the informal one, while the backward direction (∃μ → UC) of Lean is stronger (concludes UC from a weaker hypothesis). Since one direction is weaker and the other is stronger, the overall iff in Lean is not comparable to the informal iff.

### proofnet-364  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Shakarchi_exercise_2_13_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Suppose $f$ is an analytic function defined everywhere in $\mathbb{C}$ and such that for each $z_0 \in \mathbb{C}$ at least one coefficient in the expansion $f(z) = \sum_{n=0}^\infty c_n(z - z_0)^n$ is equal to 0. Prove that $f$ is a polynomial.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Shakarchi_exercise_2_13_corrected
    {f : ℂ → ℂ}
    (hf : ∃ (c : ℕ → ℂ) (k : ℕ),
        ∀ z : ℂ,
          Tendsto (fun n => ∑ i ∈ range (min n k.succ), c i * z ^ i)
            atTop (𝓝 (f z))) :
    ∃ (c : ℕ → ℂ) (k : ℕ), f = λ z => ∑ i ∈ range k.succ, c i * z ^ i
```

**Verdict:** `different`

**Reasoning**

The informal hypothesis is: f is entire and for every center z₀ ∈ ℂ, at least one Taylor coefficient cₙ(z₀) of f at z₀ vanishes. The Lean hypothesis is entirely different: it asserts the existence of a single sequence c : ℕ → ℂ and a single natural number k such that for all z, the partial sums ∑_{i < min(n, k+1)} cᵢ zⁱ converge to f(z). Since min(n, k+1) is bounded by k+1, this stabilizes to ∑_{i < k+1} cᵢ zⁱ, so the hypothesis already says f is a polynomial of degree ≤ k expanded at the origin. The Lean theorem is therefore trivial (hypothesis already gives the conclusion) and does not capture the "vanishing-coefficient at every z₀" condition at all. This is a fundamental mismatch in hypotheses, not a logical strengthening or weakening of the original claim.

## unclear (1)

### proofnet-104  &nbsp;·&nbsp; `Ireland_Rosen_exercise_18_4_corrected`

**Informal statement**

> Show that 1729 is the smallest positive integer expressible as the sum of two different integral cubes in two ways.

**Lean signature**

```lean

theorem Ireland_Rosen_exercise_18_4_corrected :
    1729 = sInf correctedSet
```

**Verdict:** `unclear`

**Reasoning**

The Lean signature asserts `1729 = sInf correctedSet`, but the definition of `correctedSet` is not provided. Whether the theorem faithfully formalizes "1729 is the smallest positive integer expressible as the sum of two different integral cubes in two ways" depends entirely on what `correctedSet` contains: it must be the set of positive integers n such that there exist (at least) two essentially distinct representations n = a³ + b³ with a ≠ b (and the two representations being different as unordered pairs, with appropriate handling of integral vs natural cubes). Without seeing this definition, I cannot verify whether the encoding correctly captures "two different integral cubes" (a ≠ b within a representation) and "two ways" (two distinct unordered pairs), nor whether `sInf` over the empty case or sign conventions are handled. Hence the faithfulness is genuinely undetermined.
